{u'full_article': u'<p>OTTAWA \u2014 A half dozen Iroquois First Nations\xa0are\xa0calling the proposed Energy East pipeline a "dangerous project,"\xa0less than 24 hours after the federal government announced additional reviews to bolster public confidence in the pipeline assessment\xa0process \u2014 including more consultations with indigenous communities.</p><p>It was just another sign of the acrimonious debate ahead as the Liberal government attempts to find consensus on\xa0building\xa0"sustainable" oil pipelines to an ocean port in order to export land-locked crude oil.</p><p>That debate consumed the House of Commons on Thursday, as the Conservative opposition presented a\xa0motion\xa0effectively calling for a vote of confidence and support in TransCanada\'s proposed $15.7-billion pipeline\xa0to carry Alberta and Saskatchewan bitumen to New Brunswick refineries and ports.</p><p>On Wednesday, Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr and Environment Minister Catherine McKenna announced that current project assessments, including Energy East and the Trans Mountain line in B.C., would have to be assessed\xa0on\xa0the upstream greenhouse gases emitted in the production of the oil they propose to carry. The Liberal government also said it would appoint a ministerial representative to consult indigenous communities, while extending the time frame for a decision on each of\xa0the two major pipelines.</p><p>"We need to take into account the views and concerns of Canadians, respect\xa0the rights and interests of indigenous peoples and support our natural resources sector," McKenna said in introducing the plan.</p><p>Enter the Iroquois Caucus.</p><p>The group, representing\xa0Ontario and western Quebec leaders from Akwesasne, Kahnawake, Kanesatake, Oneida Nation of the Thames, Six Nations of the Grand River, Mohawks of the Bay of Quinte and Wahta Mohawks, said Thursday they expect to be consulted by the Crown while expressing their unanimous opposition to\xa0Energy East.</p><p>"Notwithstanding the economic benefits, we have serious concerns with respect to the devastating effects this project can have on our lands and waters, our way of life and our cultures," said the news release.</p><p>Conservatives, stung by Liberal accusations they failed to get a pipeline to tidewater in spite of \u2014 or perhaps due to \u2014 their over-exuberant support of the oil and gas sector while in government, responded to the new project review plan by attempting to flush out Liberal\xa0intentions on pipelines.</p><p>A voteable Conservative motion was debated in the Commons throughout the day Thursday, with the official Opposition demanding\xa0Prime Minister Justin Trudeau\xa0publicly back\xa0the Energy East proposal.</p><p>Former\xa0Tory cabinet minister Erin O\'Toole said Trudeau needs to show leadership rather than acceding to\xa0the\xa0regional war of words between lower levels of government.</p><p>"He\'s not a traffic cop between mayors,"\xa0O\'Toole decade.</p><p>New Democrats also opposed the motion.</p><p>NDP critic Nathan Cullen, during the debate,\xa0said the Conservatives are pre-judging a\xa0project whose 27-month assessment by the National Energy Board hasn\'t even begun.\xa0</p><p>"They want to cast judgment on Energy East before it\'s even hit the hearing stage," said Cullen.</p><p>There\'s a lot of that going around.</p><p>\u2014 {u'full_article': u'<p>OTTAWA \u2014 A half dozen Iroquois First Nations are calling the proposed Energy East pipeline a "dangerous project," less than 24 hours after the federal government announced additional reviews to bolster public confidence in the pipeline assessment process \u2014 including more consultations with indigenous communities.</p><p>It was just another sign of the acrimonious debate ahead as the Liberal government attempts to find consensus on building "sustainable" oil pipelines to an ocean port in order to export land-locked crude oil.</p><p>That debate consumed the House of Commons on Thursday, as the Conservative opposition presented a motion effectively calling for a vote of confidence and support in TransCanada\'s proposed $15.7-billion pipeline to carry Alberta and Saskatchewan bitumen to New Brunswick refineries and ports.</p><p>On Wednesday, Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr and Environment Minister Catherine McKenna announced that current project assessments, including Energy East and the Trans Mountain line in B.C., would have to be assessed on the upstream greenhouse gases emitted in the production of the oil they propose to carry. The Liberal government also said it would appoint a ministerial representative to consult indigenous communities, while extending the time frame for a decision on each of the two major pipelines.</p><p>"We need to take into account the views and concerns of Canadians, respect the rights and interests of indigenous peoples and support our natural resources sector," McKenna said in introducing the plan.</p><p>Enter the Iroquois Caucus.</p><p>The group, representing Ontario and western Quebec leaders from Akwesasne, Kahnawake, Kanesatake, Oneida Nation of the Thames, Six Nations of the Grand River, Mohawks of the Bay of Quinte and Wahta Mohawks, said Thursday they expect to be consulted by the Crown while expressing their unanimous opposition to Energy East.</p><p>"Notwithstanding the economic benefits, we have serious concerns with respect to the devastating effects this project can have on our lands and waters, our way of life and our cultures," said the news release.</p><p>Conservatives, stung by Liberal accusations they failed to get a pipeline to tidewater in spite of \u2014 or perhaps due to \u2014 their over-exuberant support of the oil and gas sector while in government, responded to the new project review plan by attempting to flush out Liberal intentions on pipelines.</p><p>A voteable Conservative motion was debated in the Commons throughout the day Thursday, with the official Opposition demanding Prime Minister Justin Trudeau publicly back the Energy East proposal.</p><p>Former Tory cabinet minister Erin O\'Toole said Trudeau needs to show leadership rather than acceding to the regional war of words between lower levels of government.</p><p>"He\'s not a traffic cop between mayors," O\'Toole said during the day-long debate.</p><p>Trudeau told the House the Liberal majority would vote against the Conservative motion, which he said simply reflected the "failed policies" of the last decade.</p><p>New Democrats also opposed the motion.</p><p>NDP critic Nathan Cullen, during the debate, said the Conservatives are pre-judging a project whose 27-month assessment by the National Energy Board hasn\'t even begun.</p><p>"They want to cast judgment on Energy East before it\'s even hit the hearing stage," said Cullen.</p><p>There\'s a lot of that going around.</p><p>\u2014 Follow @BCheadle on Twitter</p>', {u'full_article': u'<p>The Trudeau government\u2019s move to add new hurdles to the pipeline approval process will go down as either the brilliant counterintuitive thrust that creates social licence for languishing energy infrastructure projects or the fatal blow that condemns them to the scrap heap.</p><p>Such is the new government\u2019s desire to please everyone that it\u2019s impossible to tell whether Prime Minister Justin Trudeau is more committed to improving market access for Alberta\u2019s oil or to reducing Canada\u2019s carbon emissions. He\u2019s given both pipeline proponents and opponents hope to cling to. Inevitably, one side or the other faces major disappointment.</p><p class="videoInfo videoInfo--border videoInfo-headline">Justin Trudeau aims to bring politicians together over pipelines (CP Video)</p><p>Take Wednesday\u2019s announcement that, in addition to expanding consultations with affected First Nations, the government will impose a climate test on the Trans Mountain and Energy East pipelines, assessing the upstream greenhouse-gas emissions generated by each project. Depending on the assumptions used, those emissions will either be quite minimal or colossal.</p><p>Differing assumptions left the U.S. State Department and Environmental Protection Agency at odds over the impact TransCanada\u2019s Keystone XL pipeline would have on greenhouse-gas emissions. It\u2019s reasonable to expect a similar clash (behind closed doors, at least) between Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr and Environment Minister Catherine McKenna, whose respective roles position them as antagonists in any debate over oil-sands development.</p><p>The issue centres on whether the construction of Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain expansion or TransCanada\u2019s Energy East would lead to an increase in oil-sands production that would not otherwise occur. In the case of Keystone, the State Department concluded that \u201cthe approval or denial of any one crude oil transport project \u2026 is unlikely to significantly impact the rate of expansion in the oil sands or the continued demand for heavy crude oil in the United States.\u201d</p><p>The EPA took an opposing view, arguing that declining oil prices rendered alternative transportation methods, such as oil-by-rail, uneconomical for oil-sands producers. Hence, blocking Keystone would landlock the 830,000 daily barrels of oil it sought to transport. Approving Keystone, the EPA countered, would \u201cchange the economics of oil-sands development and result in increased oil-sands production and the accompanying greenhouse gas emissions.\u201d</p><p>Similar reasoning was used by the Pembina Institute in a 2014 study that estimated the upstream greenhouse-gas emissions from Energy East at more than 30 million tonnes, stemming from an increase in oil-sands production of up to 750,000 barrels per day. By comparison, current oil-sands production generates about 70 million tonnes of carbon dioxide equivalent. The Alberta government proposes to cap oil-sands emissions at 100 million tonnes.</p><p>\u201cThere is an important distinction between the transportation costs that existing producers are willing to pay and the conditions needed to encourage future production growth,\u201d the Pembina study said. \u201cSo, while rail can be a complement and a stop-gap, oil-sands producers want to see large-capacity pipelines built to give them the confidence to invest in new operations.\u201d</p><p>That point of view was contrasted in a discussion paper produced for the Ontario Energy Board by environmental consulting firm Navius Research, that the OEB called \u201cthe most comprehensive Canadian analysis to date on the relationship between pipeline projects and climate change.\u201d It found that rail would indeed make up for a lack of pipelines, estimating that Energy East would lead to an increase in oil-sands production of about only 90,000 barrels per day, or less than 10 per cent of the proposed pipeline\u2019s 1.1-million-barrel-per-day capacity.</p><p>In its projection of future Canadian energy supply, released Wednesday, the National Energy Board estimated that even in its \u201cpipeline constrained\u201d scenario, domestic oil production would rise to 5.6 million barrels a day by 2040. That\u2019s just 8 per cent less than the 6.1 million barrels per day the federal agency projects would be produced with new pipelines and higher prices.</p><p>What\u2019s more, current analyses of upstream greenhouse gases assume that the emissions intensity of oil-sands crude remains constant. But that intensity has declined dramatically in recent decades and, depending on your faith in science, can reasonably be expected to decline in the future. As the Navius report noted: \u201cProducers are constantly developing new and more efficient ways of extracting bitumen.\u201d</p><p>Still, not even a favourable upstream emissions assessment will placate environmental groups, more than 70 of which released an open letter Wednesday calling for Canada to \u201cturn away from carbon-enabling infrastructure\u201d such as pipelines and to \u201cleave carbon in the ground.\u201d</p><p>Sooner or later, Mr. Trudeau must resign himself to the fact that he can\u2019t please everyone.</p><p class="article__ontwitter">Follow Konrad Yakabuski on Twitter: @konradyakabuski</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/?service=rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/report-on-business/rob-commentary/eventually-trudeau-will-have-to-disappoint-someone-on-pipelines/article28437986/', u'author': u'KONRAD YAKABUSKI', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/report-on-business/rob-commentary/eventually-trudeau-will-have-to-disappoint-someone-on-pipelines/article28437986/?cmpid=rss1', u'scraped_on': 1454013440, u'published_date': u'Thu, 28 Jan 2016 15:21:52 -0500', u'title': u'Eventually, Trudeau will have to disappoint someone on pipelines', u'_id': ObjectId('56aa7c031d41c8665ddc1c62'), u'media_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>Pierre Cleroux of the Business Development Bank of Canada speaks to a Greater Kingston Chamber of Commerce groupat the Holiday Inn in Kingston on Thursday Jan. 21 2016.Ian MacAlpine / Whig-Standard</p><p>While our uber-cool prime minister lives the celebrity life\xa0and\xa0focuses\xa0his sunny gaze elsewhere\xa0(next stop: a State Dinner on March 10 at\xa0the White House!), the\xa0economic carnage in Alberta\xa0rages on.</p><p>Perhaps Justin Trudeau will\xa0notice\xa0when he\xa0pays Canada\u2019s humbled\xa0energy powerhouse\xa0a visit. He is scheduled to meet Premier Rachel Notley on Feb. 3. Meanwhile, the headlines are only\xa0getting worse. The latest cheery news from the western front?</p><p>Investment plans by small and midsize Alberta businesses (companies with up to 500 employees) are plunging, according to a new study\xa0by the Montreal-based Business Development Bank of Canada (BDC).\xa0</p><p>After polling\xa0500 smaller Alberta firms\xa0across a variety of sectors,\xa0the BDC found\xa0that business spending is expected to crater\xa0by 27 per cent this year, to $11 billion. With Alberta facing a second straight year of recession, only\xa0one in three firms expect revenues to rise.</p><p>The gloomy findings in Alberta contrast sharply with the picture nationwide.\xa0Overall, the BDC\xa0says business spending among the 4,000 small to midsize firms\xa0it surveyed across Canada\xa0is expected\xa0to stay\xa0flat this year, at\xa0about $111 billion.</p><p>In British Columbia, where a wave of foreign cash is driving real estate prices to the moon, spurring the\xa0province\u2019s economy,\xa0business spending\xa0among smaller firms is expected to spike\xa0by 14 per cent this year to roughly $33 billion.</p><p>Ontario firms project an increase of two per cent to $44 billion, the BDC says.\xa0Alberta is\xa0the only province showing an outright decline.</p><p>The struggles of\xa0small firms mirrors the ongoing carnage in Alberta\u2019s\xa0oilpatch.\xa0With crude\xa0prices plunging\xa070 per cent since June 2014, and oil pipelines blocked by the eco zealots and their political pals,\xa0Alberta\u2019s energy\xa0producers have taken an axe to their\xa0capital budgets.</p><p>Dozens of major projects have been cancelled and capital spending has been slashed by tens of billions of dollars.\xa0</p><p>\u201cDefinitely, the fact that there is less investment in the energy sector is having an impact,\u201d\xa0 says Pierre Cleroux, chief economist at the federally owned bank.</p><p>\u201cSmall and midsize firms are a big part of the value chain in the energy sector. So when you see less investment (in the oilpatch), you also see smaller companies that\xa0don\u2019t do as much business\xa0and aren\u2019t\xa0investing as much. That\u2019s a direct impact of lower investment in the oil sector.\u201d</p><p>Since capital spending in the energy sector\xa0once accounted for nearly a third of all business investment in Canada, the\xa0ripple effects of the cuts\xa0continues to spread.\xa0</p><p>Canada Mortgage and Housing Corp. now\xa0says\xa0house prices\xa0in oil-rich Alberta and Saskatchewan face\xa0a\xa0high risk of a correction.\xa0Meanwhile,\xa0new data from\xa0Statistics Canada\xa0shows\xa0that Alberta lost 19,600 jobs last year, making it\xa0the worst year\xa0for job losses\xa0since the early 1980s.</p><p>That\u2019s\xa0when another Trudeau \u2014 then\xa0prime minister Pierre Elliot Trudeau \u2014 cut\xa0Alberta\u2019s economy off at the knees by imposing\xa0the National Energy Program (NEP).\xa0</p><p>Now, Trudeau junior\xa0seems intent on inflicting NEP 2.0 on Alberta. Not by confiscating the province\u2019s rapidly depleting oil wealth, of course, but\xa0by failing to support the energy industry\xa0when it needs it most, while offering little but\xa0glib platitudes.</p><p>While former prime minister Stephen Harper moved quickly to bail out Ontario\u2019s struggling auto industry in the wake of the last recession, Trudeau has thus far offered nothing, while\xa0throwing up even more barriers for new pipeline projects.</p><p>\u201cEverywhere, projects are stalled (or being cancelled) not only by poor market conditions, but also by laborious regulatory procedures, political resistance, aboriginal claims, court rulings and hard-line environmentalists,\u201d notes\xa0Globe and\xa0Mail columnist Jeffrey Simpson, who is hardly known as a fan of Alberta\u2019s energy industry.</p><p>\u201cA lot of companies have already gone home or put projects on long-term hold, so difficult is the Canadian environment. The federal government is not making things any better with confusing statements about whether it wants development or doesn\u2019t, and on what terms.\u201d</p><p>Isn\u2019t\xa0that the truth.\xa0Hours\xa0after he\xa0told a crowd of Davos billionaires he wants Canada to be known more for its \u201cresourcefulness\u201d than its \u201cresources\u201d \u2014 a pointed jab at\xa0Harper \u2014 Trudeau seemed to offer support for TransCanada\u2019s proposed Energy East pipeline project.</p><p>But as usual, that didn\u2019t last long. On Wednesday, the Liberals rolled out\xa0new measures \u2014 including a climate assessment test and further\xa0consultation requirements for\xa0First Nations \u2014 that will inflict months of further\xa0delays on the absurdly lengthy review\xa0process that already exists for new pipelines.</p><p>Oddly,\xa0while Canada seems intent on destroying its own energy industry, the rest of the planet \u2014 including the U.S. \u2014\xa0can\u2019t pump enough oil\xa0or build new pipelines fast enough. What\u2019s wrong with those people? Don\u2019t they want to save the planet, too?</p><p>glamphier@postmedia.com</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://edmontonjournal.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://edmontonjournal.com/opinion/columnists/gary-lamphier-small-alberta-firms-slash-spending-as-pipeline-woes-mount', u'author': u'Gary Lamphier, Edmonton Journal', u'original_article_url': u'http://edmontonjournal.com/opinion/columnists/gary-lamphier-small-alberta-firms-slash-spending-as-pipeline-woes-mount', u'scraped_on': 1453987053, u'published_date': u'Thu, 28 Jan 2016 13:00:39 +0000', u'title': u'Gary Lamphier: Small Alberta firms slash spending as pipeline woes mount', u'_id': ObjectId('56aa14ed1d41c857b873ac50'), u'media_url': u'http://edmontonjournal.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>The Liberal government says it is \u201cmodernizing\u201d the way Canada reviews pipelines and other resource projects, in a revamped process that considers future greenhouse gas emissions.</p><p>The new review process seeks to promote public transparency and provides funding for First Nations consultations.</p><p>The Wednesday announcement was described as a \u201ctransition step\u201d before the Liberals establish a permanent set of rules.</p><p>The interim plan immediately applies to two highly divisive pipeline proposals \u2013- the TransMountain pipeline in B.C. and the Energy East pipeline from Alberta to New Brunswick -- and extends the government\u2019s deadline to decide the projects\u2019 fates by several months.</p><p>The Liberals are touting the changes as a step towards transparency and environmentally conscious policy. But Conservative opponents say that key questions on economic impact -- especially for hard-hit Alberta -- remain unanswered.</p><p>Green Party Leader Elizabeth May called the revamped reviews \u201can attempt to fix an unfixable process.\u201d</p><p>McKenna: \u2018No magic formula\u2019</p><p>Speaking alongside Environment Minister Catherine McKenna, Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr said the new process allows the public to better understand projects and voice concerns.</p><p>\u201cGreenhouse gas emissions for all these projects should be made public. Canadians should know all these numbers,\u201d Carr said at a Wednesday press conference in Ottawa. \u201cWithout the confidence of Canadians, none of these projects will move forward.\u201d</p><p>It remains unclear exactly how much weight the government will give to environmental impact when stacked against other factors, such as the economy or social impacts.</p><p>\u201cThere\u2019s no magic formula,\u201d McKenna said. \u201cIt\u2019s looking at the economic, environmental and social impacts and then a decision is made of what is in the public interest.\u201d</p><p>NDP MP Nathan Cullen called for \u201ca lot more clarity\u201d on the review process so that companies can better understand how to pitch a resource-related project.</p><p>\u201cClimate change is a factor -- is it 1 per cent of the factor, is it half?\u201d Cullen told CTV\u2019s Power Play. \u201cIt still feels like a subjective test.\u201d</p><p>Delayed decisions</p><p>The government won\u2019t send current pipeline proposals back to square one, but they are adding additional review time to the consideration process.</p><p>The changes effectively push back the government\u2019s pending decision on Kinder Morgan\'s TransMountain twinning project another four months to December 2016.</p><p>TransCanada\'s Energy East project has been given an additional six months for its review period, pushing the decision to mid-2018 at the earliest. In addition, three temporary members will be added to the National Energy Board to provide greater public consolation.</p><p>\u201cI\u2019m looking for a process that has the confidence of Canadians. That\u2019s why we\u2019re going to take more time,\u201d Carr said.</p><p>A ministerial adviser will be appointed by the government to consult with First Nations communities that live along the pipeline routes. The adviser effectively creates a secondary review process alongside the National Energy Board review.</p><p>In the end, Carr says the cabinet will have final say on whether the pipelines are approved or denied.</p><p>TransCanada spokesperson Mark Cooper said the company is approaching the news with caution.</p><p>"We support a strong and clear regulatory framework that helps Canadians see our commitment to building and operating oil and gas pipelines in the safest and most environmentally sound way possible," he said in a statement to The Canadian Press.</p><p>"TransCanada operates in a highly regulated industry. We will continue to work with all levels of government and our regulators to ensure the continued safe and environmentally sound transportation of our natural resources to market."</p><p>Fast: A \u2018terrible signal to the world\u2019</p><p>Criticism for the Liberals\u2019 handling of the pipeline file was swift. Even before the new review process was announced, Alberta Conservative MP Rachael Harder called out Prime Minister Justin Trudeau during question period on Wednesday, urging him to \u201cput down his selfie stick and get to work building these pipelines.\u201d</p><p>Conservative critic for environment and climate change Ed Fast told reporters that Wednesday\u2019s announcement is part of a \u201cLiberal pattern of dismissing the value of Canada\u2019s resources.\u201d</p><p>He said the new review process sends a \u201cterrible, terrible signal to the world\u201d that Canada\u2019s natural resource projects will suffer from \u201cpolitical interference.\u201d</p><p>\u201cCanada is slowly closing their doors to business and that\u2019s going to hurt Canadians badly,\u201d he said, adding that pipeline projects are important to \u201clong-term prosperity\u201d of the country.</p><p>Green Party Leader and B.C. MP Elizabeth May said the previous Conservative government \u201cdestroyed\u201d the environmental assessment process in its \u201cideological\u201d bid to push through pipeline projects. The Liberals\u2019 reaction will inevitably be \u201can imperfect response,\u201d she said.</p><p>However, May offered that the new measures are a \u201creasonable approach if and only if they (the government) rigorously review\u201d all the evidence presented at pipeline assessment hearings.</p><p>Environmental groups that have openly opposed the pipelines seemed to echo May\u2019s sentiments. Adam Scott of the Environmental Defence organization said that the resource project review has been \u201ccompletely broken\u201d in Canada over the past few years, so \u201ctoday\u2019s announcement is a good step\u2026but it\u2019s certainly not a full revamp of the process that we need to see.\u201d</p><p>\u201cThe previous process never asked truly, \u2018Are these projects in the best interests of Canadians?\u2019\u201d he said, adding that \u201casking the right questions\u201d will lead to better decisions on pipelines.</p><p>With files from the Canadian Press</p> U.S. President Barack Obama  rejected TransCanada\'s application  to build the pipeline. The company  said it would file a claim under NAFTA.  After unveiling new rules for approving pipeline projects, a decision hasbeen pushed back to mid-2018.The B.C. Supreme Court recently ruled  the province failed to properly consult with First Nations groups, an issueaddressed by new federal rules. The government has extended the deadline for a decisionto December 2016.', u'rss_url': u'http://bc.ctvnews.ca/rss/ctv-news-vancouver-1.822352', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.ctvnews.ca/politics/pipeline-reviews-to-include-environmental-regulations-first-nations-consultations-1.2754062', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.ctvnews.ca/politics/new-resource-project-reviews-to-look-at-upstream-greenhouse-gas-emissions-1.2754062', u'scraped_on': 1453944746, u'published_date': u'Wed, 27 Jan 2016 07:48:00 -0800', u'title': u'New resource project reviews to look at upstream greenhouse gas emissions', u'_id': ObjectId('56a96faa1d41c841e1d9cced'), u'media_url': u'http://bc.ctvnews.ca/'}
{u'full_article': u"<p> Dans l\u2019espoir d\u2019apaiser les tensions que suscite le projet de pipeline \xc9nergie Est, le gouvernement Trudeau en prolonge de neuf mois son \xe9valuation environnementale par le f\xe9d\xe9ral.</p><p> Les lib\xe9raux ont d\xe9voil\xe9, mercredi, une s\xe9rie de changements temporaires devant guider l\u2019Office nationale de l\u2019\xe9nergie (ON\xc9) et d\u2019autres instances f\xe9d\xe9rales dans l\u2019examen des projets actuellement \xe0 l\u2019\xe9tude.</p><p> Le ministre des Ressources naturelles, Jim Carr, a fait valoir qu\u2019il s\u2019agissait d\u2019un \xabpremier pas\xbb avant de donner suite \xe0 leur engagement \xe9lectoral de \xabmoderniser\xbb plus en profondeur l\u2019ON\xc9 et le processus d\u2019\xe9valuation environnementale.</p><p> L\u2019\xe9tude du projet de la compagnie TransCanada - qui soul\xe8ve une lev\xe9e de boucliers dans plusieurs municipalit\xe9s au Qu\xe9bec \u2013 passera ainsi de 15 \xe0 21 mois. La d\xe9cision finale du gouvernement sera pour sa part prolong\xe9e de trois mois pour un total de 27 mois. Le gouvernement devrait ainsi \xeatre amen\xe9 \xe0 prendre une d\xe9cision sur l\u2019avenir du projet en 2018.</p><p> \xabOn fait ce qu\u2019on peut avec nos principes pour accommoder \xe7a et c\u2019est certain que \xe7a va \xeatre vraiment mieux que ce ne l\u2019\xe9tait dans le pass\xe9\xbb, a pour sa part indiqu\xe9 la ministre de l\u2019Environnement, Catherine McKenna.</p><p> Gaz \xe0 effet de serre</p><p> Le gouvernement a ainsi \xe9mis certains principes devant am\xe9liorer la confiance du public dans le projet \xc9nergie Est. Parmi eux, les \xe9missions de gaz \xe0 effet de serre en amont devront \xeatre prises en compte. Des consultations \xabplus rigoureuse\xbb aupr\xe8s des peuples autochtones affect\xe9s par le projet devront \xeatre effectu\xe9es et financ\xe9es. L\u2019ON\xc9 devra \xabfaciliter\xbb la participation du public dans le processus d\u2019\xe9valuation et trois membres temporaires seront aussi nomm\xe9s par l\u2019Office.</p><p> Le d\xe9put\xe9 conservateur Steven Blaney a accus\xe9 le gouvernement de chercher \xe0 \xabr\xe9inventer le bouton \xe0 quatre trous\xbb. \xabOn a un processus stable, efficace et s\xe9curitaire et surtout le transport par pipeline, le maire Labeaume le rappelait, c\u2019est le moyen de transport le plus s\xe9curitaire d\u2019une part et d\u2019autre part c\u2019est celui qui g\xe9n\xe8re le moins de gaz \xe0 effet de serre\xbb, a-t-il lanc\xe9.</p><p> Pour le chef du NPD, Thomas Mulcair, le processus de transition annonc\xe9 par le gouvernement lib\xe9ral est \xabde la poudre aux yeux\xbb. Il accuse le premier ministre de vouloir \xabpatcher\xbb un syst\xe8me que le gouvernement pr\xe9c\xe9dent a rendu \xabd\xe9faillant\xbb.</p><p> \xabLe probl\xe8me, c'est qu'en ce moment, M. Trudeau est en train de dire on va prendre le processus conservateur, compl\xe8tement d\xe9faillant, on va vous ajouter un petit bout ici, puis le tout va \xeatre correct\xbb, a-t-il d\xe9plor\xe9.</p>", u'rss_url': u'http://www.journaldemontreal.com/rss.xml', u'language': u'fr', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.journaldemontreal.com/2016/01/27/ottawa-prolonge-de-neuf-mois-son-evaluation-environnementale-sur-le-projet-de-pipeline-energie-est', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.journaldemontreal.com/2016/01/27/ottawa-prolonge-de-neuf-mois-son-evaluation-environnementale-sur-le-projet-de-pipeline-energie-est', u'scraped_on': 1453945028, u'published_date': u'Wed, 27 Jan 2016 17:16:24 EST', u'title': u'Ottawa prolonge de neuf mois son \xe9valuation environnementale sur le projet de pipeline \xc9nergie Est', u'_id': ObjectId('56a970c41d41c841e1d9dc49'), u'media_url': u'http://www.journaldemontreal.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>The Trudeau government\u2019s overhaul of the review process for two major pipeline proposals seen as key to bolstering Alberta\u2019s ailing economy was greeted with both cautious optimism and strong criticism on Wednesday.</p><p>Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr and Environment Minister Catherine McKenna announced that the Liberal government\xa0would extend the timelines for\xa0reviewing and deciding on the Energy East and Trans Mountain oil pipelines as part of a new interim review.</p><p>Both of those proposals \u2014 as well as future major energy projects \u2014 will be assessed based on the\xa0\u201cupstream\u201d greenhouse gas emissions produced in the extraction and processing of the oil they propose to carry.</p><p class="p1">The government will also appoint a ministerial adviser who will consult with indigenous communities along the proposed pipeline routes.</p><p class="p1">McKenna said the changes are needed to \u201crebuild\xa0Canadians\u2019 trust in our environmental assessment processes,\u201d though she said there is \u201cno magic formula\u201d in how decisions will be made.\xa0</p><p class="p1">\u201cIf a project is found to have significant effects, it goes to cabinet for decision. In that decision-making process, all factors are taken into account,\u201d she told reporters in Ottawa.</p><p class="p1">\u201cIt is looking at the economic impact, the environmental impact, the social impact and then a decision is made on what is in the public interest.\u201d</p><p class="p1">The changes announced Thursday mean the Trudeau government will seek an additional four months in the assessment\xa0process for Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain line to Vancouver, pushing its decision to December 2016. TransCanada Corp.\u2019s Energy East pipeline will face an additional\xa0nine months.</p><p class="p1">Alberta\u2019s NDP government says it will support\xa0Ottawa\u2019s plan to revamp the review process for two major pipeline proposals\xa0\u2014\xa0if it leads to progress in getting them built.</p><p class="p1">Provincial Environment Minister Shannon Phillips said the Notley government can live with the changes.</p><p class="p1">\u201cIf those two pieces can lead us to a positive reception and in fact a decision on these pipelines, then we welcome that,\u201d she said in an Edmonton news conference.</p><p class="p1">\u201cOf course, we are not interested in unnecessary delays but the fact of the matter is that we have inherited a situation here that did not have the confidence of Canadians.\u201d</p><p class="p1">Phillips shrugged off the uncertainty around how greenhouse gas emissions will be weighed. She said Alberta\u2019s recently unveiled climate strategy\xa0\u2014\xa0which includes a cap on oilsands emissions, a planned carbon tax and an accelerated phase-out of coal-fired power \u2014 should be beneficial to the projects when they are reviewed under the new equation.\xa0</p><p class="p1">\u201cAll of those things working together, we know have already reset the conversation about how Alberta develops its energy products,\u201d she said.</p><p class="p1">In a statement, Wildrose Leader Brian Jean blasted the federal overhaul as \u201cmore red tape and more delays.\u201d</p><p class="p1">\u201cThe National Energy Board pipeline review process is one of the most thorough in the world, and I\u2019m discouraged that today\u2019s announcement will further delay these common sense decisions on projects that will benefit Canada\u2019s economy,\u201d he said.</p><p class="p1">With oil prices dipping under US$30 a barrel and hammering the Alberta economy, pipelines that would allow the province\u2019s energy to access new markets is seen as vital by the province.</p><p class="p1">With files from Postmedia and The Canadian Press\xa0</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/news/politics/ottawa-pushes-back-pipeline-approvals-says-ghgs-will-be-considered', u'author': u'James Wood, Calgary Herald', u'original_article_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/news/politics/ottawa-pushes-back-pipeline-approvals-says-ghgs-will-be-considered', u'scraped_on': 1453944789, u'published_date': u'Thu, 28 Jan 2016 01:07:52 +0000', u'title': u'Ottawa pushes back pipeline approvals, says GHGs will be considered', u'_id': ObjectId('56a96fd51d41c841e1d9cf8b'), u'media_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'zoom<p class="articlerelatedvideogallery section"></p><p itemprop="articleBody">OTTAWA\u2014 The federal government is revamping the way resource projects are reviewed in Canada, including a requirement to look at upstream greenhouse gas emissions and more consultation with Canada\u2019s Aboriginal peoples.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The new policy, which the government calls a transition step while it hammers out new permanent rules, will apply to two contentious pipeline projects dominating headlines: the proposed Trans Mountain oil pipeline in B.C. and TransCanada\u2019s Energy East project from Alberta to New Brunswick.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">But they\u2019ll apply to all resource projects, including LNG and mining proposals.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">\u201cWe believe it is important and, in fact, essential to rebuild Canadians\u2019 trust in our environmental assessment processes,\u201d Environment Minister Catherine McKenna told a news conference Wednesday.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">\u201cWe need to take into account the views and concerns of Canadians, respect the rights and interests of indigenous peoples and support our natural resources sector.\u201d</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Hearings on Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain twinning project are almost over and the government is extending the deadline for a decision by four months, pushing it to next December.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">As for Energy East, the government is extending the project review period by an extra six months and adding three temporary members to the National Energy Board in an effort to do greater public consultation.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">TransCanada spokesman Mark Cooper said the company needs time to digest the new rules. \u201cWe support a strong and clear regulatory framework that helps Canadians see our commitment to building and operating oil and gas pipelines in the safest and most environmentally sound way possible,\u201d he said in an emailed statement.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">\u201cTransCanada operates in a highly regulated industry. We will continue to work with all levels of government and our regulators to ensure the continued safe and environmentally sound transportation of our natural resources to market.\u201d</p><p itemprop="articleBody">A decision on Energy East is now not expected before the middle of 2018.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The new assessment rules stress the requirement for input from indigenous communities. The government says it will provide funding to assist those deliberations.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr said the changes will provide pipeline proponents greater certainty about the time involved in reaching decisions.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">\u201cIf we\u2019re going to attract the investments we need to sustainably develop our energy resources, then we have to better engage Canadians, conduct deeper consultations with indigenous peoples and base decisions on science, facts and evidence,\u201d Carr said.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">At least one advocate hailed the changes as more evidence of a new consultative, environmentally friendly approach to resource development, but complained that Energy East would be allowed to proceed under the new, transitional process.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">\u201cIf the transitional process is just for projects under review, it shouldn\u2019t apply to Energy East,\u201d Environmental Defence spokesman Adam Scott said in a statement.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">\u201cWe welcome these new design principles for a review process that assesses the full climate impacts of all infrastructure proposals, enables meaningful and ongoing public participation, and ensures the ability of indigenous peoples to exercise their decision-making authority.\u201d</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/feeds.articles.news.canada.rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/news/canada/2016/01/27/pipeline-reviews-to-look-at-upstream-greenhouse-gas-emissions.html', u'author': u'The Canadian Press', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/news/canada/2016/01/27/pipeline-reviews-to-look-at-upstream-greenhouse-gas-emissions.html', u'scraped_on': 1453945063, u'published_date': u'Wed, 27 Jan 2016 17:47:59 EST', u'title': u'Pipeline reviews to look at upstream greenhouse gas emissions', u'_id': ObjectId('56a970e91d41c841e1d9dfe8'), u'media_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>It looks as if Prime Minister Justin Trudeau is mimicking U.S. President Barack Obama\u2019s \u201call of the above\u201d energy strategy when it comes to getting the controversial Energy East pipeline approved.</p>&#13;<p>If so, it\u2019s a smart move, because by constantly proclaiming his concern about the environment and man-made greenhouse gas emissions linked to climate change, Obama has been able to grow the U.S. fossil fuel industry under the radar of political opponents and the environmental movement.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau seems to be attempting the same thing with TransCanada\u2019s proposed, $16 billion, 4,600-km, Energy East pipeline, needed to deliver land-locked Canadian oil from Alberta to New Brunswick and from there to global markets.</p>&#13;<p>That is, quell environmental opposition in eastern Canada, mainly from Quebec, by telling domestic and foreign critics of the oil sands there\u2019s a new sheriff in town -- him -- and that Canada will develop its oil resources in an environmentally responsible way, including putting a price on industrial carbon dioxide emissions.</p>&#13;<p>That\u2019s right out of Obama\u2019s playbook.</p>&#13;<p>Throughout his presidency, Obama has talked up his public financing of the U.S. renewable energy sector (with little real-world success); national carbon pricing initiative (defeated in Congress); vetoing of the Keystone XL pipeline (symbolic rather than significant), and introduction of new regulations for U.S. coal-fired power plants (to be the subject of legal challenges long after he\u2019s gone).</p>&#13;<p>By using these high-profile, if mainly symbolic policies as political cover, Obama has been able to dramatically increase U.S. oil and gas production, drill for oil in the Arctic even after the Deepwater Horizon blowout, quadruple the number of operating rigs, open up 75% of potential U.S. offshore oil resources and millions of acres in 23 states to gas and oil exploration.</p>&#13;<p>While Obama stopped the cross border leg of the Keystone XL to deliver Alberta bitumen to refineries on the U.S. Gulf Coast, he simultaneously, as he boasted in a speech to pipeline workers in Oklahoma in 2012,\u201cadded enough new oil and gas pipeline (in the U.S.) to encircle the Earth and then some.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>As Obama described it: \u201cSo we are drilling all over the place -- right now ...That\u2019s not the problem.In fact, the problem ... is that we\u2019re actually producing so much oil and gas ... that we don\u2019t have enough pipeline capacity to transport all of it to where it needs to go -- both to refineries, and then, eventually, all across the country and around the world.There\u2019s a bottleneck ... because we can\u2019t get enough of the oil to our refineries fast enough.And if we could, then we would be able to increase our oil supplies at a time when they\u2019re needed as much as possible.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>While the crash in oil prices has slowed production temporarily, Trudeau appears to be using a similar strategy in Canada.</p>&#13;<p>First, make a show of his concern for the environment -- for example by hauling more than 300 politicians and officials to the UN climate change meeting in Paris last month -- twice the size of the U.S. contingent and three times as big as the U.K.\u2019s.</p>&#13;<p>Second, urge everyone from Energy East critic Denis Coderre, the mayor of Montreal, to anti oil sands celebrity/activist Leonardo DiCaprio at the World Economic Forum, to cool their opposition because Canada will soon have a national carbon price under his leadership and Alberta Premier Rachel Notley\u2019s NDP government just introduced a carbon tax.</p>&#13;<p>Let\u2019s hope it works.</p>&#13;<p>\xa0</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/home/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, {u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>Canada doesn\u2019t need a more transparent pipeline review process. It has one of those already.</p>&#13;<p>What it needs is politicians with more courage to push through projects that receive approval from the National Energy Board (NEB).</p>&#13;<p>On Wednesday, federal Liberal Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr released some interim measures for reviewing the Trans Mountain and Energy East pipeline projects. They won\u2019t help a bit to get needed pipelines built.</p>&#13;<p>Carr\u2019s six interim guidelines are reminiscent of British Columbia\u2019s five hoops through which Alberta and oil companies must jump to get permission to build a pipeline to the West Coast.</p>&#13;<p>I have called the B.C. criteria, introduced in 2012 in connection with the Northern Gateway approval process, the \u201cmoving goalposts.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>The conditions were designed intentionally so they could never be satisfied. Whenever Alberta or Gateway\u2019s owners, Enbridge, got close to meeting one or more of the conditions, the B.C. government could change the requirements slightly and never have to say \u201cyes.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>The federal Liberals\u2019 interim measures are very similar.</p>&#13;<p>British Columbia required a comprehensive environmental review, full participation by and benefits for First Nations and a \u201cfair share\u201d of fiscal and economic benefits for their province.</p>&#13;<p>Carr insists Trans Mountain and Energy East may only move forward after there have been \u201cdeeper consultations with Indigenous peoples,\u201d better analysis of environmental impacts and appointment of \u201ca ministerial representative to engage communities \u2026 potentially affected by the project\u201d and ensure those communities are happy.</p>&#13;<p>B.C.\u2019s conditions were political cover and so are the Trudeau government\u2019s. They are so broad and so ill-defined that the Liberals could easily use them to deny construction permits to Kinder Morgan (Trans Mountain) and TransCanada (Energy East), no matter how many conditions the companies satisfy.</p>&#13;<p>Public opinion polls, not technical or scientific reviews, will determine whether the pipelines Alberta needs will proceed. If loud environmentalists and trendy editorialists continue to oppose pipelines out of alarmism over climate change, Carr, Trudeau and the Liberals will simply say \u201csorry, our conditions haven\u2019t been met. You can\u2019t have your pipelines.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>This is all about politics and nothing about \u201crestoring public confidence in the environmental review process\u201d as the prime minister and Natural Resources minister claim.</p>&#13;<p>The Northern Gateway review was the most extensive in the history of Canadian pipelines. After nearly three years of review and nearly 2,000 public submissions, the NEB approved the project to carry Alberta bitumen to a tanker port on the B.C. coast \u2013 with 209 conditions attached.</p>&#13;<p>But that wasn\u2019t good enough for the goalpost-moving B.C. government or for the extremely well-funded environmental activists opposed to the project. So even without a Parliamentary motion, back in November Trudeau effectively killed Northern Gateway by directing his Transportation minister to impose a tanker ban on the West Coast.</p>&#13;<p>Gateway might get built, but the oil couldn\u2019t go anywhere. So it is unlikely investors would ever spend the money to construct it.</p>&#13;<p>No matter what the Liberals do to change the NEB process, some provinces and most eco-activists will remain opposed to every pipeline. It\u2019s not the possibility of a pipeline leak or a tanker spill they\u2019re truly worried about. They\u2019re opposed to what\u2019s in the pipeline \u2013 oil, a carbon-based fuel that they worry will cause dangerous climate change.</p>&#13;<p>There is no way any review process can satisfy these extremists.</p>&#13;<p>That\u2019s why Carr\u2019s and the Liberals\u2019 interim measures are a fraud. So long as Quebec and B.C. remain opposed to pipelines (and so long as the Liberals want votes there more than in Alberta), all the new measures do is give Ottawa a convenient tool to kill the projects.</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/opinion/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/2016/01/27/new-initiatives-are-so-ill-defined-the-liberals-could-deny-permits-no-matter-how-many-conditions-are-met', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/2016/01/27/new-initiatives-are-so-ill-defined-the-liberals-could-deny-permits-no-matter-how-many-conditions-are-met', u'scraped_on': 1453949257, u'published_date': u'Wed, 27 Jan 2016 19:26:08 MST', u'title': u'New initiatives are so ill-defined the Liberals could deny permits no matter how many conditions are met', u'_id': ObjectId('56a9814b1d41c845ca66c1ce'), u'media_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p class="articlerelatedphotogallery section"></p><p class="articlerelatedvideogallery section"></p><p itemprop="articleBody">Notwithstanding the political furor over Montreal\u2019s opposition to the Energy East pipeline, the first test of Justin Trudeau\u2019s government\u2019s approach to the controversial pipeline file will take place in British Columbia.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">To watch the backlash that has attended the news that Montreal-area mayors believe TransCanada project is too environmentally risky for the good of the more than four million people they represent, one would think they make up a fifth column that has just seized on an original idea to sabotage Canada.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">In the House of Commons on Monday, the Conservatives rushed to the barricades calling on Trudeau to travel to Montreal to give his former Liberal colleague Denis Coderre a dressing-down in the name of national unity.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">More than a few pundits opined the prime minister would ultimately have no option other than to force a pipeline to link Alberta\u2019s oilsands to the coast of New Brunswick through his home province.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The CBC\u2019s Rick Mercer joined the chorus. \u201cThis has nothing to do with Montreal. This has nothing to do with Quebec,\u201d he ranted on this week\u2019s show, arguing that Canada\u2019s superior interest in getting more oil to market should supersede provincial or municipal considerations.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">I was away from Canada on the second week of January and I might have missed a similar Mercer segment dealing with the so-called selfishness of British Columbia. On Monday, I did not hear the Conservative opposition urging Trudeau to fly to Victoria to talk reason to Premier Christy Clark.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">And yet, in a final written submission to the National Energy Board submitted on Jan. 11, just days before the Coderre news conference, the B.C. government came down hard against a pipeline expansion that many describe as just as vital to Alberta\u2019s economic health as Energy East.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The Trans Mountain plan is different from Energy East in that it involves the tripling of the capacity of an existing pipeline. It links Alberta to the Vancouver area and the Pacific Coast. But opposition to both plans has its roots in the same public health and environmental concerns. Inasmuch as the Quebec government \u2014 in contrast with its B.C. counterpart \u2014 has not yet come down against Energy East, opposition to the Trans Mountain is also of a higher order of political magnitude.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Last May, Metro Vancouver mayors lined up against the project. At the time Vancouver\u2019s Gregor Robertson was no less blunt than Coderre last week. Based on an expert report that concluded a spill could have dire consequences for the air quality of the million people who live in the area and the region\u2019s wildlife, Robertson dismissed the project as a \u201cbad deal for Vancouver.\u201d</p><p itemprop="articleBody">In its NEB submission, Clark\u2019s government concluded the company had failed to prove it would meet stringent oil spill safety requirements.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The Kinder Morgan project was on the drawing board before Energy East. It is further along in the regulatory process with the NEB scheduled \u2014 until Trudeau\u2019s government came up with a longer timeline and an expanded consultation process \u2014 to make a recommendation to the federal government in the spring with a final cabinet decision a few months later. That will now happen in December.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The board was always expected to make any approval of the Kinder Morgan plan contingent on a series of conditions. As things stand now, those conditions would be unlikely to sway B.C.\u2019s municipal and provincial governments. </p><p itemprop="articleBody">In a damning report published this week federal environment commissioner Julie Gelfand found that the NEB fails to follow up on whether its conditions are met in the case of half the projects it oversees. The hope in federal Liberal circles is that the extended review announced on Wednesday will restore confidence in the process. </p><p itemprop="articleBody">It is hard to imagine that the federal government would bow to provincial opposition to a pipeline at the Pacific end of the country only to turn around and force an equally unpopular a project on the Atlantic side. Trudeau\u2019s success in turning the anti-pipeline tide in B.C. will go a long way to determine how far his government will push Quebec on Energy East.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">In the meantime, what we have had so far from some of the proponents of the TransCanada pipeline is an unhelpful case of two-tier political indignation.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/feeds.articles.news.rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/news/canada/2016/01/28/bc-will-help-shape-battleground-in-pipeline-debate-back-east-hbert.html', u'author': u'Chantal H\xe9bert - National Affairs', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/news/canada/2016/01/28/bc-will-help-shape-battleground-in-pipeline-debate-back-east-hbert.html', u'scraped_on': 1453975652, u'published_date': u'Thu, 28 Jan 2016 05:00:00 EST', u'title': u'B.C. will help shape battleground in pipeline debate back east: H\xe9bert', u'_id': ObjectId('56a9e8641d41c84ddf450ca1'), u'media_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>Mark Milke is a Calgary author.</p><p>In the lead-up to Confederation in 1867, one perennial concern was how the small-minded provincialism of local politicians made it difficult for merchants and travellers alike to criss-cross British North America.</p><p>Thus, in 1865, George Brown, founder of The Globe newspaper and a reform-minded politician in the Province of Canada\u2019s Legislative Assembly, compared travel to Nova Scotia and New Brunswick as akin to visiting a foreign country, where a \u201ccustoms officer meets you at the frontier, arrests your progress and levies his imposts on your effects.\u201d</p><p>That problem of artificial barriers (provincial tariffs on one\u2019s goods, for example) was one reason why Brown favoured the prospect of Confederation, and he was not alone. New Brunswick legislator John McMillan, also speaking in 1865, argued in favour of a \u201cunion of the provinces\u201d because, among other benefits, it would \u201cenable us to have free trade with our neighbours [and be] commercially the best step we could take.\u201d</p><p>Likewise, George-\xc9tienne Cartier, co-premier with John A. Macdonald for a spell in the late 1850s and early 1860s in the Province of Canada, saw that it was precisely because a cacophony of local interests existed, and could unnecessarily interfere in greater coast-to-coast prosperity, that \u201cthe federation system ought to be resorted to and would be found to work well.\u201d His colleagues, listening to his speech in February, 1865, responded with \u201cHear, hear.\u201d</p><p>Brown, McMillan, Cartier and other early politicians envisioned a Canada with a dominion (federal) government that would promote, protect and ensure the national interest. That included a free flow of goods, services and national projects. It was necessary to counter the inevitable tendency of the occasional puffed-up provincial potentate to otherwise obstruct nation building, a grander Canadian identity and a wider and deeper prosperity.</p><p>In contrast to those mid-19th century statesmen, a recent example of local potentates just popped up in Montreal.</p><p>There, Mayor Denis Coderre and other area politicians have made clear they oppose the Energy East pipeline proposed by TransCanada. That\u2019s the one that would ship 1.1 million barrels of oil daily from the Western Canada sedimentary basin to refineries in Quebec and New Brunswick. Energy East could potentially replace $8-billion worth of imported oil from Saudi Arabia and other countries that now flow into Central and Eastern Canada.</p><p>Mr. Coderre and others oppose the possible Canadian/foreign oil \u201cswap\u201d due to a psychological need for perfection: \u201cWhen it comes to transporting oil, we need a perfect score, and we cannot make mistakes,\u201d he opined in a recent column in The Calgary Herald.</p><p>Perfection is not a realistic demand, least of all for politicians or pipelines, or even for the Earth itself, considering how it groans, shudders, erupts and cracks tectonic plates, among other unpredictable activities. But for those who prefer to deal in reality, here\u2019s a useful fact: Pipelines are the safest way to transport oil, as Michel Kelly-Gagnon, president of the Montreal Economic Institute, has pointed out. But Mr. Coderre already knows that from Quebec\u2019s recent tragic encounter with another form of oil transportation: rail.</p><p>As part of his quest for absolutism against a tube that carries black liquid used by Quebeckers and everyone else, Mr. Coderre mentions his concern about carbon emissions. On such a matter, the mayor desires to reassure fellow politicians, such as those he recently met at the United Nations climate conference in Paris.</p><p>But Energy East is not just about finding a domestic market for Western oil production, it\u2019s also about displacing imports. And no honest person thinks the world will soon end its reliance on 96 million barrels of oil, daily. Thus Mr. Coderre\u2019s reality-ignoring position mimics the naysaying more often found among hard-line energy opponents who harbour romantic visions about a pre-energy Eden.</p><p>One would think Mr. Coderre and his colleagues would be more pragmatic. That includes recognizing it is oil and gas that help Quebeckers endure winter, and that such energy assists everyone else on the planet with much else. But the mayor\u2019s demand is born from a political marriage of theatrics and histrionics.</p><p>In blessed contrast to local Montreal politicians, Quebec City Mayor R\xe9gis Labeaume supports Energy East. As he told one reporter: \u201cIn a normal country, all organizations that want to build infrastructure for transporting energy should be able to do it.\u201d</p><p>Mr. Labeaume thus echoes the optimistic, entrepreneurial and national sentiments of statesmen at the time of Confederation such as Cartier, McMillan and others, or indeed of Brown, who in his 1865 speech, also advocated for a \u201cscheme\u201d \u2013 Confederation \u2013 to help Canadians develop our \u201cgreat natural resources.\u201d</p><p class="article__ontwitter">Follow us on Twitter: @GlobeBusiness</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/?service=rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/report-on-business/rob-commentary/canadas-national-interest-has-always-trumped-the-local-potentates/article28418513/', u'author': u'Mark Milke', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/report-on-business/rob-commentary/canadas-national-interest-has-always-trumped-the-local-potentates/article28418513/?cmpid=rss1', u'scraped_on': 1453975610, u'published_date': u'Thu, 28 Jan 2016 05:00:00 -0500', u'title': u'Canada\u2019s national interest has always trumped the local potentates', u'_id': ObjectId('56a9e83d1d41c84ddf4507f6'), u'media_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>When B.C. Premier Christy Clark\u2019s government rejected the expansion of Kinder Morgan Canada\u2019s Trans Mountain pipeline earlier this month, no questions were raised about the threat to national unity. When eco-friendly Vancouver Mayor Gregor Robertson later applauded his province\u2019s decision, it wasn\u2019t seen as \u201cpetty politics.\u201d</p><p>So why is Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre\u2019s rejection of the Energy East pipeline tagged as \u201cparochial\u201d? Mr. Coderre, speaking for mayors representing the four million residents of the Greater Montreal area, said the project currently on the table is not acceptable. He raised concerns about possible oil leaks in a fragile environment and said the risks, as of now, greatly outweigh the small economic benefits for the Montreal area.</p><p>The outrage in many quarters was spectacular. Saskatchewan Premier Brad Wall suggested Quebec might return its equalization payments if it so dislikes Canadian oil. Others presented the pipeline as a \u201cnation-building\u201d project. To abandon it would threaten Canada\u2019s very existence, it seems. Is this a new transcontinental railway?</p><p>Many mayors and groups across the country, including First Nations leaders, have raised concerns about, if not outright opposition to, TransCanada\u2019s Energy East project. The Ontario Energy Board, in a report last summer, was critical of the pipeline, asked that it be rerouted and said an oil-spill cleanup could cost $1-billion.</p><p>You can call it the NIMBY syndrome (not in my backyard); you can debate the fantasy-like concept of zero environmental risk. You can argue that the project is good for Canada, maybe vital to some areas.</p><p>But why is it that when the opposition comes from Quebec, it is seen to be un-Canadian?</p><p>Mr. Coderre, a former federal minister, would make a poor mascot for Quebec\u2019s independence movement (an option that touched a new low, at 34 per cent, last week, according to a CROP poll). His Liberal and federalist credentials can be detected in his DNA.</p><p>It\u2019s true that Quebec receives the most from the national equalization program, and it\u2019s true that Quebeckers like to think of themselves as greener then green, and generally underestimate the economic benefits of the oil industry for all of Canada.</p><p>But does this mean environmental concerns are unacceptable if they come from a \u201chave-not\u201d province? Does it mean everyone in Quebec should just shut up?</p><p>So Mr. Coderre is criticized for defending the interests of Montreal-area residents, allegedly without thinking about the consequences for the rest of the country. As opposed to other, more enlightened, politicians whose first thought is for people outside their constituencies? Please provide us with a picture of such a politician.</p><p>For now, TransCanada Corp. has done a poor job of selling the project. The National Energy Board has yet to approve it. The Montreal-area mayors are not part of a separatist plot. And if you read correctly, the opposition is not irrevocable.</p><p>When push comes to shove, the federal government will have the final say. Meanwhile, words of contempt and inflammatory rhetoric against Quebec political leaders might hurt national unity more than legitimate protests from local politicians.</p><p>-----------------------------</p><p>yves.boisvert@lapresse.ca</p><p class="article__ontwitter">Follow us on Twitter: @GlobeDebate</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/?service=rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/opinion/why-is-it-un-canadian-for-quebeckers-to-oppose-a-pipeline/article28423438/', u'author': u'YVES BOISVERT', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/opinion/why-is-it-un-canadian-for-quebeckers-to-oppose-a-pipeline/article28423438/?cmpid=rss1', u'scraped_on': 1453979231, u'published_date': u'Thu, 28 Jan 2016 06:00:00 -0500', u'title': u'Why is it un-Canadian for Quebeckers to oppose a pipeline?', u'_id': ObjectId('56a9f6601d41c84eb15efff4'), u'media_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>The Energy East pipeline proposed route is pictured as TransCanada officials speak during a news conference in Calgary, on Aug. 1, 2013.Jeff McIntosh / THE CANADIAN PRESS</p><p>Montreal has long been an important hub for transportation infrastructure \u2014 a hub that connects Canada from coast to coast to coast, and a hub that connects Canada to the world.</p><p>Every day, be it by railway, road or the Saint Lawrence Seaway, goods move through the city on their way to other parts of Canada or from Canada to international markets. Quebec and Canada are stronger because of it \u2014 transportation infrastructure is the foundation of Canada\u2019s collective economic strength.</p><p>Building Energy East is a nationally important piece of transportation infrastructure. It would allow Quebec refineries to use Canadian crude oil instead of imports, and it would connect New Brunswick and markets abroad to Canada\u2019s vast oil resources. All Canadians, including people in Quebec, would benefit as a result.</p><p>We need a unified national will to turn this vision into reality.</p><p>Some say the environmental risks of building Energy East outweigh the economic benefits. This argument, however, overlooks the larger benefits of Canadian oil infrastructure to Quebec and Canada, as well as the industry\u2019s safety record.</p><p>Canadian Energy Pipeline Association data shows that between 2002 and 2013, pipelines transported oil with a 99.999 per cent safety record. This includes pipelines that feed refineries in Quebec.</p><p>These refineries process oil, much of which arrives in Quebec from foreign sources such as the United States, Algeria, Angola and Kazakhstan at higher prices than Canadian oil.</p><p>The province spent nearly $14 billion in 2013 on oil imports, according to government data. That\u2019s the equivalent of the annual economic activity created by Canada\u2019s beer sector, founded in Montreal by the Molson family, and it\u2019s money that flows out of the province, with little in the way of economic return.</p><p>Canada has the third-largest oil reserves in the world, yet much of the oil for Quebec and Atlantic Canada is imported.</p><p>Expanding pipeline infrastructure in Quebec and Atlantic Canada will allow us to keep the money spent on imports in Canada, where it can help create jobs and grow the economy. That, indeed, would be a substantial economic benefit in addition to the benefits from oilsands development Quebec already enjoys.</p><p>The Canadian oilsands sector has purchased $600 million in goods and services from Quebec over the past two years. More than 190 Quebec companies, many in the Montreal area, have direct business relationships with the oilsands. By expanding Canada\u2019s pipeline infrastructure, we can build on these benefits and stimulate the kind of economic activity that keeps people working.</p><p>And this is the crux of my argument: Pipelines are nationally important infrastructure that helps build Canada\u2019s economic strength, just like highways and railroads. These Canadian infrastructure projects will generate revenue and create jobs, all without any taxpayer funding. The ability for Canadian oil to have more access to more markets is critical for the industry, the national economy and the prosperity of all Canadians, including people in Quebec.</p><p class="p1">In view of Alberta\u2019s stringent carbon policy and climate leadership plan, using responsibly produced Canadian oil in Quebec is also an opportunity to displace oil from foreign countries that have less stringent environmental standards. Many of the oil suppliers to Quebec do not have the regulatory system or transparent environmental reporting Canada has.</p><p>Environment Canada reports that emissions per barrel of oil produced from the oilsands are down 30 per cent from 1990 levels. Pilot projects in the oilsands, such as the use of solvents for in situ projects, will reduce emissions even further.</p><p>These are some facts that should inform a reasonable debate about how Canada collectively can take action to build the pipeline infrastructure that will make our provinces and country stronger.</p><p>We should all get behind this effort to slice through the Gordian knot that prevents our country from reaping the full benefits of our abundant oil and natural gas resources.</p><p>Tim McMillan is president and chief executive officer of the Canadian Association of Petroleum Producers. He lives in Calgary.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/opinion/columnists/opinion-why-quebecers-should-support-the-energy-east-pipeline', u'author': u'Edie Austin, Montreal Gazette', u'original_article_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/opinion/columnists/opinion-why-quebecers-should-support-the-energy-east-pipeline', u'scraped_on': 1453929414, u'published_date': u'Wed, 27 Jan 2016 21:05:43 +0000', u'title': u'Opinion: Why Quebecers should support the Energy East pipeline', u'_id': ObjectId('56a933c61d41c8330ca3a29e'), u'media_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>OTTAWA \u2014 The Liberal government has added two new\xa0review mechanisms\xa0to how major\xa0new pipeline proposals are assessed, but Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr says\xa0approval or\xa0rejection ultimately will come down to a judgment call by cabinet.</p><p>Carr and Environment Minister Catherine McKenna announced Wednesday that all resource projects \u2014 and specifically the hotly contested Trans Mountain and Energy East pipelines \u2014 will be assessed in part on the greenhouse gas emissions produced "upstream" in the extraction and processing of the oil they propose to carry.</p><p>The government will also\xa0appoint a ministerial adviser who will consult with indigenous communities along the proposed pipeline routes.</p><p>The new reviews will be carried out apart from\xa0and in addition to deliberations by the National Energy Board, which under the previous Conservative government was given the power to do environmental assessments and public consultations under circumscribed timelines.</p><p>"We believe it is important and, in fact, essential to rebuild Canadians\' trust in our environmental assessment processes," McKenna said at a news conference.</p><p>"We need to take into account the views and concerns of Canadians, respect the rights and interests of indigenous peoples and support our natural resources sector."</p><p>McKenna said the goal is to make major resource project decisions based on scientific evidence, but political and economic imperatives will clearly be\xa0part of the deliberations.</p><p>"The government of Canada is free to establish its own review and its own criteria for those reviews in whatever context it chooses," Carr responded when asked how the various reviews mechanisms will be weighed and assessed.</p><p>"And the cabinet ultimately will decide \u2014 weighing all the factors that are important to determine the national interest for these major projects."</p><p>The changes are an interim step while the government works out a new project review process after consulting Canadians, a process McKenna said could take years.</p><p>The temporary measures will apply to all project assessments, including liquefied natural gas terminals and mining proposals.</p><p>But the immediate impact will be on\xa0two contentious pipeline projects that are currently dominating headlines:\xa0Kinder Morgan\'s\xa0proposal to twin the\xa0Trans Mountain oil pipeline in B.C. and TransCanada\'s Energy East project from Alberta to New Brunswick.</p><p>NEB hearings on the\xa0Trans Mountain\xa0project are almost over and the government is extending the deadline for a decision by four months, pushing it\xa0to next December.</p><p>As for Energy East, the government is extending the project review period by an extra six months and adding three temporary members to the National Energy Board in an effort to do greater public consultation. A decision on the pipeline is not expected before the middle of 2018 under the new timeline.</p><p>TransCanada spokesman Mark Cooper said the company needs time to digest the new rules.</p><p>"We support a strong and clear regulatory framework that helps Canadians see our commitment to building and operating oil and gas pipelines in the safest and most environmentally sound way possible," he said in an emailed statement.</p><p>"TransCanada operates in a highly regulated industry. We will continue to work with all levels of government and our regulators to ensure the continued safe and environmentally sound transportation of our natural resources to market."</p><p>Tim McMillan of the Canadian Association of Petroleum Producers said the industry organization welcomes the Liberal move to clarify the transition rules, but remains concerned about further delays and duplication built into the review process.</p><p>"Getting our product to market in a timely fashion is and has been a challenge," said McMillan.</p><p>Environmental groups, which have said Canada can approve no new pipelines if it is serious about meeting its international climate change commitments, applauded Wednesday\'s changes \u2014 even though the Liberals insist they\'re committed to getting Alberta and Saskatchewan crude to tidewater for export.</p><p>Conservative MPs were not impressed.</p><p>"This is not good news for the natural resources sector," said Conservative energy critic Candice Bergen, adding it sends a discouraging signal to investors. "This another layer, another process."</p><p>In Edmonton, Alberta Environment Minister Shannon Phillips said the province is behind any process that improves public confidence and bolsters the chances of pipelines getting built.</p><p>"Of course we are not interested in unnecessary delays, but the fact of the matter is that we have inherited a (review process) situation that didn\'t have the confidence of Canadians," Phillips said.</p><p>Alberta has already taken steps to reduce greenhouse gas emissions with a new climate strategy introduced last fall, she added.\xa0That process will cap oilsands emissions, phase out coal-fired electricity generation, and impose a broad-based carbon tax.</p><p>"We have done our part to earn that confidence of Canadians," said Phillips.</p><p>"Now the federal government is, (through) this interim solution, attempting to have that honest conversation and ensure that voices are heard so that we can get to a decision on these matters."</p><p>\u2014 Follow @BCheadle on Twitter</p><p>Bruce Cheadle, The Canadian Press</p><p>Note to readers: This is a corrected story.
