{u'full_article': u'<p>OTTAWA \u2014 The Liberal government has added two new review mechanisms to how major new pipeline proposals are assessed, but Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr says approval or rejection ultimately will come down to a judgment call by cabinet.</p><p>Carr and Environment Minister Catherine McKenna announced Wednesday that all resource projects \u2014 and specifically the hotly contested Trans Mountain and Energy East pipelines \u2014 will be assessed in part on the greenhouse gas emissions produced "upstream" in the extraction and processing of the oil they propose to carry.</p><p>The government will also appoint a ministerial adviser who will consult with indigenous communities along the proposed pipeline routes.</p><p>The new reviews will be carried out apart from and in addition to deliberations by the National Energy Board, which under the previous Conservative government was given the power to do environmental assessments and public consultations under circumscribed timelines.</p><p>"We believe it is important and, in fact, essential to rebuild Canadians\' trust in our environmental assessment processes," McKenna said at a news conference.</p><p>"We need to take into account the views and concerns of Canadians, respect the rights and interests of indigenous peoples and support our natural resources sector."</p><p>McKenna said the goal is to make major resource project decisions based on scientific evidence, but political and economic imperatives will clearly be part of the deliberations.</p><p>"The government of Canada is free to establish its own review and its own criteria for those reviews in whatever context it chooses," Carr responded when asked how the various reviews mechanisms will be weighed and assessed.</p><p>"And the cabinet ultimately will decide \u2014 weighing all the factors that are important to determine the national interest for these major projects."</p><p>The changes are an interim step while the government works out a new project review process after consulting Canadians, a process McKenna said could take years.</p><p>The temporary measures will apply to all project assessments, including liquefied natural gas terminals and mining proposals.</p><p>But the immediate impact will be on two contentious pipeline projects that are currently dominating headlines: Kinder Morgan\'s proposal to twin the Trans Mountain oil pipeline in B.C. and TransCanada\'s Energy East project from Alberta to New Brunswick.</p><p>NEB hearings on the Trans Mountain project are almost over and the government is extending the deadline for a decision by four months, pushing it to next December.</p><p>As for Energy East, the government is extending the project review period by an extra six months and adding three temporary members to the National Energy Board in an effort to do greater public consultation. A decision on the pipeline is not expected before the middle of 2018 under the new timeline.</p><p>TransCanada spokesman Mark Cooper said the company needs time to digest the new rules.</p><p>"We support a strong and clear regulatory framework that helps Canadians see our commitment to building and operating oil and gas pipelines in the safest and most environmentally sound way possible," he said in an emailed statement.</p><p>"TransCanada operates in a highly regulated industry. We will continue to work with all levels of government and our regulators to ensure the continued safe and environmentally sound transportation of our natural resources to market."</p><p>Tim McMillan of the Canadian Association of Petroleum Producers said the industry organization welcomes the Liberal move to clarify the transition rules, but remains concerned about further delays and duplication built into the review process.</p><p>"Getting our product to market in a timely fashion is and has been a challenge," said McMillan.</p><p>Environmental groups, which have said Canada can approve no new pipelines if it is serious about meeting its international climate change commitments, applauded Wednesday\'s changes \u2014 even though the Liberals insist they\'re committed to getting Alberta and Saskatchewan crude to tidewater for export.</p><p>Conservative MPs were not impressed.</p><p>"This is not good news for the natural resources sector," said Conservative energy critic Candice Bergen, adding it sends a discouraging signal to investors. "This another layer, another process."</p><p>In Edmonton, Alberta Environment Minister Shannon Phillips said the province is behind any process that improves public confidence and bolsters the chances of pipelines getting built.</p><p>"Of course we are not interested in unnecessary delays, but the fact of the matter is that we have inherited a (review process) situation that didn\'t have the confidence of Canadians," Phillips said.</p><p>Alberta has already taken steps to reduce greenhouse gas emissions with a new climate strategy introduced last fall, she added. That process will cap oilsands emissions, phase out coal-fired electricity generation, and impose a broad-based carbon tax.</p><p>"We have done our part to earn that confidence of Canadians," said Phillips.</p><p>"Now the federal government is, (through) this interim solution, attempting to have that honest conversation and ensure that voices are heard so that we can get to a decision on these matters."</p><p>\u2014 Follow @BCheadle on Twitter</p><p>Note to readers: This is a corrected story. {u'full_article': u'<p>Guess who\'s going to dinner?</p>&#13;<p>In an unusual twist on Canada-U.S. relations, which for years have failed to live up to the stereotype of the world\'s two supposedly closest allies, a departing president with nothing to gain is having a rookie Canadian prime minister over for an honour that\'s become rare for us, a White House state dinner.</p>&#13;<p>U.S. President Barack Obama will roll out the red carpet for Prime Minister Justin Trudeau on March 10, evidently a hastily-arranged timeline for a function that the White House, on its website, says normally takes six months to prepare.</p>&#13;<p>South of the border, tongues have been wagging about which Canadian celebrities -- the U.S. does recognize some -- might be included on the guest list along with Trudeau, no stranger to celebrity himself despite his short months in office.</p>&#13;<p>North of the border, however, the attention paid to the upcoming gala has been more sober, noting, for example, that it\'s been 19 years since a Canadian prime minister was last feted at the White House.</p>&#13;<p>Indeed, it\'s been so long since a glass was hoisted to a PM at 1600\xa0Pennsylvania Ave., that one guest bound to be there -- Hillary Clinton, the presumptive Democratic nominee for the fall presidential race -- was the last First Lady to do so, when she and then-president Bill Clinton welcomed Jean Chretien in 1997.</p>&#13;<p>Cut through the polite dinner talk, and that should be worriesome to Canadians.</p>&#13;<p>State dinners are more than just a meal; they\'re an instrument of diplomacy and power. They have been since 1874, when Ulysses S. Grant held the first foreign state dinner for the king of Hawaii, a sovereign oceanic state the U.S. was already engulfing. Only those who matter to the Americans get invited.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau, at this stage, is more selfie than statesman, so it\'s not his diplomatic skills Obama wants. More likely, it\'s one liberal leader wanting to introduce another to those he hopes will succeed him.</p>&#13;<p>Still, there\'s no shortage of issues that need attention between Ottawa and Washington: a fragile economy, global trade, the energy sector, the war against ISIS, among them.</p>&#13;<p>On many files, our interests don\'t intersect, a point underlined this week as Canadian energy giant TransCanada filed a lawsuit against the Obama administration for rejecting its proposed Keystone XL oilsands pipeline to the Gulf Coast.</p>&#13;<p>Make no mistake, this should be a long overdue working meal.</p>&#13;<p>Bon appetit.</p>&#13;<p>-- Postmedia Network</p><p class="clearfix ads-container"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.thewhig.com/opinion/editorial/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.thewhig.com/2016/01/07/state-dinner-chatter-should-be-interesting', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.thewhig.com/2016/01/07/state-dinner-chatter-should-be-interesting', u'scraped_on': 1453835887, u'published_date': u'Thu, 07 Jan 2016 19:08:35 EST', u'title': u'State dinner chatter should be interesting', u'_id': ObjectId('56a7c6741d41c806b87b4c19'), u'media_url': u'http://www.thewhig.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p> BROMONT \u2013 TransCanada devra payer des milliards de dollars en redevances au Qu\xe9bec pour construire son ol\xe9oduc, s\u2019il n\u2019en tient qu\u2019\xe0 Fran\xe7ois Legault.</p><p> \xabSi l\u2019Alberta fait passer des milliards de dollars de baril de p\xe9trole pour l\u2019exportation, je pense que ce serait normal que le Qu\xe9bec obtienne des milliards de dollars de redevance. Plusieurs politiciens de l\u2019ouest du pays ont d\xe9nonc\xe9 le double discours du Qu\xe9bec, qui re\xe7oit de la p\xe9r\xe9quation, mais l\xe8ve le nez sur le p\xe9trole canadien. Le premier ministre de la Saskatchewan Brad Wall a demand\xe9 aux maires de \xabpoliment retourner leur part des 10 milliards en p\xe9r\xe9quation\xbb.</p><p> Le chef caquiste et son \xe9quipe ne condamnent pas ces propos. Legault.</p><p> Il demande maintenant au premier ministre Couillard \xabd\u2019agir\xbb et de trouver une solution puisque ce dossier est \xabtr\xe8s important pour l\u2019Ouest canadien\xbb. {u'full_article': u'&#13;<p id="fb-root"/><p class="p1">Abitibi Bowater is at it again.</p><p class="p3">In 2010 the company squeezed $130 million out of Canadian taxpayers after the Government of Newfoundland and Labrador expropriated Abitibi\u2019s hydroelectric\xa0assets\xa0in Grand Falls-Windsor and took back water and timber rights.</p><p class="p3">The company threatened to sue Canada under Chapter 11 of the North American Free Trade Agreement (NAFTA), prompting the Harper administration to settle out of court.</p><p class="p3">This all went down after Abitibi chose to pull out of the province and used the excuse of imminent bankruptcy to give minimum compensation to workers and pensioners, while paying nothing toward cleaning up the environmental damage it had caused.\xa0</p><p class="p3">The company never did go out of business; after emerging from bankruptcy proceedings under a new name, Resolute Forest Products, it was business as usual on the mainland. \xa0</p><p class="p3">This time the company closed down a mill in Shawinigan, Que., and is again suing under NAFTA because, it claims, a competing mill in Port Hawkesbury, N.S. was given an unfair trade advantage when it was subsidized by the Government of Nova Scotia.</p><p class="p3">The Port Hawkesbury mill closed in 2011 but was purchased by Pacific West Commercial for $33 million and reopened one year later with the guarantee of provincial government aid of approximately $12 million a year over a 10-year period. Today more than 1,000 people in Cape Breton are employed by Port Hawkesbury Paper. \xa0</p><p class="p3">In addition to claiming direct losses of around $70 million, Resolute Forest Products is seeking unspecified consequential damages that could ultimately mean much higher compensation.</p>What\u2019s wrong with this lawsuit?<p class="p3">At first glance, this lawsuit might appear reasonable to some. That is, until one considers two important points.</p><p class="p4">First, the original argument for including an investor-state dispute settlement mechanism in NAFTA was to prevent governments from discriminating against foreign corporations in favour of local businesses. There is a loophole in NAFTA, however, which allows Canadian corporations to register in the United States and then claim they are American for the purpose of suing Canada. This can be done\xa0as a mail box company in the state of Delaware, for instance.</p><p class="p3">Resolute Forest Products\u2019 head office is in Montreal and, according to the company\u2019s website, the \u201cvast majority\u201d of the forests where it harvests trees are in Canada. Doesn\u2019t this\xa0suggest that Resolute\u2014like Calgary-based Lone Pine Resources, which is currently suing Canada for $118.9 million USD as a result of\xa0Quebec\u2019s decision to ban fracking in the interest of protecting the\xa0natural environment\u2014is really a Canadian company in American disguise?\xa0</p><p class="p3">Secondly, the Resolute Forest Products lawsuit could be a \u2018back door\u2019 challenge to the whole idea of subsidies. This is a new, and very significant, twist because trade agreements generally don\u2019t attempt to eliminate government\u2019s right to use subsidies to stimulate local economies. Even wildly pro-free trade governments haven\u2019t argued for that.</p><p class="p3">For example, the Comprehensive Economic\xa0and Trade Agreement (CETA) did not in any way address the huge subsidies European countries give to their fishing industries. This is why Newfoundland and Labrador\u2019s acquiescence to the elimination of the its minimum processing requirements (MPRs) that former governments had designed to protect local communities and local workers was very shortsighted, even with the $280 million MPR federal compensation fund. \xa0</p><p class="p3">What the Resolute Forest Products lawsuit suggests is that we now have very limited options when it comes to leveling the very uneven playing field that exists in the international fisheries. We can apparently forget the concept of provincial subsidies or bailouts to help local producers and local jobs, as they can be challenged by industry players in other provinces who happen to have European affiliates or have registered in the United States.</p><p class="p3">The Resolute Forest Product lawsuit shows how trade agreements can generate far-reaching consequences that governments never anticipated. Canada, in particular, has not been very smart in its pursuit of trade agreements. Just our NAFTA lawsuits alone have gained us the dubious distinction of being the most sued developed country in the world. The United States, by contrast, has yet to lose a NAFTA lawsuit.</p>Nova Scotia\u2019s other NAFTA lawsuit<p class="p3">This is the second time in a year that Nova Scotia has been implicated in a NAFTA lawsuit. In 2015, the Canadian government was successfully sued by Bilcon, a Delaware registered company, for denying the company\u2019s application to open a quarry in Nova Scotia. </p><p class="p3">A federal-provincial review panel had rejected the quarry on environmental grounds, but the arbitration panel decided that the company had been treated unfairly and ruled in its favour. Compensation could climb as high as $300 million even though the project never reached the construction stage. </p><p class="p3">One of the arbitrators in the case admitted the decision was a \u201cremarkable step backwards in environmental protection\u201d\xa0in Canada.</p>TransCanada\u2019s $15 billion lawsuit against the U.S.<p class="p2">TransCanada Corp. has just launched a $15 billion NAFTA claim for damages because of the U.S. decision (based largely on environmental concerns) to deny a permit for the controversial Keystone XL pipeline. The pipeline itself would apparently cost somewhere in the vicinity of $3 billion. The extra $12 billion is for the projected loss of future profits.\xa0</p><p class="p3">Given that Canada has its own pipeline issues, ones hopes the newly elected federal government is taking note. Don\u2019t count on it, though \u2014 Justin Trudeau\u2019s enthusiastic pursuit of another free trade deal with China suggests the new prime minister is as keen on signing trade agreements as his predecessor was.</p>Ideology trumps reality<p class="p3">The Canadian government\u2019s ideological belief\xa0that all trade must be good appears unshakeable \u2014 so unshakeable that politicians have continually refused to discuss the negative impact these agreements might have both on the economy and on democratic decision-making. \xa0\xa0</p><p class="p5">In particular, there\u2019s been no talk in government about the accelerating costs of investor-state litigation, other than the former Harper government\u2019s threat to reclaim losses\xa0from those provinces foolish enough to incur a lawsuit.\xa0</p><p class="wp-caption-text">Abitibi Bowater\u2019s hydro assets and timber and water rights for its mill on the Exploits River in Grand Falls-Windsor were taken away by the provincial government in 2008.\xa0Photo by Graham Kennedy.</p><p class="p5">There\u2019s been no talk about the chilling effect the potential for lawsuits might have on the traditional use of subsidies (in the manner of the Port Hawkesbury example) or \u2018buy local\u2019 policies (prohibited under CETA) to stimulate struggling local economies.\xa0</p><p class="p5">And there is no acknowledgement of the continuing damage investor-state lawsuits are doing to environmental protection. Apparently government doesn\u2019t think it important that Canada has already been sued five times under NAFTA\xa0for trying to protect our environment.\xa0</p><p class="p5">Why is there an unwillingness to confront these consequences?</p><p class="p3">Trade agreements are going to bring us an ever-accelerating barrage of lawsuits that will straitjacket government of our own affairs. There can be no doubt of that. With financial institutions offering to back challenges for a percentage of the profits, investor-state lawsuits are just too tempting an opportunity to be passed up by corporations looking for a way to make easy money.\xa0</p><p class="p3">The eventual cumulative effect of these lawsuits on Canadian sovereignty will be profound. Democracy is being eviscerated, not through a frontal attack by roaring lions, but by corporate termites nibbling away at our traditional rights.\xa0</p><p class="p3">Marilyn Reid is a member of the Council of Canadians and Citizens Against CETA.</p>&#13;<p class="big-text title">Like this article? Help us publish more like it.</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="box-break"/>&#13;&#13;<p class="box-header-text">SPIN A YARN</p>&#13;<p class="help-box-text">&#13;Help spread important ideas and perspectives throughout N.L. and beyond.&#13;</p>&#13;<p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="box-break"/>&#13;&#13;<p class="box-header-text">GIVE US A FISH</p>&#13;<p class="help-box-text">&#13;As a non-profit media outlet, one-time donations are also a big help.&#13;</p>&#13;Donate&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="box-break"/>&#13;&#13;<p class="box-header-text">TEACH US TO FISH</p>&#13;<p class="help-box-text">&#13;Contribute what you can each month to sustain The Independent.&#13;</p>&#13;Join&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;', u'rss_url': u'http://theindependent.ca/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://theindependent.ca/2016/01/14/new-nafta-lawsuits-reveal-disturbing-dangerous-trend/', u'author': u'Marilyn Reid', u'original_article_url': u'http://theindependent.ca/2016/01/14/new-nafta-lawsuits-reveal-disturbing-dangerous-trend/', u'scraped_on': 1453840991, u'published_date': u'Thu, 14 Jan 2016 13:02:21 +0000', u'title': u'New NAFTA lawsuits reveal disturbing, dangerous trend', u'_id': ObjectId('56a7da5f1d41c80b0df1a1f1'), u'media_url': u'http://theindependent.ca/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre wants TransCanada to "redo its homework" on the proposed Energy East pipeline before he gives his stamp of approval, after discussing the project with Prime Minister Justin Trudeau.</p><p>The energy company is seeking to overhaul an existing natural gas pipeline and build new sections to create a 4,600-kilometre stretch that would deliver crude oil from Alberta\'s oilsands, through Montreal, to a refinery in New Brunswick.</p><p>The project aims to bring Alberta\'s oil to international markets, and would ship 1.1 million barrels of crude oil across the country a day. Alberta, which lost 19,000 jobs last year, hopes the project will boost its economy. New Brunswick is also depending on Energy East to bring in much-needed jobs and revenue.</p><p>In an appearance on CTV\'s Power Play Tuesday, Coderre said the project was "bad" from Day 1, and TransCanada has presented a poorly prepared plan.</p><p>"The heat should be on TransCanada to do their homework and be available to be part of the plan," said Coderre.</p><p>"There are many, many questions and I\'m responsible for my people."</p><p>But of late, the heat has been on Coderre to accept the pipeline.</p><p>The Montreal mayor, as well as some of his counterparts from across the province, came out last week against the project, saying concerns over environmental risks outweigh potential economic benefits.</p><p>"We have to put up a responsible, balance approach between the economy and sustainable development," Coderre said Tuesday.</p><p>"There\'s no plan B to the planet."</p><p>The announcement brought tensions to a head between Coderre and western Canadian politicians, including Saskatchewan Premier Brad Wall, Alberta Premier Rachel Notely and Calgary Mayor Naheed Nenshi.</p><p>Notely called Coderre\'s move "short-sighted," but others, such as Wall, had harsher words.</p><p>"I trust Montreal-area mayors will politely return their share of $10B in equalization supported by West," Wall tweeted.</p><p>The war of words led Trudeau to sit down with Coderre on Tuesday to discuss the issue.</p><p>"My responsibility as prime minister is to make sure that on national projects we\'re behaving in a way that contributes to the economy, to a secure environment, to bringing people together and mostly to creating a better future for future generations," said Trudeau.</p><p>Trudeau added that Ottawa will establish a review process to provide energy companies a chance to prove to municipalities that a project is in its best interest.</p><p>"The responsibility of the federal government is to establish a clear process whereby people can evaluate the projects in a rigorous and open manner," he said.</p><p>The review will also include requirements forcing energy companies to account for all greenhouse gas emissions associated with the project.</p><p>Coderre said that he has been asking Trudeau for environmental assessments on potential pipeline spills.</p><p>He said that his main concern is that the TransCanada will endanger local environments as will require 100 km of pipeline to pass through wetlands, forest and agricultural lands.</p><p>"We\'ve got to be really careful to protect our water," said Coderre.</p><p>Coderre also defended his stance by saying that he is not anti-pipeline, pointing to the city\'s eventual approval of Enbridge\'s Line 9 pipeline reversal.</p><p>"I\'m not a dogmatic. As a matter of fact, we\'ve already said yes to pipelines from Enbridge, but there are some people that have to do their job and their homework," said Coderre.</p><p>Coderre said that TransCanada has done little to allay his fears about the prevention of a spill and the potential follow-up strategy.</p><p>"It is not a blank cheque saying that, \'OK we\'re going Sera, Sera,\'" said Coderre.</p><p>"We feel for everybody but we have to do it a proper way."</p><p>Coderre also criticized the company for not participating in Montreal-area consultations on the project.</p><p>"You cannot just come like this and don\'t play in an inclusive way," he said.</p><p>Meanwhile, Energy East\'s vice-president for New Brunswick and Quebec, Louis Bergeron, told The Canadian Press on Tuesday that the company plans to keep working with all the parties involved with the potential pipeline and he hopes to meet with Coderre.</p><p>Bergeron added that the company is hoping to change its approach to the project.</p><p>"I think with my background and the experience I got building one pipeline in the province of Quebec in the last few years, I know what it takes to succeed," he said. "I am right now completing my management team in Montreal and the No. 1 criteria to work for me is to be a respectful person."</p><p>Bergeron fronted the development of the 240-kilometre Pipeline Saint-Laurent, which connects Ultramar\'s refinery near Quebec City with its Montreal petroleum product distribution centre.</p><p>With files from The Canadian Press</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://edmonton.ctvnews.ca/rss/ctv-news-edmonton-1.822347', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.ctvnews.ca/politics/transcanada-needs-to-redo-its-homework-on-energy-east-coderre-1.2751955', u'author': u'Michael Shulman', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.ctvnews.ca/politics/transcanada-needs-to-redo-its-homework-on-energy-east-coderre-1.2751955', u'scraped_on': 1453857442, u'published_date': u'Tue, 26 Jan 2016 03:24:00 -0700', u'title': u"TransCanada needs to 'redo its homework' on Energy East: Coderre", u'_id': ObjectId('56a81aac1d41c8186fc804c1'), u'media_url': u'http://edmonton.ctvnews.ca/'}

{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>The mayors of Edmonton and Calgary are standing together on Canada\u2019s controversial pipeline issue.</p>&#13;<p>Both mayors said politics have to be taken out of the issue if the country is to move forward.</p>&#13;<p>Controversy erupted in the last week after Montreal-area municipalities, led by Mayor Denis Coderre, came out in strong opposition to the Energy East pipeline proposed to ship Alberta oilsands crude to refineries in New Brunswick.</p>&#13;<p>On Tuesday, Coderre met with Prime Minister Justin Trudeau and appeared to soften his stance somewhat, hinting he would be prepared to rethink Calgary-based TransCanada Corp.\u2019s project if the company was more forthcoming with details and prepared to address environmental concerns.</p>&#13;<p>Speaking to reporters at the legislature after he and Calgary mayor Naheed Nenshi met with the NDP cabinet, Edmonton Mayor Don Iveson said local politicians have to think about what\u2019s best for the country.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cJust imagine if we were trying to build a railroad 100 years ago and mayors were saying no. But politicizing this is not good for the country.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>Nenshi acknowledged that Coderre\u2019s job is to raise concerns and \u201cget the best possible deal that he can get.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>\u201cBut I think what\u2019s really important here, and what I appreciated the Prime Minister saying today, is that there is a process in place. It\u2019s a rigorous science-based process,\u201d he said.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cThe National Energy Board knows what it\u2019s doing and certainly the Liberal government wants to enhance that process, which is fine, as long as it doesn\u2019t result in unnecessary delay. But we should abide by the process.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau has promised his Liberal government will overhaul the regulatory review for pipeline projects under the NEB.</p>&#13;<p>The prime minister said his government will create a review process whereby energy companies get a chance to prove to cities and towns across the country that a project is in their best interests.</p>&#13;<p>With files from Jodie Sinnema, Edmonton Journal and The Canadian Press\xa0</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.ottawasun.com/news/canada/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.ottawasun.com/2016/01/26/nenshi-iveson-politicizing-energy-east-not-good-for-the-country', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.ottawasun.com/2016/01/26/nenshi-iveson-politicizing-energy-east-not-good-for-the-country', u'scraped_on': 1453858499, u'published_date': u'Tue, 26 Jan 2016 18:37:35 EST', u'title': u"Nenshi, Iveson: Politicizing Energy East 'not good for the country'", u'_id': ObjectId('56a81ec71d41c8192231d12c'), u'media_url': u'http://www.ottawasun.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>A handful of anti-pipeline activists with lock cutters and the will to get arrested have become Canadian oil producers\u2019 newest hurdle to delivering crude to markets.</p><p>Enbridge Inc. said it was forced to shut its Line 9 from Sarnia, Ont., to Montreal for about four hours after the company\u2019s control centre detected that a valve had been tampered with and closed late on Sunday. It was the fourth time in two months that an Enbridge line was shut after a such incident. While deliveries weren\u2019t affected by the shutdowns, the company plans to enact permanent security measures at valve sites, Graham White, a company spokesman, said, declining to disclose the costs. \u201cThey have used lock cutters every time,\u201d Mr. White said. \u201cIn the latest three incidents, they destroyed locks or fences, sabotaged the equipment and departed without staging a protest.\u201d</p><p>Saboteurs represent the latest hassle Western Canada faces in getting its crude to coastal markets as plans to build new pipelines are stymied by environmental and legal challenges. Pipeline development in Canada has been under increasing public scrutiny and has become a political football for local, provincial and federal leaders. Last week, Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre, along with about 80 other Quebec mayors, announced his opposition to TransCanada Corp.\u2019s plans to build the Energy East line to Saint John on the Atlantic coast.</p><p>Tampering with pipelines is the latest form of opposition to moving crude oil, said Steven Paget, an analyst at FirstEnergy Capital Corp. in Calgary. \u201cIt doesn\u2019t seem that they thought they\u2019d need to have armed guards at every pump station.\u201d</p><p>Enbridge\u2019s Line 9 provides eastern Canadian refineries with western Canadian and U.S. crude. The line went into operation last year after its direction was reversed amid opposition from local communities. In the latest incident, the Enbridge control centre detected a valve closing at 11:15 p.m. and the line was shut while it was inspected, Mr. White said. Unlike in the first two incidents, the company received no calls in advance of the action.</p><p>On Dec. 7, three protesters manually shut a valve and chained themselves to the equipment at a fenced-in site along Line 9 about 80 kilometres west of Montreal. They were removed by police. Ten days later, the line was shut again by protesters. On Jan. 4, the company\u2019s Line 7 was shut for about three hours after flow was partly restricted by protesters near Cambridge, Ont.</p><p>\u201cThe People versus Enbridge line 9\u201d group page on Facebook posted a picture of a protester breaking a chain with a lock cutter last month.</p><p>\u201cWe are taking additional permanent security measures at valve sites to help discourage such activities,\u201d Mr. White said in an e-mail. \u201cWe would not discuss details publicly since that would risk reducing the effectiveness of those measures.\u201d</p><p>Oil sands producers including Suncor Energy Inc. and Canadian Natural Resources Ltd. have clamoured for years for new pipelines to increase access to world markets, in a bid to improve the price they receive for crude. Heavy Western Canadian select is among the world\u2019s cheapest grades, selling for less than $15 (U.S.) a barrel last week.</p><p>Last year, U.S. President Barack Obama rejected TransCanada\u2019s planned Keystone XL line to the U.S. Gulf Coast. Kinder Morgan Inc. is set to face new environmental hurdles to expanding its Trans Mountain line from Alberta to tidewater at Burnaby, B.C.</p><p>New security measures to protect pipelines might include drones or cameras, Mr. Paget said. \u201cThe alternative is terrifying,\u201d he said. \u201cTurning a valve on a pipeline is like jamming the brake on a train. It poses a very high risk.\u201d</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/?service=rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/report-on-business/industry-news/energy-and-resources/pipeline-saboteurs-becoming-oil-patchs-newest-foes/article28403596/', u'author': u'ROBERT TUTTLE and JEREMY VAN LOON', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/report-on-business/industry-news/energy-and-resources/pipeline-saboteurs-becoming-oil-patchs-newest-foes/article28403596/?cmpid=rss1', u'scraped_on': 1453858042, u'published_date': u'Tue, 26 Jan 2016 17:45:27 -0500', u'title': u'Pipeline saboteurs becoming oil patch\u2019s newest foes', u'_id': ObjectId('56a81d021d41c8186fc81b14'), u'media_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>The mayors of Edmonton and Calgary are standing together on Canada\u2019s controversial pipeline issue.</p>&#13;<p>Both mayors said politics have to be taken out of the issue if the country is to move forward.</p>&#13;<p>Controversy erupted in the last week after Montreal-area municipalities, led by Mayor Denis Coderre, came out in strong opposition to the Energy East pipeline proposed to ship Alberta oilsands crude to refineries in New Brunswick.</p>&#13;<p>On Tuesday, Coderre met with Prime Minister Justin Trudeau and appeared to soften his stance somewhat, hinting he would be prepared to rethink Calgary-based TransCanada Corp.\u2019s project if the company was more forthcoming with details and prepared to address environmental concerns.</p>&#13;<p>Speaking to reporters at the legislature after he and Calgary mayor Naheed Nenshi met with the NDP cabinet, Edmonton Mayor Don Iveson said local politicians have to think about what\u2019s best for the country.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cJust imagine if we were trying to build a railroad 100 years ago and mayors were saying no. What kind of country would we have?\u201d said Iveson.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cI think it\u2019s incumbent on all of us to try to work toward yes. Bring the concerns forward, have them addressed duly through the regulatory process. But politicizing this is not good for the country.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>Nenshi acknowledged that Coderre\u2019s job is to raise concerns and \u201cget the best possible deal that he can get.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>\u201cBut I think what\u2019s really important here, and what I appreciated the Prime Minister saying today, is that there is a process in place. But we should abide by the process.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau has promised his Liberal government will overhaul the regulatory review for pipeline projects under the NEB.</p>&#13;<p>The prime minister said his government will create a review process whereby energy companies get a chance to prove to cities and towns across the country that a project is in their best interests.</p>&#13;<p>With files from Jodie Sinnema, Edmonton Journal and The Canadian Press\xa0</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/news/alberta/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/2016/01/26/nenshi-iveson-politicizing-energy-east-not-good-for-the-country', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/2016/01/26/nenshi-iveson-politicizing-energy-east-not-good-for-the-country', u'scraped_on': 1453859300, u'published_date': u'Tue, 26 Jan 2016 16:37:35 MST', u'title': u"Nenshi, Iveson: Politicizing Energy East 'not good for the country'", u'_id': ObjectId('56a821e41d41c819e9983afd'), u'media_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>OTTAWA -- Stephen Harper once described the Keystone XL pipeline project as a "no-brainer."</p>&#13;<p>But U.S. President Barack Obama thought otherwise and, after Harper left office, spiked the project that would have taken Western Canadian crude to tidewater in Texas.</p>&#13;<p>Without Keystone, the next best option to get that crude to new markets and bring more wealth to the entire country is the Energy East project, a monster-sized $15-billion proposal from Transcanada Pipelines to take that oil all the way to Saint John and an Atlantic port.</p>&#13;<p>It may also be a "no-brainer" to its supporters but Harper\'s successor, Prime Minister Justin Trudeau, refuses to engage in anything remotely resembling what Liberals describe as "cheerleading."</p>&#13;<p>Because of that, Trudeau is being called out by his political opponents for not showing nearly enough ardour for the oil business and Western Canada.</p>&#13;<p>Remarks like the kind he made last week in Davos about Canada being about resourcefulness not necessarily resources gave his critics some ammunition on this file.</p>&#13;<p>And yet, while Trudeau is officially neutral on any pipeline project pending regulatory reviews, there are now enough signs to conclude that, if TransCanada does its bit and satisfies soon-to-be toughened environmental and safety regulations, the government appears ready to help create what Trudeau\'s inner circle calls the "social licence" without which pipeline projects will not succeed.</p>&#13;<p>The first step is to quickly fix serious deficiencies at the National Energy Board (NEB) identified Wednesday by the federal environment commissioner, an auditor of Parliament.</p>&#13;<p>Commissioner Julie Gelfand found that the NEB is not doing adequate checks and follow-ups to make sure that the 100 or so companies that operate 73,000 kilometres of oil and gas pipelines in Canada are upholding their regulatory and safety commitments.</p>&#13;<p>If Canadians have no trust in the pipeline regulator, there will be little trust in any process to build new pipelines. There is every indication that the Trudeau government agrees and will quickly fix the deficiencies at the NEB.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau will also have to spend some political capital, and he did a bit of that Wednesday with an early morning meeting in Montreal with former Liberal MP Denis Coderre. Coderre is now Montreal\'s mayor and, for much of last week, the unapologetic champion of opponents of Energy East.</p>&#13;<p>But after 45 minutes with Trudeau, Coderre\'s opposition appeared to have softened.</p>&#13;<p>"At the end of the day, it is all about being respected," Coderre said by way of explanation for the fight he picked with some Western Canadian leaders.</p>&#13;<p>A door that appeared firmly shut last week could open again. And it was Trudeau that created that opportunity.</p>&#13;<p>Ontario Premier Kathleen Wynne -- a key Trudeau ally -- also sent a signal of hope for Energy East boosters when she stood next to Alberta Premier Rachel Notley at a press conference in Toronto and lent her cautious, qualified support for the pipeline project.</p>&#13;<p>Finally, one need only look at how Liberals in the House of Commons are defusing attacks from the Conservatives on pipeline politics.</p>&#13;<p>Natural Resources Minister Gary Carr turned away criticisms from Tory MPs by noting that, after a decade of Stephen Harper, no pipeline to tidewater was approved let alone built.</p>&#13;<p>The clear implication in Carr\'s criticism is that the Trudeau government\'s different way of doing things will produce results. Where the Conservatives failed, the Liberals are suggesting they\'ll succeed in seeing the construction of a pipeline.</p>&#13;<p>\xa0</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/news/canada/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/2016/01/26/trudeau-liberals-quietly-set-stage-for-energy-east-success', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/2016/01/26/trudeau-liberals-quietly-set-stage-for-energy-east-success', u'scraped_on': 1453866690, u'published_date': u'Tue, 26 Jan 2016 21:34:27 CST', u'title': u'Trudeau Liberals quietly set stage for Energy East success', u'_id': ObjectId('56a83ec31d41c86d67833c62'), u'media_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>Environment Commissioner Julie Gelfand speaks with the media following the release of the 2015 Fall Reports of the Commissioner of the Environment and Sustainable Development in Ottawa on Tuesday Jan. 26, 2016.Adrian Wyld / THE CANADIAN PRESS</p><p>The \u201cHarper era\u201d for the National Energy Board \u2014 with so much focus on speeding\xa0up the\xa0regulatory review process for new oil pipelines \u2014 is quickly coming to an end in Prime Minister Justin Trudeau\u2019s Canada.</p><p>The idea of \u201cone project, one review\u201d conducted\xa0by the NEB within a two-year\xa0window\xa0was a key\xa0element\xa0of the omnibus Bill C-38 unveiled by then prime minister Stephen Harper\u2019s Conservatives in 2012 to eliminate duplication and inefficiency and deter\xa0use of the regulatory process to delay major energy\xa0developments.</p><p>It also gave cabinet the right to overrule the\xa0NEB if it rejected an application.\xa0</p><p>Harper\u2019s pipeline-backing government made it\xa0perfectly\xa0clear that \u201cstrong economic growth\u201d was the primary outcome Ottawa desired\xa0from the changes to\xa0energy regulation.</p><p>Almost four years later, the three big projects proposed to move crude for Alberta\u2019s oilsands to tidewater ports and on to global\xa0markets \u2014 Enbridge\u2019s Northern Gateway, Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain expansion and TransCanada\u2019s Energy East \u2014 are each\xa0mired in delays or controversy and remain\xa0closer to the drawing board than putting pipe in the ground.</p><p>Now concerns are\xa0emerging from Trudeau\u2019s government over the NEB\u2019s mandate and its ability to carry it\xa0out.</p><p>A report by the federal commissioner of the environment and sustainable development\xa0tabled Tuesday in the House of Commons warned the federal regulator\xa0needs to keep better track of pipeline projects just as Ottawa plans to add the impact of greenhouse gas emissions to the criteria for the NEB to determine if a project is in the public interest.</p><p>It said\xa0the NEB hasn\u2019t\xa0done enough to ensure\xa0companies are complying with conditions set out when projects are approved. Northern Gateway, for example, had 209 conditions to be met before Enbridge can\xa0proceed.\xa0</p><p>The NEB conducted 950 compliance verification activities from\xa02012 to\xa02014 and\xa0found\xa0\u201cdeficiencies in company compliance\u201d in more than one-quarter of them, the report said.\xa0The commissioner\u2019s office examined 42 of those cases of \u201cdeficiencies\u201d and determined\xa0the NEB didn\u2019t properly follow up more than half of the time.</p><p>\u201cIt is clear the Board needs to do more to keep pace with the rapidly changing context in which it operates,\u201d commissioner Julie Gelfand said in Ottawa.</p><p>Gelfand made six recommendations for\xa0tracking compliance and emergency preparedness as well as attracting and retaining staff to do the work. The board said they reflected\xa0areas the NEB had recognized as needing improvement and expects to \u201ccomprehensively\u201d address them by the end of 2016. </p><p>Trudeau promised to overhaul the NEB \u2014 he accused\xa0Harper of turning the federal regulator\xa0from a referee to a\xa0cheerleader during\xa0last year year\u2019s\xa0election campaign \u2014 and a new federal pipeline safety act is expected by June.</p><p>The NEB dates to 1959 and relocated\xa0from Ottawa to Calgary in 1991.\xa0It\xa0regulates about 73,000\xa0kilometres of interprovincial and international pipelines.</p><p>If built, the $25 billion in proposed projects would nearly double Canada\u2019s pipeline capacity by 2020. NEB\xa0chairman Peter Watson has said the regulator is at a \u201ccritical juncture\u201d given the complexity of the issues and public scrutiny and he\u2019s\xa0been implementing a \u201cmodernization\u201d plan for more than a year.</p><p>Pipelines regulated by the NEB transported oil and gas valued at more than $162 billion in 2014 but industry has said a lack of capacity\xa0led to\xa0steep discounts on Canadian crude.</p><p>Watson previously said he doesn\u2019t \u201cquibble\u201d with the need for climate debate\xa0but maintained it is\xa0up to politicians to determine if that\u2019s part of the NEB\u2019s mandate. As for staffing concerns, Gelfand isn\u2019t alone in raising the issue. The\xa0Canadian Energy Pipeline Association told\xa0a Senate committee on pipeline safety the same thing last year although\xa0widespread oilpatch layoffs may resolve that issue. </p><p>It\u2019s evident tinkering with the NEB\u2019s mandate or its operations won\u2019t satisfy the critics and even major\xa0efforts\xa0to address\xa0climate issues have done\xa0little to quiet opponents. Alberta Premier Rachel Notley introduced a carbon tax and set\xa0a 100-megatonne cap\xa0on annual GHG emissions from the oilsands in November but it\u2019s done little\xa0to quell\xa0the debate.</p><p>Notley and Trudeau have offered support for pipelines in response to criticism this week of\xa0Energy East by Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre. Trudeau said Tuesday\xa0it\u2019s\xa0\u201cthe responsibility of the federal government is to establish a clear process whereby people can evaluate the projects in a rigorous and open manner.\u201d</p><p>The response from the U.S. environmental group 350.org to a\xa0climate test for pipelines was \u201cit\u2019s\xa0time\xa0Canadian politicians stopped squabbling over this pipeline or that pipeline and woke up to the 21st century.\u201d</p><p>Politicians\xa0can empower regulators or make\xa0polluters pay \u2014 and should for a number of reasons \u2014 but there\u2019s not going to be any quid pro quo\xa0from environmentalists for those\xa0efforts. As with\xa0Harper, they don\u2019t want a rigorous\xa0review\xa0of\xa0pipelines, they simply want their way at any cost.\xa0</p><p>Either way, Canadians pay the price.</p><p>Stephen Ewart is a Calgary Herald columnist</p><p>sewart@calgaryherald.com</p><p>twitter.com/stephen_ewart</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/business/energy/ewart-harper-era-neb-coming-to-an-abrupt-end-under-trudeau', u'author': u'Stephen Ewart, Calgary Herald', u'original_article_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/business/energy/ewart-harper-era-neb-coming-to-an-abrupt-end-under-trudeau', u'scraped_on': 1453896186, u'published_date': u'Wed, 27 Jan 2016 12:00:03 +0000', u'title': u'Ewart: Harper-era NEB coming to an abrupt end under Trudeau', u'_id': ObjectId('56a8b1fa1d41c8174b89d183'), u'media_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>The federal government is overhauling how it examines major energy projects to factor in greenhouse gas emissions to a greater extent, and is extending the deadlines for reviewing and deciding on the Energy East and Trans Mountain oil pipelines.</p>&#13;<p>Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr and Environment Minister Catherine McKenna announced new interim measures for reviewing energy projects while the government takes more time to craft a permanent environmental assessment regime for resource development that they hope will improve public confidence in the process. The government expects it will take a few years to craft a new, permanent regulatory review process.</p>&#13;<p>In the meantime, the new interim principles will apply to natural resource developments subject to federal environmental assessments, including pipelines, liquefied natural gas and mining projects.</p>&#13;<p>The ministers announced a specific set of measures for the</p>&#13;<p>Energy East pipeline and Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain pipeline, including assessing the upstream greenhouse gas emissions \u2013 that is, emissions from oil production \u2013 associated with the project and to make the information public.</p>&#13;<p>The government will also undertake \u201cdeeper consultations\u201d with Indigenous peoples potentially affected by the project and provide funding to support the consultations.</p>&#13;<p>For Energy East, the government plans to appoint the three temporary members to the regulator during the review, as well as expand public input into the NEB review process, including public and community engagement.</p>&#13;<p>The natural resources minister will seek a six-month extension of the NEB\u2019s legislated review time (to 21 months from 15 months) for Energy East as well as an extra three months for a final government decision (to six months from three months).</p>&#13;<p>That would mean the total review and decision timeframe would be 27 months, with a final decision from the federal government on the Energy East project expected in mid-2018.</p>&#13;<p>For Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain pipeline, the government will appoint a ministerial representative to consult communities, including Indigenous peoples potentially affected by the project.</p>&#13;<p>The government is also seeking a four-month extension of its time limit for making a decision on the Trans Mountain project, meaning it would now have until December 2016 to make a decision (instead of August 2016).</p>&#13;<p>While the government prepares a new, broader environmental assessment process, the following principles will apply to all natural resources projects currently under regulatory review:</p>&#13;<p>\u2013 Assessing all direct and upstream greenhouse gas emissions linked to projects under review;</p>&#13;<p>\u2013 No project proponent will be asked to return to the starting line;</p>&#13;<p>\u2013 Decisions will be based on science, \u201ctraditional knowledge of Indigenous peoples,\u201d and other relevant evidence;</p>&#13;<p>\u2013 The views of the public and affected communities will be sought and considered; and</p>&#13;<p>\u2013 Indigenous peoples will be \u201cmeaningfully consulted\u201d and, where appropriate, impacts on their rights and interests will be assessed.</p>&#13;<p>TransCanada\u2019s Energy East project and Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain pipeline expansion are currently under review by the National Energy Board.</p>&#13;<p>The Pacific NorthWest LNG project located near Prince Rupert, B.C. is currently being reviewed by the Canadian Environmental Assessment Agency, as is the Woodfibre LNG project near Squamish, B.C.</p>&#13;<p>McKenna is hosting meetings with provincial and territorial environment ministers in Ottawa starting Thursday, which will lay the groundwork for a first ministers\u2019 meeting in early March to establish a new national climate-change plan.</p>&#13;<p>The new rules announced Wednesday came a day after the federal environment commissioner said the National Energy Board is doing an \u201cinadequate\u201d job tracking the petroleum sector\u2019s compliance with pipeline approval conditions, and that there have been significant problems in the regulator\u2019s reviews of companies\u2019 emergency procedures.</p>&#13;<p>Debate over Energy East has sparked an East-West conflict after Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre, a former Liberal MP, and 81 other mayors from surrounding municipalities came out last week against the Energy East pipeline before NEB hearings on the project have even begun.</p>&#13;<p>Their opposition to the project spurred criticism from Saskatchewan Premier Brad Wall, Calgary Mayor Naheed Nenshi and Edmonton Mayor Don Iveson, who have said the project is in the best interests of the country.</p>&#13;<p>Conservative Leader Rona Ambrose has warned that opposition to Energy East in Quebec and other parts of Canada is a threat to national unity and is reminding many westerners of the once-loathed National Energy Program from Pierre Trudeau\u2019s Liberal government.</p>&#13;<p>TransCanada\u2019s 4,600-kilometre Energy East pipeline would transport 1.1 million barrels of oil a day from Alberta and Saskatchewan to refineries in Quebec (one in Montreal, one near Quebec City) and on to a deep-water marine terminal in Saint John, N.B.</p>&#13;<p>Kinder Morgan\u2019s expansion plans would nearly triple the capacity of its current Trans Mountain pipeline to 890,000 barrels of oil a day from the 300,000 barrels currently shipped in the existing pipeline.</p>&#13;<p>The 1,150-kilometre twinned Trans Mountain pipeline would run from near Edmonton to Burnaby, B.C. Kinder Morgan hopes pipeline construction can start in 2016 and go into service in 2018.</p>&#13;<p>The proposed $11.4-billion Pacific NorthWest LNG facility and marine terminal would be located on Lelu Island near Prince Rupert and convert natural gas to LNG for export to Pacific Rim markets in Asia. Pacific NorthWest LNG is a consortium controlled by Malaysia\u2019s state-owned Petronas.</p>&#13;<p>The NEB review of the Trans Mountain project must be completed by May 20, 2016, after the hearing process was delayed over controversy.</p>&#13;<p>In August 2015, the NEB panel reviewing the Trans Mountain project said any evidence prepared by Steven Kelly, a consultant at the time who prepared information in favour of the project, must be removed from the regulatory application.</p>&#13;<p>Kelly had just been appointed to a seven-year term at the National Energy Board. The NEB panel said his dual role could raise concerns about the integrity of the review.</p>&#13;<p>The NEB\u2019s review process, initially scheduled to be completed by late January, was lengthened until May after Kelly\u2019s evidence was removed.</p>&#13;<p>Environmental and civil society groups have assailed the NEB review of the Trans Mountain expansion, with dozens of participants dropping out of a process they say is unfair and biased.</p>&#13;<p>NDP Leader Tom Mulcair said Wednesday the old environmental assessment rules need to be completely tossed out and that project reviews should start over, something he said the Liberals have promised to do.</p>&#13;<p>During a federal election campaign stop in B.C. in August, Trudeau said the Kinder Morgan review process \u201cneeds to be redone.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>Mulcair said that restarting the assessment process for projects already under review is even more necessary considering the environment commissioner\u2019s damning report Tuesday about the NEB\u2019s pipeline oversight.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cWhat Mr. Trudeau promised during the election campaign wasn\u2019t a little bit of a patchwork on top of the existing system,\u201d Mulcair said.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cWhat we learned (Tuesday) from the environment commissioner is that basis, that foundation, is completely defective. So you can\u2019t build solidly on a defective foundation. You have to start over again.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau and the Liberal government promised in their platform to introduce a new environmental assessment process for energy projects that has more teeth.</p>&#13;<p>They also pledged to \u201cmodernize the National Energy Board\u201d to ensure it better reflects regional views, has \u201csufficient expertise\u201d in areas like environmental science and community development, and considers input from aboriginal groups.</p>&#13;<p>jfekete@ottawacitzien.com</p>&#13;<p>Twitter.com/jasonfekete</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/news/canada/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/2016/01/27/pipeline-rule-overhaul-in-works', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/2016/01/27/pipeline-rule-overhaul-in-works', u'scraped_on': 1453944798, u'published_date': u'Wed, 27 Jan 2016 17:26:44 MST', u'title': u'Pipeline rule overhaul in works', u'_id': ObjectId('56a96fe01d41c841e1d9cfde'), u'media_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>There will be talking.</p><p class="story-ad"></p><p>In a joint press conference late Wednesday afternoon \u2014 after markets closed, thankyouverymuch \u2014 Environment and Climate Change Minister Catherine McKenna and Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr unveiled a moderately modified evaluation process for pipeline projects already being assessed.</p><p>READ MORE: Feds announce new rules for assessing oil\xa0pipelines</p><p>Chiefly, the changes affect Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain, the twinning of an existing pipeline from Alberta to British Columbia\u2019s Lower Mainland; and TransCanada\u2019s Energy East, which would carry bitumen emulsion from Alberta\u2019s oilsands to the east coast and, if it goes forward as proposed, would be the biggest pipeline project in Canada\u2019s history.</p><p>The feds want more time to assess these projects \u2014 six months, in Trans Mountain\u2019s case. Carr and McKenna promised more consultation and a more robust evaluation of the project\u2019s impacts on greenhouse gas emissions, although it isn\u2019t clear how much weight greenhouse gas emissions will have on final decisions.</p><p>READ MORE: NEB monitoring of pipeline safety is still \u2018inadequate\u2019</p><p>But Wednesday\u2019s announcement was chiefly an interim measure, designed as a placeholder while the Liberal government hashes out a new environmental assessment process.</p><p>\u201cRight now we\u2019re in a transition period, which makes it tough,\u201d McKenna said.</p><p>\u201cIt\u2019s not fair to proponents to make them start at the very beginning.\u201d</p><p>PIPELINE POLITICS: Oilsands and the election</p><p>WATCH: Natural Resources Minister talks about pipelines and public confidence</p><p>The federal Liberals\u2019 resource project plans have been a bit of a question mark since the election\xa0campaign.</p><p>At the time, Trudeau condemned the existing assessment process as illegitimate and failing its duty to consult with people affected and address their concerns.</p><p>\u201cIt is not for governments to be cheerleaders for various pipelines,\u201d Trudeau said in August\xa0when asked point-blank if he supports the Enbridge and Kinder Morgan\xa0pipelines.</p><p>\u201cThere is no longer a choice to be made between what\u2019s good for the economy and what\u2019s good for the environment,\u201d he said at the time, adding that his party\u2019s committed to \u201coverhauling the environmental assessment process to make sure \u2026 that Canadians\xa0worried about the scientific risks associated with specific projects are part of the solution and get their concerns heard and addressed.\u201d</p><p>WATCH: Trudeau on pipelines during the election campaign</p><p>But he and his party stopped short of specifying what their promise of new, beefed-up rules would mean for projects already in the regulatory pipeline.</p><p>\u201cWe are committed to restoring credibility to the environmental assessment process. Projects that are under review would be required to respond to this strengthened process,\u201d spokesperson Jean-Luc Ferland told Global News in an email Aug. 20.</p><p>Immediately after the election, new Burnaby North-Seymour MP Terry Beech told Burnaby Now\xa0the Trans Mountain pipeline project will have to start over.</p><p>\u201cKinder Morgan will have to go through a new, revised process,\u201d he said.</p><p>A month later, Environment Minister McKenna said the opposite: On her way to the Paris climate conference, she told reporters Trans Mountain and other projects already in the evaluation pipeline will be evaluated under the existing rules.</p><p>\u201cProjects initiated under the original system will continue on that path,\u201d she said.</p><p>This month, that changed again, when a spokesperson for Natural Resources Minister Carr told Bloomberg the government was about to unveil new, \u201ctransitional\u201d review hurdles for Trans Mountain and others.</p><p>Which brings us to Wednesday\u2019s announcement.</p><p>\u201cWe have an immediate situation that requires the immediate attention of the Government of Canada,\u201d Carr said.</p><p>\u201cWe are hopeful that Canadians will say, \u2018Well, it\u2019s better than we\u2019ve had; it\u2019s not perfect, but it\u2019s better than the lack of confidence we have to work with now. And it will be, probably, not quite as good as the permanent solution which will be the beneficiary of this new process.\u201d</p><p>But for all the emphasis on talk, engagement and consultation, Wednesday\u2019s announcement blindsided some of the very people whose concerns it was meant to assuage.</p><p>It was news to multiple lawyers representing B.C.\u2019s Tsleil-Waututh Nation, which has been in court as recently as last Friday arguing\xa0the feds failed in their constitutional duty to consult regarding the Trans Mountain process.</p><p>\u201cThat\u2019s somewhat disappointing,\u201d said Eugene Kung, a lawyer for the Tsleil-Waututh Sacred Trust Initiative.</p><p>\u201cIt certainly doesn\u2019t seem consistent with the rhetoric of consultation and starting a new relationship with First Nations.\u201d</p><p>It\u2019s especially awkward because Crown lawyers called for a judicial detente in court last week, allowing them time to settle some of Tsleil-Waututh\u2019s concerns outside the courtroom.</p><p>Kung thinks the feds should have put ongoing pipeline projects on hold while it hashes out new environmental assessment rules.</p><p>\u201cThe process is broken and it needs fixing,\u201d he said.</p><p>\u201cI think the Liberals\xa0campaigned on doing that, on fixing it. It\u2019s unfortunate they didn\u2019t take that first very logical and pragmatic step of pausing the processes in order to fix it.\u201d</p><p>The companies affected were more circumspect.</p><p>\u201cWhile we have concerns about how this delay could impact the Project schedule, we support the principle that public confidence in the review process is crucial,\u201d Kinder Morgan said in an emailed statement.</p><p>\u201cWe need some time to evaluate what this means for Energy East in-service,\u201d TransCanada spokesperson Mark Cooper said in an email.</p><p>\u201cHowever, at this point our focus remains a 2020 in-service date.\u201d</p><p>The opposition Conservatives were unimpressed.</p><p>\u201cThe message that was set today was a terrible, terrible signal to the world that we are going to have ongoing political interference,\u201d said Tory Environment and Climate Change critic Ed Fast.</p><p>So was Greenpeace.</p><p>\u201cSimply counting the greenhouse gas emissions won\u2019t change anything,\u201d said Keith Stewart, head of Greenpeace\u2019s energy campaign.</p><p>\u201cThe key question is what kind of climate test projects have to meet in order to be considered to be in the national interest.\u201d</p><p>Lawyer Maxime Faille is particularly concerned\xa0there was no mention of the seven-fold increase in marine traffic he says would result from Trans Mountain\u2019s twinning \u2014 a significant concern to the Tslail-Waututh.</p><p>\u201cI\u2019m\xa0encouraged to see a reference to increased consultation with aboriginal communities \u2014 that\u2019s critical.\u201d</p><p>But he\u2019ll believe the rhetoric when he sees it in action.</p><p>\u201cNow, obviously, is where the rubber hits the road. We\u2019ll have to see what happens.\u201d</p><p>NDP leader Tom Mulcair accused Trudeau of backpedalling on his commitment to firm environmental regulations.</p><p>\u201cDon\u2019t forget, what Mr. Trudeau promised during the election campaign wasn\u2019t a little bit of a patchwork on top of the existing system,\u201d Mulcair told reporters Wednesday morning.</p><p>\u201cNow what he\u2019s saying is, \u2018We\u2019ll take what\u2019s already been done, but we might add a little bit of a patch on top of it.\u2019 \u2026\xa0You can\u2019t build solidly on a defective foundation. You have to start over again.\u201d</p><p>WATCH: Mulcair on pipeline regulations</p><p>With a report from Monique Muise in Ottawa</p>Follow @amp6', u'rss_url': u'http://globalnews.ca/environment/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://globalnews.ca/news/2480676/canadas-pipeline-project-process-whats-changed-and-what-does-it-mean/', u'author': u'Anna Mehler Paperny', u'original_article_url': u'http://globalnews.ca/news/2480676/canadas-pipeline-project-process-whats-changed-and-what-does-it-mean/', u'scraped_on': 1453944625, u'published_date': u'Wed, 27 Jan 2016 22:57:35 +0000', u'title': u'Pipe dreams: Why no one\u2019s happy with Canada\u2019s changing pipeline process', u'_id': ObjectId('56a96f321d41c841e1d9c314'), u'media_url': u'http://globalnews.ca/'}
{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>The federal government is overhauling how it examines major energy projects to factor in greenhouse gas emissions to a greater extent, and is extending the deadlines for reviewing and deciding on the Energy East and Trans Mountain oil pipelines.</p>&#13;<p>Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr and Environment Minister Catherine McKenna announced new interim measures for reviewing energy projects while the government takes more time to craft a permanent environmental assessment regime for resource development that they hope will improve public confidence in the process. The government expects it will take a few years to craft a new, permanent regulatory review process.</p>&#13;<p>In the meantime, the new interim principles will apply to natural resource developments subject to federal environmental assessments, including pipelines, liquefied natural gas and mining projects.</p>&#13;<p>The ministers announced a specific set of measures for the</p>&#13;<p>Energy East pipeline and Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain pipeline, including assessing the upstream greenhouse gas emissions \u2013 that is, emissions from oil production \u2013 associated with the project and to make the information public.</p>&#13;<p>The government will also undertake \u201cdeeper consultations\u201d with Indigenous peoples potentially affected by the project and provide funding to support the consultations.</p>&#13;<p>For Energy East, the government plans to appoint the three temporary members to the regulator during the review, as well as expand public input into the NEB review process, including public and community engagement.</p>&#13;<p>The natural resources minister will seek a six-month extension of the NEB\u2019s legislated review time (to 21 months from 15 months) for Energy East as well as an extra three months for a final government decision (to six months from three months).</p>&#13;<p>That would mean the total review and decision timeframe would be 27 months, with a final decision from the federal government on the Energy East project expected in mid-2018.</p>&#13;<p>For Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain pipeline, the government will appoint a ministerial representative to consult communities, including Indigenous peoples potentially affected by the project.</p>&#13;<p>The government is also seeking a four-month extension of its time limit for making a decision on the Trans Mountain project, meaning it would now have until December 2016 to make a decision (instead of August 2016).</p>&#13;<p>While the government prepares a new, broader environmental assessment process, the following principles will apply to all natural resources projects currently under regulatory review:</p>&#13;<p>\u2013 Assessing all direct and upstream greenhouse gas emissions linked to projects under review;</p>&#13;<p>\u2013 No project proponent will be asked to return to the starting line;</p>&#13;<p>\u2013 Decisions will be based on science, \u201ctraditional knowledge of Indigenous peoples,\u201d and other relevant evidence;</p>&#13;<p>\u2013 The views of the public and affected communities will be sought and considered; and</p>&#13;<p>\u2013 Indigenous peoples will be \u201cmeaningfully consulted\u201d and, where appropriate, impacts on their rights and interests will be assessed.</p>&#13;<p>TransCanada\u2019s Energy East project and Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain pipeline expansion are currently under review by the National Energy Board.</p>&#13;<p>The Pacific NorthWest LNG project located near Prince Rupert, B.C. is currently being reviewed by the Canadian Environmental Assessment Agency, as is the Woodfibre LNG project near Squamish, B.C.</p>&#13;<p>McKenna is hosting meetings with provincial and territorial environment ministers in Ottawa starting Thursday, which will lay the groundwork for a first ministers\u2019 meeting in early March to establish a new national climate-change plan.</p>&#13;<p>The new rules announced Wednesday came a day after the federal environment commissioner said the National Energy Board is doing an \u201cinadequate\u201d job tracking the petroleum sector\u2019s compliance with pipeline approval conditions, and that there have been significant problems in the regulator\u2019s reviews of companies\u2019 emergency procedures.</p>&#13;<p>Debate over Energy East has sparked an East-West conflict after Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre, a former Liberal MP, and 81 other mayors from surrounding municipalities came out last week against the Energy East pipeline before NEB hearings on the project have even begun.</p>&#13;<p>Their opposition to the project spurred criticism from Saskatchewan Premier Brad Wall, Calgary Mayor Naheed Nenshi and Edmonton Mayor Don Iveson, who have said the project is in the best interests of the country.</p>&#13;<p>Conservative Leader Rona Ambrose has warned that opposition to Energy East in Quebec and other parts of Canada is a threat to national unity and is reminding many westerners of the once-loathed National Energy Program from Pierre Trudeau\u2019s Liberal government.</p>&#13;<p>TransCanada\u2019s 4,600-kilometre Energy East pipeline would transport 1.1 million barrels of oil a day from Alberta and Saskatchewan to refineries in Quebec (one in Montreal, one near Quebec City) and on to a deep-water marine terminal in Saint John, N.B.</p>&#13;<p>Kinder Morgan\u2019s expansion plans would nearly triple the capacity of its current Trans Mountain pipeline to 890,000 barrels of oil a day from the 300,000 barrels currently shipped in the existing pipeline.</p>&#13;<p>The 1,150-kilometre twinned Trans Mountain pipeline would run from near Edmonton to Burnaby, B.C. Kinder Morgan hopes pipeline construction can start in 2016 and go into service in 2018.</p>&#13;<p>The proposed $11.4-billion Pacific NorthWest LNG facility and marine terminal would be located on Lelu Island near Prince Rupert and convert natural gas to LNG for export to Pacific Rim markets in Asia. Pacific NorthWest LNG is a consortium controlled by Malaysia\u2019s state-owned Petronas.</p>&#13;<p>The NEB review of the Trans Mountain project must be completed by May 20, 2016, after the hearing process was delayed over controversy.</p>&#13;<p>In August 2015, the NEB panel reviewing the Trans Mountain project said any evidence prepared by Steven Kelly, a consultant at the time who prepared information in favour of the project, must be removed from the regulatory application.</p>&#13;<p>Kelly had just been appointed to a seven-year term at the National Energy Board. The NEB panel said his dual role could raise concerns about the integrity of the review.</p>&#13;<p>The NEB\u2019s review process, initially scheduled to be completed by late January, was lengthened until May after Kelly\u2019s evidence was removed.</p>&#13;<p>Environmental and civil society groups have assailed the NEB review of the Trans Mountain expansion, with dozens of participants dropping out of a process they say is unfair and biased.</p>&#13;<p>NDP Leader Tom Mulcair said Wednesday the old environmental assessment rules need to be completely tossed out and that project reviews should start over, something he said the Liberals have promised to do.</p>&#13;<p>During a federal election campaign stop in B.C. in August, Trudeau said the Kinder Morgan review process \u201cneeds to be redone.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>Mulcair said that restarting the assessment process for projects already under review is even more necessary considering the environment commissioner\u2019s damning report Tuesday about the NEB\u2019s pipeline oversight.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cWhat Mr. Trudeau promised during the election campaign wasn\u2019t a little bit of a patchwork on top of the existing system,\u201d Mulcair said.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cWhat we learned (Tuesday) from the environment commissioner is that basis, that foundation, is completely defective. So you can\u2019t build solidly on a defective foundation. You have to start over again.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau and the Liberal government promised in their platform to introduce a new environmental assessment process for energy projects that has more teeth.</p>&#13;<p>They also pledged to \u201cmodernize the National Energy Board\u201d to ensure it better reflects regional views, has \u201csufficient expertise\u201d in areas like environmental science and community development, and considers input from aboriginal groups.</p>&#13;<p>jfekete@ottawacitzien.com</p>&#13;<p>Twitter.com/jasonfekete</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/news/canada/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/2016/01/27/pipeline-rule-overhaul-in-works', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/2016/01/27/pipeline-rule-overhaul-in-works', u'scraped_on': 1453945980, u'published_date': u'Wed, 27 Jan 2016 18:26:44 CST', u'title': u'Pipeline rule overhaul in works', u'_id': ObjectId('56a9747e1d41c842aa999452'), u'media_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/'}
