{u'full_article': u'<p>OTTAWA \u2014 The Liberal government has added two new review mechanisms to how major new pipeline proposals are assessed. Here are some quotes on the announcement:</p><p>"We believe it is important and, in fact, essential to rebuild Canadians\' trust in our environmental assessment processes. We need to take into account the views and concerns of Canadians, respect the rights and interests of indigenous peoples and support our natural resources sector." Environment Minister Catherine McKenna.</p><p>___</p><p>"The government of Canada is free to establish its own review and its own criteria for those reviews in whatever context it chooses. And the cabinet ultimately will decide \u2014 weighing all the factors that are important to determine the national interest for these major projects." Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr.</p><p>___</p><p>"We support a strong and clear regulatory framework that helps Canadians see our commitment to building and operating oil and gas pipelines in the safest and most environmentally sound way possible. TransCanada operates in a highly regulated industry. We will continue to work with all levels of government and our regulators to ensure the continued safe and environmentally sound transportation of our natural resources to market." TransCanada spokesman Mark Cooper.</p><p>___</p><p>"While we have concerns about how this delay could impact the project schedule, we support the principle that public confidence in the review process is crucial and look forward to working with the Government of Canada on how our years of work in the area of consultation will be considered. We are confident we can demonstrate to Canadians, both the need for this Project and our commitment to building and operating this pipeline to the highest standards of environmental performance while supporting Aboriginal communities and providing lasting benefits for all Canadians." Ian Anderson, president of Kinder Morgan Canada.</p><p>___</p><p>"This is not good news for the natural resources sector. This another layer, another process." Conservative energy critic Candice Bergen.</p><p>___</p><p>"We have done our part to earn that confidence of Canadians. Now the federal government is, (through) this interim solution, attempting to have that honest conversation and ensure that voices are heard so that we can get to a decision on these matters." Alberta Environment Minister Shannon Phillips.</p><p>___</p><p>\u201cWhile we welcome this attempt by the federal government to clean up the broken regulatory process left behind by the Harper government, today\u2019s announcement does little to address our longstanding concerns with the Kinder Morgan pipeline review process." Karen Campbell, Ecojustice lawyer.</p><p>___</p><p>"We are encouraged that the federal government is finally listening to the concerns of Canadians with its new transitional pipeline review process. However, it doesn\u2019t make sense that Energy East would fall under anything but a completely reformed process. As Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr acknowledged, the National Energy Board (NEB) hasn\u2019t yet deemed the Energy East application complete; the review has not begun. If the transitional process is just for projects under review, it shouldn\u2019t apply to Energy East." Adam Scott of Environmental Defence.</p><p>___</p><p>\u201cIf this new process is rigorous and the government does what it says it will do, they will come to the inevitable conclusion that the Kinder Morgan pipeline cannot be built. That is what the science says, that is what the communities say and that is what the First Nations are saying.\u201d Sven Biggs, pipeline campaigner for ForestEthics Advocacy.</p><p>___</p><p>"The truth is that the current system must be totally replaced. The National Energy Board has only been doing environmental assessments since the previous Conservative government gutted the environmental assessment process with C-38 in 2012. It is ill-suited for the role and woefully inexperienced. We need to return to the regime as it existed before the previous Conservative government took an axe to it. We need to build on what worked." Green party Leader Elizabeth May.</p><p>___</p><p>\u201cThe Trudeau government has a big job on its hands fixing pipeline reviews in this country. The recent audit of the NEB reveals deep flaws in the institution, while the board itself is dominated by individuals with a background in the fossil fuel industry. There is also the ongoing priority to reverse and then strengthen the many changes made by Harper that derailed our environmental regulations.\u201d Maude Barlow, national chairperson of the Council of Canadians.</p><p>___</p><p>\u201cIn addition to these review rules, Canada must establish a science-based climate plan that includes minimum standards on carbon pricing, and that builds upon existing provincial action. Existing provincial climate commitments will not ensure Canada meets its 2030 emissions reductions goal, and are not sufficient to ensure Canada lives up to its fair share of the Paris Agreement." Erin Flanagan, federal policy director at the Pembina Institute.</p><p>___</p><p>\u201cGreenpeace applauds Prime Minister Trudeau for taking the first steps in reforming the badly flawed environmental review process and adding an integral step to infrastructure reviews. The new rules must ensure that all energy projects are in line with Canada\u2019s commitment to doing its part to stabilize global temperature increase below 1.5 degrees. That means no new tar sands mines or pipelines." Peter Louwe of Greenpeace.</p><p>___</p><p>"Recovery of the national economy depends on getting these pipelines built but we also need to accept that this will not happen without public support. What will be critical now is everyone stepping up to the plate so we do not face further delays. Everyone involved will need to consider not just their local interest but also the national interest." Dylan Jones, president of the Canada West Foundation.</p><p>___</p><p>"What will be important to our members and to the upstream oil and gas industry is how will this affect the in-service date of the projects and we\'ll be looking into that in the coming days. I think we have an obligation to have a thorough and robust regulatory and environmental oversight, but we should always be conscious of not duplicating the process." Tim McMillan, head of the Canadian Association of Petroleum Producers.</p><p>___</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/search/?c%5B%5D=news%2A&d=&d1=&d2=&f=rss&l=25&q=&s=start_time&sd=desc&t=article', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/news/national/quotes-on-the-liberal-government-s-environmental-review-announcement/article_1cc364dc-1ecd-58ec-aa73-0bc74e694af7.html', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/news/national/quotes-on-the-liberal-government-s-environmental-review-announcement/article_1cc364dc-1ecd-58ec-aa73-0bc74e694af7.html', u'scraped_on': 1453947716, u'published_date': u'Wed, 27 Jan 2016 20:29:29 -0500', u'title': u"Quotes on the Liberal government's environmental review announcement", u'_id': ObjectId('56a97b441d41c845267f028d'), u'media_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>Tony Keller is editorial page editor</p><p>On Wednesday, the Trudeau government announced new rules for reviewing pipeline projects. Among them is that a pipeline\u2019s climate-change impact \u2013 will it increase production of greenhouse gases? \u2013 must be assessed when considering whether to give it the green light. At almost the same time as the government was making its announcement, the National Energy Board, which among other things is the body that reviews new pipelines, released a major report on the future of Canada\u2019s energy industry. You don\u2019t have to read far into that NEB report to find it implicitly endorsing the idea that pipelines indirectly increase greenhouse-gas emissions.</p><p>According to the NEB, Canadian oil production in 2040 will be 56 per cent higher than it is now, at 6.1-million barrels a day. However, that prediction rests on the assumption that sufficient pipeline capacity will be built to move all of that oil to market. In an alternate scenario \u2013 what the NEB calls its \u201cConstrained Oil Pipeline Capacity Case,\u201d where none of the major pipelines now on the table are approved \u2013 Canadian oil production in 2040 will be markedly lower, at only 5.6-million barrels. And without new pipelines, capital expenditures in the oil patch will be \u201creduced by 15 per cent in the conventional oil sector, and by nine per cent in the oil sands.\u201d</p><p>It all comes down to Economics 101. If you want to encourage consumption of something, make it cheaper. If you want to discourage it, make it more expensive. That\u2019s why economists who want to reduce greenhouse-gas emissions believe in putting a price on carbon with carbon taxes.</p><p>Pipelines are generally the cheapest and most efficient way to move oil to market. Pipelines lower the price the producers must pay to move their oil, which means that the profit Western Canadian producers earn is higher \u2013 encouraging more oil production. And because pipelines can transport oil at lower cost than the alternatives, they make some of Western Canada\u2019s more costly and otherwise marginal oil operations economically viable.</p><p>If the three major pipelines under consideration \u2013 Enbridge\u2019s Northern Gateway, Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain expansion and TransCanada\u2019s Energy East \u2013 are never built, then the NEB\u2019s best guess is that will result in an extra half-million barrels of Canadian oil a day being priced out of the market. That oil will simply remain in the ground. If the pipelines are built, then that oil will be taken out the ground, refined and burned, with significant greenhouse-gas emissions.</p><p>The NEB looked at what would happen to prices for Canada\u2019s two main Western oil benchmarks \u2013 heavy grade Western Canada Select (WCS), and the light grade known as Mixed Sweet Blend (MSW) \u2013 without new pipeline construction. The difference between the MSW price and the North American benchmark of West Texas Intermediate would widen, says the NEB, by about $5 (U.S.) per barrel. That\u2019s how much less producers would get paid for their oil. And the spread between WCS and West Texas would grow to \u201cabout $10 (U.S.) per barrel. This is the approximate extra cost that would be incurred to transport Canadian heavy crude to the U.S. Gulf Coast by rail instead of by pipeline.\u201d At those lower prices, a lot of Canadian oil production becomes uneconomic.</p><p>The bottom line? \u201cThe assumptions of the Constrained Case have implications for Canada\u2019s energy system,\u201d says the NEB. \u201cReliance on a more costly form of transportation such as rail, as well as increased competition for market share and pipeline capacity, leads to lower prices received by Canadian producers, net of transportation costs. This leads investment, crude-oil production, economic growth, energy use and GHG emissions to grow more slowly.\u201d</p><p>Now flip the previous sentences around: Reliance on a less costly form of transportation such as pipelines leads to higher prices received for Canadian producers. This leads investment, crude-oil production, economic growth, energy use and GHG emissions to grow more quickly.</p><p>How will the new, greenhouse gas-conscious, pipeline-review process deal with that fact?</p><p>The logical way to lower Canada\u2019s greenhouse-gas emissions is to reduce demand for oil and other carbon-based fuels by putting a tax on them. A carbon tax would give governments a big new revenue source, which could be used to boost spending or lower other taxes. Forcing one part of the economy to be less efficient (by preventing new pipelines from being built and having oil move by more costly rail) might also raise oil prices and lower consumption. Then again, it might just shut down some Canadian production, with no effect on output in the rest of the world or consumption in Canada. (Isn\u2019t that what\u2019s happening already?)</p><p>Whatever the carbon-emissions outcome, it would result in no new revenue for government or economic benefits for Canadians. That\u2019s why reducing Canadian oil production by kneecapping pipelines as a punishment for their efficiency, rather than reducing emissions economy-wide through a tax-based carbon price, is a half-baked idea.</p><p>But many environmental groups have long wanted pipeline reviews to more fully consider their greenhouse gas consequences, and given the NEB\u2019s own conclusions, it\u2019s not hard to see why. \u201cWe applaud the Trudeau government\u2019s decision to add a \u2018climate test\u2019 to the assessment process,\u201d said Steven Guilbeault, senior director at \xc9quiterre in a press release. \u201cWe believe that this climate test will enable the government to ensure that no project gets approval if it does not meet the Paris Agreement to limit temperature rise to 1.5 degrees Celsius.\u201d</p><p>Over the coming months, as several pipeline reviews move toward the finish line, Canadians will find out exactly what this new test means, and how it\u2019s going to work.</p><p class="article__ontwitter">Follow us on Twitter: @GlobeDebate</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/?service=rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/opinion/yes-pipelines-increase-greenhouse-gases-will-acknowledging-it-kneecap-canadas-oil-industry/article28434293/', u'author': u'Tony Keller', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/opinion/yes-pipelines-increase-greenhouse-gases-will-acknowledging-it-kneecap-canadas-oil-industry/article28434293/?cmpid=rss1', u'scraped_on': 1454005325, u'published_date': u'Thu, 28 Jan 2016 13:06:00 -0500', u'title': u'Yes, pipelines increase greenhouse gases. Will acknowledging it kneecap Canada\u2019s oil industry?', u'_id': ObjectId('56aa5c4e1d41c862b1cdbaa8'), u'media_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>Prime Minister Justin Trudeau, left, shakes hands with Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre following their joint press conference in Montreal, Tuesday, January 26, 2016. THE CANADIAN PRESS/Graham Hughes0127 biz gmo pipelinesGraham Hughes / THE CANADIAN PRESS</p><p>Scottish Enlightenment philosopher David Hume said it best: reason is, and ought only to be, a slave to the passions.</p><p>If you\u2019ve ever tried to reason yourself into a feeling (\u201cI really ought to love Mr. X, or Wagner operas, or pickled herring\u201d), you\u2019ll know that for the vast majority of humanity it is a vain enterprise. That\u2019s because feelings don\u2019t come from reason and the mind. They come from emotions and the heart. </p><p>Hume\u2019s point was that reason doesn\u2019t tell us what to want, only how to get it. You may be surprised to learn that this matters where oil pipelines are concerned.</p><p>Ottawa has announced it intends to expand the factors regulators consider when weighing whether to approve new pipelines, such as Kinder-Morgan, Energy East and others. Henceforth the National Energy Board, for example, when considering pipelines will be called upon to assess the contribution of upstream operations to greenhouse gas emissions in addition to things such as design, safety, environmental impact and conformity with legal requirements such as consulting and accommodating affected aboriginal communities.</p><p>That\u2019s perfectly reasonable. The issue of climate change caused by human activities is a huge one and has spawned significant public opposition within Canada to our petroleum industry and to the pipeline infrastructure needed to gets its products to market. </p><p>The problem, however, is that our whole regulatory process for pipeline approval is based on reason, on a thoughtful attempt to design a process that examines objective evidence dispassionately. When that weighing of the evidence shows that pipelines meet the tests established by legislators and regulators, those pipelines are approved.</p><p>Opposition to pipelines, however, is notoriously resistant to reason and evidence. </p><p>The U.S. State Department conducted numerous environmental reviews of the proposed Keystone XL pipeline designed to bring Alberta and U.S. crude to the U.S. Gulf Coast. Those reviews included looking at the impact on greenhouse gas emissions. Every one of these reasoned evidence-based reviews said that KXL\u2019s impact would be negligible and that this was not a ground to refuse the presidential permit TransCanada Pipeline needed to build the project.</p><p>But the pipeline opponents didn\u2019t care about the evidence. They believed that they were eco-warriors fighting for \u201cclimate justice.\u201d President Barack Obama bowed to their unreasoning opposition to pipelines when he turned down KXL.</p><p>Here in Canada, in an attempt to mute the opposition of environmental groups to pipelines to the east and west coasts, Alberta\u2019s NDP government negotiated a deal with some of the oilpatch and several environmental groups that would cap the greenhouse gas emissions of the oilsands and raise already high environmental standards even higher. </p><p>This strategy of making reasoned process changes to win over opponents has already failed. On the West Coast, the opposition to the Kinder Morgan pipeline to Burnaby, B.C., is only getting noisier, while the federal Liberals themselves are trying to kill the Northern Gateway project indirectly by legislating an oil tanker ban in northern B.C. waters.</p><p>In Montreal all the local mayors have come out in opposition to Energy East. On the day Ottawa announced its new regulatory measures, more than 70 non-governmental organizations wrote the prime minister and premiers to oppose any new pipelines.</p><p>Toughened process requirements cannot satisfy pipeline opponents or even cause them to be less vociferous in their attacks because they believe that any process that approves pipelines is itself vicious and flawed and they are willing to toss democracy, the rule of law and balanced treatment of conflicting interests on the trash heap to achieve their aims.</p><p>At some point our politicians are going to confront the hard truth that there are no process fixes that will satisfy pipeline opponents; they believe pipelines are bad and the approval process a weapon to be commandeered in the struggle against them. There is no compromise that will win their assent. Then our leaders will have to make choices about whose side they\u2019re on. It won\u2019t be pretty. </p><p>Brian Lee Crowley (twitter.com/brianleecrowley) is the Managing Director of the Macdonald-Laurier Institute, an independent non-partisan public policy think tank in Ottawa: www.macdonaldlaurier.ca.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://ottawacitizen.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://ottawacitizen.com/opinion/columnists/crowley-pipeline-opposition-emotional-not-reasoned', u'author': u'Tyler Dawson, Ottawa Citizen', u'original_article_url': u'http://ottawacitizen.com/opinion/columnists/crowley-pipeline-opposition-emotional-not-reasoned', u'scraped_on': 1454079874, u'published_date': u'Fri, 29 Jan 2016 14:58:23 +0000', u'title': u'Crowley: Pipeline opposition emotional, not reasoned', u'_id': ObjectId('56ab7f841d41c810d8570a4f'), u'media_url': u'http://ottawacitizen.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>TransCanada Corp. is going on the offensive in its efforts to win over wary Quebeckers to its controversial Energy East oil pipeline project.</p><p>Company officials on Wednesday announced a deal with a power equipment manufacturer to supply electrical equipment for the pipeline in the Montreal region that will create up to 120 jobs in Quebec.</p><p>And they said that is just one step in a series of other anticipated job-creating partnerships with companies in Quebec, Louis Bergeron, vice-president for Energy East in Quebec and New Brunswick, said.</p><p>\u201cWe have the intention of continuing with other partners in Quebec,\u201d Mr. Bergeron said at a news conference Wednesday.</p><p>John Sioni, president of the Energy East Pipeline Project, said Calgary-based TransCanada is receptive to the safety, environmental and economic concerns of Quebeckers regarding the $15.7-billion pipeline, which would move 1.1 million barrels of Western crude through Quebec and on to a refinery in Saint John, N.B.</p><p>\u201cI would hope that [Wednesday\u2019s announcement] would be seen as an expression of our intent to develop economic benefits in Quebec,\u201d he said.</p><p>Added Mr. Bergeron: \u201cIt\u2019s one step in this direction. {u'full_article': u'<p>TransCanada is trying to convince Quebecers to support its proposed Energy East pipeline by discussing the economic benefits.</p><p>Wednesday morning TransCanada said that if the pipeline from Alberta to New Brunswick is approved, it would mean the creation of about 200 jobs in Quebec for pumping stations alone.</p><p>120 of those jobs would be with the firm to ABB, a Montreal-based international company that builds electrical pumping stations it calls "e-houses."</p><p>ABB says the pumping stations are designed to TransCanada\'s specifications to minimize the local environmental impact.</p><p>TransCanada says the actual construction of the pipeline would create more than 3,000 jobs during nine years of planning and building.</p><p>Louis Bergeron, the Quebec-New Brunswick vice-president for TransCanada, admits he has an uphill battle swaying public opinion.</p><p>"We did a survey last year and we concluded that there\'s one Quebecer that is very familiar with Energy East, but three out of four will say they\'ve heard about it," said Bergeron.</p><p>"We need to better explain, better communicate.</p><p>In recent weeks the 82 mayors that make up the Montreal Metropolitan Community announced their formal opposition to the pipeline, and said they would voice that opposition at National Energy Board hearings.</p><p>Bergeron said TransCanada believes those hearings will give the public a chance to air grievances and become informed.</p><p>"I believe that the BAPE hearings and the Farmland Protection Commission Hearings, which should take place in 2016, will be an opportunity for us to better explain," said Bergeron.</p><p>The CMM mayors say they believe the risks of the pipeline outweigh the benefits, with possible benefits of $2 million per year, but an estimate that cleaning a spill could cost up to $10 billion.</p><p>They are joined in their opposition by First Nations groups and Environmental organizations. \xa0</p>', {u'full_article': u'<p class="story-times dtstamp">Posted: Thursday, February 4, 2016 6:00 am</p><p class="byline">Energy East pipeline: Slights and oversightsLetter to the editor by Peter Langchroniclejournal.com| 0\xa0comments</p><p>Last week the audit by the federal environment commissioner, Julie Gelfand, stated that half the National Energy Board cases which they studied lacked documentation, and a final analysis. Yet, the NEB is now considering whether TransCanada Pipeline Corporation\u2019s submission on their proposed Energy East pipeline is complete. NEB is the regulator!</p><p>Simultaneously we hear that TransCanada has made some 700 changes to its original submission on Energy East, which has resulted in $3 billion additional costs. Apparently, according to their CEO, Russ Girling, TCC is \u201clistening and acting\u201d on what they have heard from concerned citizens.</p><p>But, conspicuously absent from those 700 changes is anything to say that the old section of Energy East pipe from Saskatchewan to Cornwall, Ont., won\u2019t simply be converted \u2014 rather than be laid as new pipe. This, of course, is the section of pipe which has leaked and exploded some 12 times through our region in as many years. TCC is the corporation!</p><p>Meanwhile, Ontario Premier Kathleen Wynne has given support to Alberta Premier Rachel Notley in her quest to \u201cget product to market.\u201d This is after the Ontario Energy Board last year concluded that the risks of Energy East outweighed the benefits. This is our provincial government!</p><p>So, while TCC, NEB, and Ontario are lining up to make the pipeline a reality, we, the people affected (with more risk than benefits), are left to suffer the slights and oversights of their planning.</p><p>We must work together to change this picture.</p><p>Peter Lang</p><p>Kaministiquia</p><p class="page_navigation"/><p class="clear"/><p class="clear"/><p class="clear"/><p class="clear"/><p class="story-keywords moz-border">Posted inLetters To EditoronThursday, February 4, 2016 6:00 am.</p><p class="OUTBRAIN" data-src="http://www.chroniclejournal.com/opinion/letters_to_editor/energy-east-pipeline-slights-and-oversights/article_b2182856-cac3-11e5-80cf-775425fb3e9a.html" data-widget-id="AR_1" data-ob-template="TownNews"/><p class="clear"/><p id="taboola-div"/>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/search/?c%5B%5D=opinion%2Fletters_to_editor%2A&d=&d1=&d2=&f=rss&l=25&q=&s=start_time&sd=desc&t=article', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/opinion/letters_to_editor/energy-east-pipeline-slights-and-oversights/article_b2182856-cac3-11e5-80cf-775425fb3e9a.html', u'author': u'Letter to the editor by Peter Lang', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/opinion/letters_to_editor/energy-east-pipeline-slights-and-oversights/article_b2182856-cac3-11e5-80cf-775425fb3e9a.html', u'scraped_on': 1454583885, u'published_date': u'Thu, 04 Feb 2016 06:00:00 -0500', u'title': u'Energy East pipeline: Slights and oversights', u'_id': ObjectId('56b3304d1d41c8224e2fcc11'), u'media_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>The war of words between Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre andWildrose Leader Brian Jean has taken a Stone Age turn.</p>&#13;<p>Jean had accused Montreal mayors of hypocrisy for opposing the Energy East pipeline over economic and environmental concerns, while allowing raw sewage to be dumped in the St. Lawrence River last fall and for Quebec\'s acceptance of equalization payments. He also said the decision was based on politics, not science.</p>&#13;<p>Coderre fired back at Jean in an interview on Radio-Canada.</p>&#13;<p>"You have to allow me a moment to laugh at a guy like Brian Jean, when he says he relies on science. These are probably the same people who think the Flintstones is a documentary," he said.</p>&#13;<p>Coderre, a former Liberal MP, was echoing a comment made by Liberal strategist Warren Kinsella in 2000 against then-Canadian Alliance leader Stockwell Day.</p>&#13;<p>A day after Coderre and fellow Montreal-area mayors came out swinging against Energy East and the Alberta government\'s pipeline strategy came under fire, Premier Rachel Notley received some welcome support from Ontario\'s premier.</p>&#13;<p>In a joint news conference in Toronto on Friday, Kathleen Wynne praised Notley for the NDP government\'s climate change plan and gave at least tentative backing to the $15.7-billion Energy East pipeline, which would move Alberta oilsands crude to the east coast for export.</p>&#13;<p>The Liberal premier said Alberta\'s efforts "makes the national conversation about climate targets and pipelines easier."</p>&#13;<p>"We appreciate that there is a need for a way to get Canadian oil, that is allowed under Alberta\u2019s new emission cap, to overseas markets. And the people of Ontario care a great deal about the national economy and the potential jobs that this proposed pipeline project could create in our province and across the country,\u201d said Wynne.</p>&#13;<p>Coderre\'s announcement that municipalities in the city\'s metropolitan area were opposed to Energy East prompted\xa0a flurry of criticism from Alberta and western Canadian politicians aimed at Coderre, with the NDP government describing his attitude as "both ungenerous and short-sighted."</p>&#13;<p>But Notley also faced criticism, with the Wildrose leader saying Montreal\'s rejection showed that the NDP plan to gain social licence for pipeline through tougher environmental policies had failed.</p>&#13;<p>The NDP climate plan released last fall calls for the implementation of a broad-based carbon tax in 2017, an oilsand emissions cap, the accelerated phase-out of coal and a sharp reduction in methane emissions.</p>&#13;<p>At the news conference, Notley repeated the criticism of Coderre as short-sighted and touted the pan-Canadian economic benefits of Energy East, which is being pushed forward by Calgary-based TransCanada Corp.</p>&#13;<p>"This pipeline is part of building a strong Canada, it\'s part of building a strong economy," she said.</p>&#13;<p>"And let me just say, a pipeline is the safest way, the most efficient way (to ship oil) {u'full_article': u'<p>The National Energy Board has serious problems in its effort to ensure the safety of pipelines, the federal environment commissioner says \u2013 a finding that will provide more fuel for the country\u2019s heated debate over two proposed multibillion-dollar projects.</p><p>In an audit released Tuesday, the commissioner, Julie Gelfand, said the Calgary-based federal regulator did not adequately track the implementation of conditions that the board itself required in approving pipelines, nor did it consistently follow up on deficiencies in companies\u2019 overall regulatory compliance.</p><p>With the proposed expansion of oil-and-gas pipeline capacity, \u201cit is clear that the National Energy Board needs to do more to keep pace with the rapidly changing context in which it is operating,\u201d Ms. Gelfand said in a statement.</p><p>The environment commissioner also found fault with the Pest Management Regulatory Agency, saying it has been issuing conditional approvals for controversial pesticides and leaving them on the market despite evidence of environmental damage.</p><p>Her report comes amid growing East-West tensions over the proposed Energy East project, and continued opposition in British Columbia to the expansion of the TransMountain pipeline that carries crude from Alberta to Vancouver.</p><p>Prime Minister Justin Trudeau met Tuesday with Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre, who \u2013 along with his area colleagues \u2013 precipitated a cross-Canada political storm last week over their opposition to TransCanada Corp.\u2019s Energy East project. That proposed $15.7-billion pipeline would carry 1.1-million barrels a day of crude through the Montreal area to eastern refineries and export terminals.</p><p>Mr. Trudeau has pledged to strengthen the environmental approvals process, which critics complain became a rubber stamp under the previous Conservative government. The government plans to subject the two proposed pipelines to an assessment of how they would impact greenhouse-gas emissions in the upstream oil sector in Alberta, sources told The Globe and Mail Monday.</p><p>The environment commissioner \u2013 part of the Office of the Auditor General \u2013 released a report five years ago that found serious shortcomings in the National Energy Board\u2019s oversight of pipeline safety. In its report Tuesday, it said the NEB had addressed many of those concerns, but that significant gaps remain.</p><p>The auditors found that in half of the 49 pipeline approvals they examined, the NEB did not adequately track whether companies satisfied the conditions set by the board, while there were \u201cdeficiencies\u201d in 22 of 42 cases in which the board was dealing with regulatory non-compliance for existing operations.</p><p>\u201cThis is important because pipeline approval conditions and regulatory requirements exist so that pipelines can be built and operated safely,\u201d the audit report said.</p><p>It also found the NEB was having trouble recruiting and retaining the staff needed to meet its regulatory mandate.</p><p>The audit urged the board to strengthen its risk-assessment and emergency-management planning. For example, a third of companies\u2019 emergency procedural manuals \u2013 which are reviewed by the board \u2013 lacked necessary documentation.</p><p>Together with the proposed Northern Gateway project, the megaprojects approved or under consideration would double the country\u2019s oil transportation capacity, with proposed investment totalling more than $25-billion.</p><p>The Harper government approved the Northern Gateway project, with 209 conditions imposed by the NEB. Enbridge Inc. is still working to meet those, though the Liberal government vows to ban tanker traffic in the area, a move which would effectively kill the project. If approved, the TransMountain or Energy East project would also face scores of conditions to ensure the work was done properly.</p><p>In its response contained in the audit report, the NEB agreed with the auditor\u2019s conclusions. It said it does assess whether companies meet approval conditions but will document its work more rigorously. It said it is working to strengthen its compliance effort and will \u201cclarify the consequences for companies that do not undertake corrective action\u201d within the required time frame.</p><p>On pesticides, the auditor concluded that the federal regulatory agency has fallen short of its mandate of \u201cpreventing unacceptable risks to the health of Canadians and the environment from the use of pesticides.\u201d</p><p>At issue is its practice of issuing conditional registration for prolonged periods; such registrations are granted when risks are deemed acceptable but more information is required. Eight of nine products that have been conditionally registered for more than a decade are neonicotinoids which, the auditors noted, are now widely in use in Canada and are suspected of being a threat to bees, other pollinators and broader ecosystems.</p><p>Last week, the Pest Management Regulatory Agency announced it would no longer issue conditional registration, effective June 1.</p><p class="article__ontwitter">Follow Shawn McCarthy on Twitter: @smccarthy55</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/?service=rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/news/politics/federal-watchdog-hits-national-energy-board-on-scrutiny-of-pipeline-safety/article28392658/', u'author': u'Shawn McCarthy', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/news/politics/federal-watchdog-hits-national-energy-board-on-scrutiny-of-pipeline-safety/article28392658/?cmpid=rss1', u'scraped_on': 1453823406, u'published_date': u'Tue, 26 Jan 2016 10:16:32 -0500', u'title': u'Federal watchdog hits National Energy Board on scrutiny of pipeline safety', u'_id': ObjectId('56a795b01d41c87ba5c9ac46'), u'media_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p> Les pipelines de comp\xe9tence f\xe9d\xe9rale ne sont pas surveill\xe9s ad\xe9quatement par l\u2019Office national de l\u2019\xe9nergie, signale la commissaire \xe0 l\u2019environnement et au d\xe9veloppement durable, dans un rapport rendu public mardi.</p><p> L\u2019audit de Julie Gelfand soul\xe8ve plusieurs lacunes observ\xe9es aupr\xe8s de cet organisme f\xe9d\xe9ral, ayant la responsabilit\xe9 de r\xe9glementer et de surveiller les quelque 73 000 kilom\xe8tres de pipeline traversant le pays et qu\u2019exploitent une centaine de compagnies p\xe9troli\xe8res et gazi\xe8res.</p><p> Or, la commissaire conclut que l\u2019Office national de l\u2019\xe9nergie (ON\xc9) n\u2019a pas surveill\xe9 de mani\xe8re appropri\xe9e la mise en \u0153uvre des conditions d\u2019approbation des pipelines ni effectu\xe9 de suivi ad\xe9quat des \xe9carts de conformit\xe9 des compagnies. Elle d\xe9plore en outre que les syst\xe8mes de suivi de l\u2019ON\xc9 soient \xabd\xe9suets et inefficaces\xbb.</p><p> La commissaire souligne \xe9galement la difficult\xe9 qu\u2019\xe9prouve l\u2019ON\xc9 \xe0 recruter du personnel sp\xe9cialis\xe9 et \xe0 le maintenir en poste pour assurer l\u2019int\xe9grit\xe9 des pipelines et la conformit\xe9 des r\xe8glements. Elle ajoute que l\u2019ON\xc9 devra accentuer ses efforts dans un contexte o\xf9 les grands projets de pipeline pourraient presque doubler la capacit\xe9 actuelle d\u2019ici 2020.</p><p> D\u2019apr\xe8s l\u2019audit, l\u2019ON\xc9 a ainsi effectu\xe9 des suivis ad\xe9quats, pour s\u2019assurer que les pipelines se conformaient v\xe9ritablement \xe0 leurs conditions d\u2019approbation et les avait document\xe9s correctement, dans seulement la moiti\xe9 des cas examin\xe9s, soit 25 sur 49.</p><p> \xabPour les 24 autres cas, soit le suivi effectu\xe9 manquait d\u2019exactitude ou n\u2019\xe9tait pas \xe0 jour; soit il manquait des documents cl\xe9s dans le dossier; soit les dossiers ne contenaient pas d\u2019analyse finale des renseignements et des rapports soumis par la compagnie ou de conclusion, \xe0 savoir si les conditions avaient \xe9t\xe9 respect\xe9es enti\xe8rement\xbb, indique le rapport.</p><p> La commissaire a ainsi constat\xe9, lors de l\u2019examen d\u2019un dossier, que l\u2019ON\xc9 n\u2019avait pas v\xe9rifi\xe9 si une compagnie s\u2019\xe9tait conform\xe9e \xe0 une condition impos\xe9e, comme pr\xe9senter une \xe9tude d\xe9terminant les effets sur l\u2019environnement de la rupture d\u2019un pipeline transportant un produit combustible. He seems tohave a good grasp of the issues and promotes Canada\u2019s energy industry.Our current leaders fail miserably at understanding the energy industryor representing the country or province. Governments are not run ontaxes alone but need industry for healthy growth and prosperity. Canadais badly in need of leaders. Currently we have none!</p>&#13;<p>ED SKAPIN</p>&#13;<p>(There\u2019s no question Canada and U.S. need to repair their relationship.)</p>&#13;<p>TWO CANADAS</p>&#13;<p>In reply to the letter from Mike Roebuck: I do agree 110% with your comment, however it does not go far enough. Look at the map of Canada and you will soon see that there is very little we in the West have in common with the East. The West believes in criminal justice, the East believes in hug-a-thug sentencing. The West believes in less government in our lives, the East wants more. The West believes in prudent fiscal management (that is arguable in Alberta right now) whereas the East lives for huge deficits and paying off their friends. After all, the Liberals are entitled to their entitlements. From the first days of Confederation, the West have been the cash cow for the East. From the wheat board raping western farmers to the \u201980s NEP and now the new NEP (called a carbon tax). Enough! It\u2019s time to revive the Reform Party (or similar) with the goal of separation. This time, the West wants out!</p>&#13;<p>DEAN LUDTKE</p>&#13;<p>(Sorry, Dean, but we firmly believe in one Canada.)</p>&#13;<p>HELP THE VETS</p>&#13;<p>A recent article indicates there are at least 2,250 vets who are considered homeless. This is a deplorable situation yet the Drama Queen wants to bring in 25,000 more people to house, clothe, feed and provide medical care for. As if wait times aren\u2019t long enough now for medical treatment, just wait until 25,000 more get into the system. These refugees should have been housed in a refugee camp in Turkey or some other Middle Eastern country until such time as the situation is stabilized. They could then be resettled in their own country. And please do not tell me we can look after both our homeless and the refugees because we obviously can\u2019t.</p>&#13;<p>GORDON RICKETTS</p>&#13;<p>(It\u2019s a crying shame if there are ANY vets living on the streets.)</p>&#13;<p>\xa0</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/opinion/letters/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/2016/01/10/letters-jan-11', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/2016/01/10/letters-jan-11', u'scraped_on': 1453823138, u'published_date': u'Sun, 10 Jan 2016 17:43:04 MST', u'title': u'Letters, Jan. 11', u'_id': ObjectId('56a794a81d41c87ba5c997de'), u'media_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>It\u2019s not often we find ourselves in agreement with Calgary Mayor Naheed Nenshi, but when he\u2019s right, he\u2019s right: Montreal\u2019s mayor Denis Coderre is a pompous fool over the Energy East project.</p>&#13;<p>On Thursday, Coderre announced he would oppose TransCanada\u2019s $12-billion, 4,600-kilometre, Energy East pipeline from Alberta to refineries and ports in New Brunswick. Too environmentally risky, Coderre charged. Not enough economic benefits, he harrumphed.</p>&#13;<p>Nenshi, quite correctly, pointed out that large sections of the pipeline already exist \u2013 like the part that already flows right into Montreal. Coderre\u2019s city isn\u2019t being asked to take on new risk. Indeed, as Nenshi reminded is counterpart, \u201cThis is a project to modernize (the existing stretch), to bring it up to even better standards.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>If anything, Energy East will reduce the risk of an environmentally damaging oil leak in Quebec.</p>&#13;<p>As to Coderre\u2019s claim that the project will create just 33 new jobs in his city and generate just $2 million in new municipal revenues annually, that\u2019s a very, very blinkered view.</p>&#13;<p>If built, Energy East will add $55 billion to Canada\u2019s GDP over its functioning lifespan. Substantial amounts of that (about $17 billion) will flow to senior levels of government (mainly Ottawa) which will distribute it down to municipalities such as Coderre\u2019s Montreal.</p>&#13;<p>Ottawa is paying $2 billion for a new bridge linking Montreal with its suburbs on the south shore of the St. Lawrence. Where does Mayor Coderre fantasize that that money is coming from? Magical fairies? A leprechaun and his Lucky Charms?</p>&#13;<p>The Quebec government is also the recipient of half of all the equalization money paid out by Ottawa, upwards of $8 billion a year. That money funds Quebec\u2019s lavish social programs such as cheap tuition and daycare.</p>&#13;<p>But that money doesn\u2019t appear out of the mists. It is underwritten in large part by Albertans and the energy industry. Since about half of the $8 billion the Quebec government receives gets spent in Montreal and area (like the subsidies that keep Bombardier open), perhaps Coderre could rethink biting the hand that feeds his city and its economy.</p>&#13;<p>Alberta also has nothing to learn from Coderre about environmental protection. Two months ago, rather than spend $1 billion treating 8 billion litres of raw sewage, Coderre allowed it all to be dumped into the St. Lawrence River. As our own Lorne Gunter reminds today, that increased the levels of fecal pollution in the river by a factor of nearly 14,000 times.</p>&#13;<p>Then, to add insult to injury, when Wild Rose Party Leader Brian Jean pointed out the hypocrisy of Coderre\u2019s eco-stance, the self-satisfied and pompous Montreal mayor jeered on French CBC that Wild Rose are \u201cthe same people who think the Flintstones is a documentary.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>Coderre sure has a lot of arrogance for a man whose city and province don\u2019t pay their own way. He sure seems to like Alberta\u2019s money, but when it comes to the energy, resources and hard work that generates the cash his city depends on \u2013 oh, no, he\u2019s too good for that.</p>&#13;<p>\xa0</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.calgarysun.com/opinion/editorial/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.calgarysun.com/2016/01/22/montreal-mayor-denis-coderre-is-a-pompous-fool-over-the-energy-east-project', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.calgarysun.com/2016/01/22/montreal-mayor-denis-coderre-is-a-pompous-fool-over-the-energy-east-project', u'scraped_on': 1453823175, u'published_date': u'Fri, 22 Jan 2016 15:55:46 MST', u'title': u'Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre is a pompous fool over the Energy East project', u'_id': ObjectId('56a794cc1d41c87ba5c99948'), u'media_url': u'http://www.calgarysun.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>TransCanada\'s Keystone pipeline facilities are seen in Hardisty, Alta., on Friday, Nov. 6, 2015. Lee Hayes and his coworker Dave Stuart have a blunt assessment of U.S. President Barack Obama\'s rejection of the Keystone XL pipeline: "It sucks." The two 50-something men make their living maintaining equipment in the tiny, quiet town of Hardisty, Alta., where oilsands crude was to have begun its journey along Keystone XL.Jeff McIntosh / THE CANADIAN PRESS</p><p>The National Energy Board is doing an \u201cinadequate\u201d job of tracking the petroleum sector\u2019s compliance with pipeline approval conditions and significant problems remain in the regulator\u2019s reviews of companies\u2019 emergency procedures, Canada\u2019s environment commissioner says in a new report.</p><p>The Calgary-based NEB also has failed to improve public access to information on how companies are complying with conditions attached to pipeline approvals, while the board struggles with recruiting and retaining specialists in pipeline safety and regulatory compliance, Environment and Sustainable Development Commissioner Julie Gelfand says in her report released Tuesday.</p><p>A review of the NEB\u2019s oversight of federally regulated pipelines found several instances where the board isn\u2019t adequately fulfilling its duties, at the same time as $25 billion worth of pipeline megaprojects \u2013 such as Energy East, Trans Mountain expansion and Northern Gateway \u2013 have been approved or are under review by the regulator, the report says.</p><p>Federal Commissioner of the Environment and Sustainable Development Julie Gelfand.Ottawa Citizen</p><p>\u201cOur audit concluded that the Board did not adequately track companies\u2019 implementation of pipeline approval conditions, and that it was not consistently following up on company deficiencies. We found that the Board\u2019s tracking systems were outdated and inefficient,\u201d Gelfand said.</p><p>\u201cWith the anticipated increase of pipeline capacity and the coming into force of the Pipeline Safety Act by June 2016 (which gives the NEB new powers on emergency response), it is clear that the National Energy Board needs to do more to keep pace with the rapidly changing context in which it is operating.\u201d</p><p>The problems identified at the NEB come at a critical time for pipeline construction and energy development in Canada.</p><p>Just last week, Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre and 81 other mayors from surrounding municipalities came out in opposition to TransCanada\u2019s $15.7-billion Energy East pipeline, which would ship oilsands crude from northern Alberta to Quebec and on to New Brunswick.</p><p>They argued there was minimal economic gain for their region and unacceptable environmental risk. Prime Minister Justin Trudeau met Coderre on Tuesday morning to discuss the matter.</p>Related<p>Trudeau and the Liberal government have promised to introduce a new environmental assessment process for energy projects that has more teeth.</p><p>They also pledged in their platform to \u201cmodernize the National Energy Board\u201d to ensure it better reflects regional views, and has \u201csufficient expertise\u201d in areas like environmental science and community development.</p><p>The environment commissioner\u2019s audit found the NEB\u2019s tracking and documentation of pipeline approval conditions was adequate for about half (25 of 49) of the cases examined between 2000 and 2014.</p><p>But in the remaining 24 cases, the board\u2019s tracking was inaccurate or out of date, key documentation was missing, or the files lacked a final analysis and conclusion of whether the conditions had been fully satisfied, the report says.</p><p>In one case, the NEB didn\u2019t demonstrate that it has properly assessed or received all the required reports from a company on the employment, training and economic opportunities for aboriginal groups during the construction phase.</p><p>In another case, the commissioner\u2019s office found the NEB didn\u2019t track company compliance with a condition that required the firm to file a study examining the environmental effects of a possible pipeline rupture. The pipeline ran close to caribou habitat.</p><p>\u201cBoard records did not indicate whether this condition was satisfied, despite more than 10 years passing,\u201d the report says.</p><p>In another area, the environment commissioner\u2019s office found the NEB has improved its follow-up on company non-compliance, but that \u201cgaps remain.\u201d</p><p>In 22 of 42 cases examined by the environment commissioner, the NEB\u2019s follow-up was either inconsistent or improperly documented, the report says.</p><p>\u201cCompliance tracking information was out of date or inaccurate, the timeliness of the Board\u2019s follow-up varied, or the Board\u2019s analysis or conclusion on company compliance was missing,\u201d the report says.</p><p>Between 2012 and 2014, the National Energy Board conducted about 950 compliance verification activities, of which more than one-quarter (252 of 950) \u201cidentified deficiencies in company compliance.\u201d</p><p>The environment commissioner\u2019s office examined 42 of the 252 compliance deficiencies and found the NEB did not consistently or properly follow-up on compliance verification in more than half of the cases where there were issues (22 of the 42 cases examined).</p><p>The NEB faced \u201csignificant, system-wide challenges with the information management tools it used to track company compliance,\u201d the report says.</p><p>On emergency preparedness, the audit found the NEB faces \u201cimportant areas for improvement\u201d in fulfilling its role as the lead federal agency in pipeline emergency response.</p><p>The board has improved its review of company emergency procedures manuals, but one-third of them still need to be updated, the report says.</p><p>These emergency procedure manuals outline how each company will respond in the event of an emergency, such as a pipeline rupture.</p><p>\u201cThe Board must ensure that these manuals are complete and up to date, so that, in the event of an emergency, the relevant company has a clear response plan to follow,\u201d the report says.</p><p>The new Pipeline Safety Act that will come into force no later than June 2016 gives the NEB additional responsibilities in this area, including powers to take over emergency response in certain circumstances.</p><p>Compounding some of the problems is that the NEB is struggling to recruit and retain staff, particularly in the areas of safety and engineering.</p><p>The NEB expects the retirement rate in areas of safety and engineering could total about 25 per cent over the next five years.</p><p>\u201cThe Board identified these resource constraints as factors that could limit its ability to perform all its compliance verification and monitoring activities,\u201d the report says.</p><p>The environment commissioner made six recommendations to the NEB related to tracking company compliance, emergency preparedness, and attracting and retaining staff. The Board has accepted all of the recommendations.</p><p>jfekete@ottawacitizen.com</p><p>Twitter.com/jasonfekete</p><p>\xa0</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://ottawacitizen.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://ottawacitizen.com/news/national/national-energy-board-doing-inadequate-job-of-tracking-compliance-with-pipeline-approvals', u'author': u'Jason Fekete, Ottawa Citizen', u'original_article_url': u'http://ottawacitizen.com/news/national/national-energy-board-doing-inadequate-job-of-tracking-compliance-with-pipeline-approvals', u'scraped_on': 1453823231, u'published_date': u'Tue, 26 Jan 2016 15:33:03 +0000', u'title': u"National Energy Board doing 'inadequate' job of tracking compliance with pipeline approvals", u'_id': ObjectId('56a794ff1d41c87ba5c99e1a'), u'media_url': u'http://ottawacitizen.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>The mayor of Montreal\u2019s staunch public opposition to the Energy East pipeline could have backfired, one political expert warned.</p>&#13;<p>Denis Coderre, who also heads a coalition of 82 Montreal-area municipalities, triggered sharp criticism from politicians in western Canada last week when he announced his group believes the pipeline\u2019s environmental risks outweigh economic benefits.</p>&#13;<p>As his critics took to radio and television talk shows, Coderre defended his position in a newspaper op-ed distributed across the country.</p>&#13;<p>He argued the pipeline, which would move oilsands crude to the East Coast for export, would cross several major rivers and could threaten Montreal-area drinking water supplies if there was a leak or spill.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cIn a context where the Earth\u2019s nations are talking about even more restrictive measures to limit (greenhouse gas) emissions, we cannot justify the construction of a pipeline, which also delivers more risks than real profits,\u201d Coderre wrote.</p>&#13;<p>But Coderre and his coalition of mayors may have achieved the opposite of what they were hoping would emerge from their widely publicized line in the sand, said David Taras, a Mount Royal University professor.</p>&#13;<p>Their opposition triggered messages of support from much more important players in the pipeline debate: Prime Minister Justin Trudeau and Ontario Premier Kathleen Wynne, Taras said.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cThey understand the consequences for the economy and for the political nightmare that this could explode into,\u201d Taras said, noting that pipeline opposition could hurt Canada\u2019s economy.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cTrudeau and Wynne over the weekend I think were trying to cool things down and say, \u2018Let\u2019s get on with the process and ultimately we need pipelines.\u2019 \u201d</p>&#13;<p>Wynne gave tentative backing to Energy East, while Trudeau said pipeline projects can move ahead if environmental concerns are addressed.</p>&#13;<p>Calgary Mayor Naheed Nenshi said Coderre\u2019s position runs counter to broader ambitions for unity among the provinces.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cI don\u2019t think it\u2019s particularly helpful that on Friday, Mayor Coderre tweeted as though it matters that there are more people in Montreal than there are in Saskatchewan,\u201d Nenshi said on CBC Radio\u2019s political program The House.</p>&#13;<p>Proponents of Energy East have long pitched the pipeline that would pump Alberta crude to eastern Canadian refineries as a nation-building exercise.</p>&#13;<p>The pipeline would open market access for Alberta producers while sending eastern refineries crude that is cheaper than the oil they import from overseas, according to Calgary-based TransCanada Corp., the company behind the project.</p>&#13;<p>Nenshi denounced the \u201cpolitics of division\u201d he said has emerged from the pipeline dustup.</p>&#13;<p>\u201cEveryone in Alberta is concerned about emissions, is concerned about environmental sustainability,\u201d he said. \u201cPeople in Quebec are concerned about economic prosperity and would rather get their oil from their neighbours \u2026 than people in Venezuela and Saudi Arabia.\u201d</p>&#13;<p>Alberta\u2019s NDP government hopes to secure support for oilsands pipelines by ushering in a carbon tax designed to reduce emissions, but the Opposition Wildrose has argued the stance of Montreal-area mayors is evidence the attempt has failed.</p>&#13;<p>Anne McLellan, a former Liberal deputy prime Minister and natural resources minister, said Coderre and the rest of his coalition will have a chance to register their complaints during hearings before the National Energy Board, the regulator.</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p {u'full_article': u'<p>\xa0The federal government wants all cities and communities to weigh in on and approve national projects, such as TransCanada\u2019s proposed Energy East pipeline, before moving forward with them.</p><p>But at\xa0a news conference held at Montreal\u2019s city hall Tuesday morning, Prime Minister Justin Trudeau said it\u2019s up to TransCanada, not the federal government, to convince Canadians that the Energy East pipeline project is in the public\u2019s best interest. The government should act as a\xa0responsible arbitrator in the issue, he said.\xa0</p><p>Tuesday morning, Trudeau and Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre had their first official meeting since Trudeau\u2019s election.</p><p>Last week, Coderre and 81 other mayors from\xa0the\xa0Montreal\xa0Metropolitan Community\xa0came out against TransCanada\u2019s pipeline project, causing\xa0interim Conservative Leader Rona Ambrose, among others, to say Monday that Coderre\u2019s position was \u201caffecting national unity.\u201d</p><p>Tuesday\u2019s meeting, however, had been planned for weeks, Coderre said.</p><p>Asked if his objective is to change the views of Coderre and the other mayors, and whether he agrees with Ambrose\u2019s comments, Trudeau said his concern is to find a way to \u201cexport our resources in a responsible way.\u201d</p><p>\u201cIt\u2019s a conversation that takes place across the country, and my responsibility is to listen, collaborate, and engage with the different point of views,\u201d Trudeau said.\xa0</p><p>\u201cMy responsibility as prime minister is to make sure that on national projects, we\u2019re behaving in a way that contributes both to the economy and to a secure environment. It\u2019s to bring the people together and to create a better future for future generations.\u201d</p><p>Asked if the federal government could go forward with the project without having approval from the 82 mayors of the Montreal region, Trudeau said he\u2019s focused on establishing a process \u201cby which TransCanada, or any other company, can go show that its project is in the public\u2019s best interest and try to gain their approval.\u201d</p><p>Trudeau said a clear process will soon be in place that will evaluate the different environmental issues at play and give all Canadians \u2014 including the different levels of government, scientists and aboriginal communities \u2014 the chance to \u201chave their voices heard and participate in the decision making.\u201d</p>Related<p>Coderre reiterated his position, and said he\u2019s not being disrespectful by asking TransCanada \u201cto do its homework.\u201d</p><p>\u201cIt\u2019s a matter of clearly participating in the process,\u201d he said, \u201cAnd\xa0having a\xa0balanced approach between sustainable development and economic development.\u201d</p><p>Coderre and Trudeau said they discussed a number of other subjects in their meeting,\xa0including infrastructure, the Syrian refugee crisis, and Montreal\u2019s 375th anniversary in 2017.</p><p class="graf--p">jfeith@postmedia.com</p><p class="graf--p">Twitter.com/jessefeith</p><p></p><p>Canadian Prime Minister Justin Trudeau, left, and Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre address reporters at Montreal city hall, Jan. 26, 2016. They discussed, among other things, the mayor\u2019s opposition to the proposed Energy East pipeline.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/business/energy/justin-trudeau-denis-coderre-meet-against-backdrop-of-energy-east-tuesday', u'author': u'Jesse Feith, Montreal Gazette', u'original_article_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/business/energy/justin-trudeau-denis-coderre-meet-against-backdrop-of-energy-east-tuesday', u'scraped_on': 1453823126, u'published_date': u'Tue, 26 Jan 2016 15:30:14 +0000', u'title': u"Ottawa wants all communities' input on big projects, Trudeau tells Coderre", u'_id': ObjectId('56a794961d41c87ba5c997a1'), u'media_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>MONTREAL \u2014 The federal government\'s role is not to force cities such as Montreal to accept energy projects, Prime Minister Justin Trudeau said Tuesday, a day after his opponents demanded he tell the city\'s mayor to "smarten up" and accept the controversial Energy East pipeline.</p><p>Trudeau met with Denis Coderre for 45 minutes Tuesday to discuss a range of issues including the recent decision by the Montreal mayor and the organization that represents area municipalities to reject the 4,600-kilometre pipeline.</p><p>The prime minister said his government will create a review process whereby energy companies get a chance to prove to cities and towns across the country that a project is in their best interests.</p><p>"The responsibility of the federal government is to establish a clear process whereby people can evaluate the projects in a rigorous and open manner," Trudeau said.</p><p>He added the new review rules will include additional requirements forcing energy companies to "take into account all greenhouse gas emissions, including those upstream."</p><p>The Conservatives have accused Trudeau of lacking leadership on the energy file.</p><p>Opposition Leader Rona Ambrose says Trudeau should be championing pipelines and calling on Coderre and other mayors to accept Energy East to enable Alberta\'s oilsands to be able to be shipped overseas.</p><p>Conservative MP Candice Bergen told Trudeau in the Commons on Monday to "call Mr. Coderre and tell him to smarten up."</p><p>Trudeau showed no signs he told Coderre anything of the sort and added to reporters his government\'s new review process for pipelines and energy projects is on its way \u2014 but gave no timeline.</p><p>TransCanada (TSX:TRP) plans to build a pipeline from Alberta\'s oilsands, through Montreal, ending in New Brunswick, in order to open new markets.</p><p>The project would ship 1.1 million barrels of crude oil a day and allow Alberta\'s energy \u2014 which is currently landlocked \u2014 to be exported to international markets other than the United States.</p><p>Coderre and several other Quebec mayors came out last week against the project, arguing the environmental risks associated with it far outweigh the economic benefits.</p><p>That triggered a war of words with certain western Canadian politicians, including Saskatchewan Premier Brad Wall and Brian Jean, head of the Opposition Wildrose party in Alberta.</p><p>Coderre and the other mayors of Montreal\'s surrounding cities and towns say clean-up costs of a potential spill could reach $10 billion, while the city would only get around $2 million a year in economic benefits from the pipeline.</p><p>Rejecting Energy East is also partly a political decision, as surveys indicate the majority of Quebecers are against the project.</p><p>Coderre hinted Tuesday that if TransCanada were more forthcoming with details of the project and address the city\'s environmental concerns, his mind could be changed.</p><p>"If we have a role to play then people have work to do," he said. "We will participate fully in the (review) process. It\'s not because we are asking questions and we are asking people to do their homework that we are not being respectful.</p><p>"At the end of the day it\'s all about being respected. It\'s to be responsible and have a balanced approach between economic development and sustainable development and cities are playing that role to make sure we can get that."</p><p>Coderre pointed out that the Montreal area threw its support behind the Enbridge project because "they did their homework."</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/search/?c%5B%5D=news%2Fnational%2A&d=&d1=&d2=&f=rss&l=25&q=&s=start_time&sd=desc&t=article', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/news/national/pipelines-trudeau-says-his-job-is-to-bring-people-together/article_9db768f3-05e6-543a-9496-c0c5d9d80e4c.html', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/news/national/pipelines-trudeau-says-his-job-is-to-bring-people-together/article_9db768f3-05e6-543a-9496-c0c5d9d80e4c.html', u'scraped_on': 1453823270, u'published_date': u'Tue, 26 Jan 2016 09:09:05 -0500', u'title': u'Pipelines: Trudeau says his job is to bring people together', u'_id': ObjectId('56a795261d41c87ba5c9a0e7'), u'media_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>Guess who\'s going to dinner?</p>&#13;<p>In an unusual twist on Canada-U.S. relations, which for years have failed to live up to the stereotype of the world\'s two supposedly closest allies, a departing president with nothing to gain is having a rookie Canadian prime minister over for an honour that\'s become rare for us, a White House state dinner.</p>&#13;<p>U.S. President Barack Obama will roll out the red carpet for Prime Minister Justin Trudeau on March 10, evidently a hastily-arranged timeline for a function that the White House, on its website, says normally takes six months to prepare.</p>&#13;<p>South of the border, tongues have been wagging about which Canadian celebrities -- the U.S. does recognize some -- might be included on the guest list along with Trudeau, no stranger to celebrity himself despite his short months in office.</p>&#13;<p>North of the border, however, the attention paid to the upcoming gala has been more sober, noting, for example, that it\'s been 19 years since a Canadian prime minister was last feted at the White House.</p>&#13;<p>Indeed, it\'s been so long since a glass was hoisted to a PM at 1600\xa0Pennsylvania Ave., that one guest bound to be there -- Hillary Clinton, the presumptive Democratic nominee for the fall presidential race -- was the last First Lady to do so, when she and then-president Bill Clinton welcomed Jean Chretien in 1997.</p>&#13;<p>Cut through the polite dinner talk, and that should be worriesome to Canadians.</p>&#13;<p>State dinners are more than just a meal; they\'re an instrument of diplomacy and power. They have been since 1874, when Ulysses S. Grant held the first foreign state dinner for the king of Hawaii, a sovereign oceanic state the U.S. was already engulfing. Only those who matter to the Americans get invited.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau, at this stage, is more selfie than statesman, so it\'s not his diplomatic skills Obama wants. More likely, it\'s one liberal leader wanting to introduce another to those he hopes will succeed him.</p>&#13;<p>Still, there\'s no shortage of issues that need attention between Ottawa and Washington: a fragile economy, global trade, the energy sector, the war against ISIS, among them.</p>&#13;<p>On many files, our interests don\'t intersect, a point underlined this week as Canadian energy giant TransCanada filed a lawsuit against the Obama administration for rejecting its proposed Keystone XL oilsands pipeline to the Gulf Coast.</p>&#13;<p>Make no mistake, this should be a long overdue working meal.</p>&#13;<p>Bon appetit.</p>&#13;<p>-- Postmedia Network</p><p class="clearfix ads-container"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.lfpress.com/opinion/editorial/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.lfpress.com/2016/01/07/state-dinner-chatter-should-be-interesting', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.lfpress.com/2016/01/07/state-dinner-chatter-should-be-interesting', u'scraped_on': 1453823446, u'published_date': u'Thu, 07 Jan 2016 19:08:35 EST', u'title': u'State dinner chatter should be interesting', u'_id': ObjectId('56a795da1d41c87ba5c9b11b'), u'media_url': u'http://www.lfpress.com/'}

government is to establish a clear process whereby people can evaluate the projects in a rigorous and open manner," Trudeau said.</p><p>He added the new review rules will include additional requirements forcing energy companies to "take into account all greenhouse gas emissions, including those upstream."</p><p>The Conservatives have accused Trudeau of lacking leadership on the energy file.</p><p>Opposition Leader Rona Ambrose says Trudeau should be championing pipelines and calling on Coderre and other mayors to accept Energy East to enable Alberta\'s oilsands to be able to be shipped overseas.</p><p>Conservative MP Candice Bergen told Trudeau in the Commons on Monday to "call Mr. Coderre and tell him to smarten up."</p><p>Trudeau showed no signs he told Coderre anything of the sort and added to reporters his government\'s new review process for pipelines and energy projects is on its way -- but gave no timeline.</p><p>TransCanada (TSX:TRP) plans to build a pipeline from Alberta\'s oilsands, through Montreal, ending in New Brunswick, in order to open new markets.</p><p>The project would ship 1.1 million barrels of crude oil a day and allow Alberta\'s energy -- which is currently landlocked -- to be exported to international markets other than the United States.</p><p>Coderre and several other Quebec mayors came out last week against the project, arguing the environmental risks associated with it far outweigh the economic benefits.</p><p>That triggered a war of words with certain western Canadian politicians, including Saskatchewan Premier Brad Wall and Brian Jean, head of the Opposition Wildrose party in Alberta.</p><p>Coderre and the other mayors of Montreal\'s surrounding cities and towns say clean-up costs of a potential spill could reach $10 billion, while the city would only get around $2 million a year in economic benefits from the pipeline.</p><p>Rejecting Energy East is also partly a political decision, as surveys indicate the majority of Quebecers are against the project.</p><p>Coderre hinted Tuesday that if TransCanada were more forthcoming with details of the project and address the city\'s environmental concerns, his {u'full_article': u'<p class="articlerelatedphotogallery section"></p><p class="articlerelatedvideogallery section"></p><p itemprop="articleBody">In the wake of British Columbia\u2019s recent announcement that it cannot support Kinder Morgan\u2019s proposed Trans Mountain pipeline expansion, conservative pundits and oil companies are turning their sights to TransCanada\u2019s Energy East pipeline and tanker proposal. But there\u2019s no reason to believe that Energy East \u2014 the last pipe plan standing \u2014 will get built.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">By opposing Kinder Morgan, B.C. Premier Christy Clark has shown that her priority is protecting the best interests of the people of B.C. Ontario should learn from this.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">B.C.\u2019s opposition to the proposal was based on five conditions, ranging from spill response to First Nations consultation, to guaranteeing economic benefits for the people of B.C. Kinder Morgan failed to meet these conditions, especially when it came to putting in place a \u201cworld class\u201d spill prevention system and emergency response plan.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Oil companies and politicians are now shifting their focus to the last mega-pipeline proposal on the table \u2014 Energy East. But its prospects look even worse than Kinder Morgan\u2019s.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">On Thursday Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre, on behalf of 82 Montreal-area mayors, voiced official opposition to the proposal. After an extensive review and public consultation, Montreal municipal leaders said the potential risks outweigh any possible economic benefits to communities. The review showed that the project could have unacceptable social, economic, environmental and public security impacts.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Like B.C. Premier Clark and the Montreal mayors, Quebec Premier Philippe Couillard set out clear conditions that Energy East would need to meet in order to win the province\u2019s approval. Ontario Premier Kathleen Wynne did the same thing back in 2014 \u2014 even though on Friday she ignored that Energy East fails to meet any of the reasonable conditions her own government put in place to safeguard Ontario residents. And it doesn\u2019t look as though TransCanada will be able to meet those conditions.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Among the criteria is the need for world-class contingency plans and an emergency response program. But the Ontario Energy Board, in its review of Energy East, decried that emergency response plans were either inadequate or not available. Guaranteeing the safety of Energy East is no small feat given that the pipeline would traverse the entire breadth of Ontario and much of Quebec while crossing nearly 1,000 rivers and lakes. Add to this that much of the proposed Energy East route wasn\u2019t built to carry tarsands oil but lower-pressure natural gas. An oil pipeline would never have been allowed so close to so many bodies of water.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">There are serious concerns around what happens when the pipeline inevitably leaks, especially after the U.S. National Academy of Sciences released a landmark study just last month that shows pipelines carrying tarsands oil (diluted bitumen) need special spill response measures that don\u2019t yet exist.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Especially damning is the Ontario Energy Board\u2019s top-line finding: the environmental risks of Energy East outweigh the potential benefits to Ontarians. The board found that economic benefits projected for the province may have been over-estimated, and \u201cany claims about substantial GDP growth and job creation in Ontario from pipeline construction should be viewed critically.\u201d </p><p itemprop="articleBody">Another unfulfilled provincial condition: properly consult with First Nations, which the Chiefs of Ontario have already formally noted. This is a serious matter. The B.C. Supreme Court just found in favour of the Coastal First Nations challenging the Northern Gateway pipeline project on exactly this \u2014 lack of proper consultation.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">There\u2019s also the public: the majority of Ontarians oppose Energy East. And for good reason, Lake Temagami, Lake Superior and the Ottawa River are no less deserving of protection than the Great Bear Rainforest.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">On top of all this, the federal government has promised a new, meaningful review process for all energy projects. The National Energy Board hasn\u2019t started to review the Energy East proposal, so it should be put through this new, strengthened process.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">All this suggests that Energy East\u2019s fate will be much like that of Kinder Morgan: the Ontario government must oppose the pipeline, just like the B.C. government said no to the Trans Mountain project. Based on recent history, this is the most likely outcome.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">It\u2019s time to let go of the pipe dream and instead speed up Canada\u2019s transition to a modern, clean energy economy. Premier Wynne, time to make your move.</p><p data-type="endnote"><p itemprop="articleBody">Keith Brooks is Clean Economy Director at Environmental Defence.</p></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/feeds.articles.opinion.rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/opinion/commentary/2016/01/24/time-for-kathleen-wynne-to-take-a-stand-against-energy-east.html', u'author': u'Keith Brooks', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/opinion/commentary/2016/01/24/time-for-kathleen-wynne-to-take-a-stand-against-energy-east.html', u'scraped_on': 1453823439, u'published_date': u'Sun, 24 Jan 2016 04:30:00 EST', u'title': u'Time for Kathleen Wynne to take a stand against Energy East', u'_id': ObjectId('56a795cf1d41c87ba5c9afd6'), u'media_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>Prime Minister Justin Trudeau says the new federal government wants to oversee a full and fair national debate over controversial energy issues such as pipelines and will act as a \u201cresponsible mediator.\u201d</p><p>The Liberals intend to see that all aspects of energy and resource proposals are carefully examined with the interests of all concerned, including First Nations communities, Mr. Trudeau said Tuesday after meeting with Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre, who has voiced strong opposition to Calgary-based TransCanada Corp.\u2019s Energy East pipeline.</p><p class="videoInfo videoInfo--border videoInfo-headline">Justin Trudeau aims to bring politicians together over pipelines (CP Video)</p><p>\u201cIt\u2019s not up to the federal government to decide in advance which projects it wants to do or what it wants to promote. We have had over the past 10 years a government that acted like a cheerleader on these projects rather than being a responsible mediator setting up a clear, open, rigorous and transparent process and that\u2019s what we\u2019re going to do,\u201d Mr. Trudeau told reporters after a 45-minute meeting with Mr. Coderre.</p><p>But he stressed the importance of stringent environmental reviews. \u201cCanadians know you can\u2019t build a strong economy without protecting the environment,\u201d Mr. Trudeau said, adding that his government plans to strengthen existing assessment processes.</p><p>\u201cThe federal government\u2019s role is to put in place a process through which TransCanada or any other company might proceed to demonstrate that a project is in the public interest and get Canadians\u2019 approval.\u201d</p><p>\u201cWe will both build on previous work done by the processes that did exist and we will ensure that in the process going forward we take into account all greenhouse gas emissions, including those upstream,\u201d he told reporters.</p><p>The federal government intends to require a separate climate test for proposed pipelines and a planned LNG export terminal, which are now under regulatory review, to determine their impact on Canada\u2019s greenhouse gas emissions, possibly resulting in new delays on major projects.</p><p>Mr. Coderre said he welcomes Mr. Trudeau\u2019s approach, which takes sustainable development into account, but stressed that it\u2019s up to the proposing companies to do their homework and address public concerns over environmental risks.</p><p>Last week, Mr. Coderre \u2013 a former federal Liberal cabinet minister \u2013 and 81 Montreal area mayors condemned the $15.7-billion Energy East pipeline, which would move 1.1 million barrels of Western crude through Quebec and on to a refinery in Saint John, N.B., saying it presents an environmental risk.</p><p>The move provoked outrage from Western Canadian leaders, including Alberta Premier Rachel Notley, Saskatchewan Premier Brad Wall and Calgary Mayor Naheed Nenshi.</p><p>With a file from Shawn McCarthy</p><p class="article__ontwitter">Follow
