opponents demanded he tell the city\u2019s mayor to \u201csmarten up\u201d and accept the controversial Energy East pipeline.</p><p>Trudeau met with Montreal mayor Denis Coderre for 45 minutes Tuesday to discuss a range of issues including the recent decision by the Montreal mayor and the organization that represents area municipalities to reject the 4,600-kilometre pipeline.</p><p>The prime {u'full_article': u'<p>Dear reader, I owe you an apology. I\'ve been writing this column on sustainability for nearly two years now and I\'ve yet to define what sustainability means, exactly.</p><p>A close friend who\'s evidently not a regular reader of mine told me how interesting he found a recent column. But he quickly - if a little sheepishly - added that "sustainability" seems to be a bit of a buzz word that gets used in a number of different ways, not all of them reconcilable.</p><p>He\'s not wrong. Not entirely, anyway.</p><p>For one, lots of folks whose actions appear utterly unsustainable proudly bandy the word about.</p><p>Take TransCanada Pipelines, the company behind the Keystone XL and Energy East oil pipeline projects. It might surprise you to learn that TransCanada was named to the Dow Jones Sustainability World Index for the 12th consecutive year in 2013. The Dow Jones sustainability index purports to measure the stock performance of the world\'s leading companies in terms of economic, environmental and social criteria.</p><p>According to estimates prepared by the Pembina Institute, the crude production needed to fill the Energy East pipeline would generate an additional 30 to 32 million tonnes of carbon emissions each year - the equivalent of adding more than seven million cars to Canada\'s roads (pembina.org/pub/2519). How can a company proposing such a project be lauded as "sustainable"?</p><p>Or take our federal government, which also happens to be TransCanada\'s biggest cheerleader. In 2008, the Harper government simultaneously passed and then erased from its memory the Federal Sustainable Development Act, my favourite law.</p><p>Why\'s it my favourite? Section 5 of the Act proclaims that "[t]he Government of Canada accepts the basic principle that sustainable development is based on an ecologically efficient use of natural, social and economic resources and acknowledges the need to integrate environmental, economic and social factors in the making of all decisions by government."</p><p>How great would Canada be if the government integrated environmental, economic and social factors when making policy decisions?</p><p>But I digress. The point I really want to make is this: Just because some misuse and abuse the term, sustainability is still a useful concept.</p><p>The term was first defined in 1987 in what is known as the Brundtland Report, which defined "sustainable development" as "development that meets the needs of the present without compromising the ability of future generations to meet their own needs."</p><p>OK, you say, that\'s a start, but that definition seems to raise as many questions as it answers. What are our "needs," exactly? And what will be the "needs" of future generations, who can\'t yet even speak for themselves? How do we sort this out?</p><p>There\'s certainly no shortage of proposals at the moment. In fact, we seem to have entered a new phase in environmental law and policy. I call it the manifesto phase, because it seems like there\'s a new manifesto released every other week proclaiming a solution to the sustainability riddle.</p><p>Last month, for example, over 70 Canadian researchers released Acting on Climate Change: Solutions from Canadian Scholars, which I\'ve already discussed in a couple of recent columns (www.sustainablecanadadialogues.ca/en/endorsement).</p><p>Not to be outdone, another Canadian group, which styles itself as Canada\'s Ecofiscal Commission, released a report earlier this month called The Way Forward: A Practical Approach to Reducing Canada\'s Greenhouse Gas Emissions (www.ecofiscal.ca/wayforward/).</p><p>At the same time, yet another manifesto - An Ecomodernist Manifesto (www.ecomodernism.org/) - seeks to liven things up by outright rejecting the ostensibly responsible idea that we have to harmonize with nature to avoid economic and ecological collapse, which, to be fair, would certainly put a damper on things.</p><p>Instead, the authors of the Ecomodernist Manifesto argue that "[i]ntensifying many human activities - particularly farming, energy extraction, forestry, and settlement - so that they use less land and interfere less with the natural world is the key to decoupling human development from environmental impacts. These socioeconomic and technological processes are central to economic modernization and environmental protection. Together they allow people to mitigate climate change, to spare nature, and to alleviate global poverty."</p><p>And coming soon, just in time for the summer blockbuster season, is Pope Francis\' highly anticipated encyclical on climate change, which will follow from the conference being held later this month in the Vatican City entitled Protect the Earth, Dignify Humanity: The Moral Dimensions of Climate Change and Sustainable Development (www.endslavery.va/content/endslavery/en/events/protect.html).</p><p>Of all the recent sustainability manifestos I\'ve cited, the Pope\'s encyclical may be the most important. More than any of the others, the Pope\'s encyclical is expected to highlight the social dimensions of sustainability, including the moral responsibility of everyone - world leaders, corporations and ordinary citizens alike - to become involved in imagining what exactly a sustainable future might look like.</p><p>Jason MacLean teaches environmental law at Lakehead University and writes here biweekly.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/search/?c%5B%5D=opinion%2Fcolumns%2Fjason_maclean%2A&d=&d1=&d2=&f=rss&l=25&q=&s=start_time&sd=desc&t=article', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/opinion/columns/jason_maclean/what-is-sustainability-anyway/article_9655a2f2-e77e-11e4-97ae-6ffaa69af070.html', u'author': u'SUSTAINABILITY MATTERS by Jason MacLean', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/opinion/columns/jason_maclean/what-is-sustainability-anyway/article_9655a2f2-e77e-11e4-97ae-6ffaa69af070.html', u'scraped_on': 1453823259, u'published_date': u'Mon, 20 Apr 2015 08:00:00 -0400', u'title': u'What is sustainability, anyway?', u'_id': ObjectId('56a7951b1d41c87ba5c9a072'), u'media_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p class="articlerelatedphotogallery section"></p><p class="articlerelatedvideogallery section"></p><p itemprop="articleBody">The end of oil. A worthless currency. Could the apocalyptic economic news get any worse?</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The Star last week quoted one investment advisor calling for a 55 cent dollar. He\u2019s not alone. Oil will soon be in the teens, goes another prediction. It\u2019s already under $10 a barrel for bitumen \u2014 the gooey, tar-like stuff that was to move through TransCanada Corp.\u2019s Keystone pipeline.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The pessimism has a global reach. A front page headline last week in the usually sober Wall Street Journal: \u201cStocks take a beating as alarm grows.\u201d A business page article in the London Guardian: \u201cSell everything, ahead of a stock market crash, say economists at the Royal Bank of Scotland.\u201d</p><p itemprop="articleBody">There\u2019s an old saying that at the point of maximum pessimism the worst is over. The effects may linger and public perception lags the reality, but things won\u2019t fall much more.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Is this that time? We won\u2019t know except in hindsight, but there are signs that the pros are starting to position themselves for the next upward economic cycle. David Wolf, a Princeton-trained economist who between 2009 and 2013 served as adviser to the Governor of the Bank of Canada, doesn\u2019t see a global recession. He thinks oil and the dollar have fallen so quickly, the odds are for a rebound, not a steeper drop.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">\u201cThe farther and faster oil and the dollar goes down, the faster they will come back into balance,\u201d Wolf said in an interview.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Before his Bank of Canada days, Wolf was head of Canadian economics and chief strategist at investment house Merrill Lynch. He is currently a portfolio manager at Fidelity Investments, where he is making decisions on where to invest. He\u2019s increasing holdings of Canadian shares.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Here\u2019s an edited version of our conversation:</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Q: If oil makes up less than 15 per cent of the economy, why is the price collapse hurting us somuch?</p><p itemprop="articleBody">A: The income of all Canadians has dropped because the oil, gas and the commodities we sell aren\u2019t worth as much. So we are poorer.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Companies pump less oil, profits fall and people lose their jobs. That affects Tim Horton\u2019s in Fort McMurray and banks in Calgary and the real estate agent trying to sell a house in Alberta. Manufacturers in Ontario and Quebec who, with a strong dollar, switched to things that oil and gas producers need, now sell less.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Folks on the East Coast who went out west for work and brought the money home don\u2019t have those jobs.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">That\u2019s why the spillover effect is much broader than the industry. What appears to be a small part of the economy can have a big effect.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Q: Where are oil and the dollar going?</p><p itemprop="articleBody">A: I\u2019m no longer a forecaster and it\u2019s frankly a mug\u2019s game trying to pick levels.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Sentiment is getting more extreme and when sentiment gets to an extreme, that\u2019s the best time to buy.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Q: What about share prices?</p><p itemprop="articleBody">A: Last year, we got on the side of the boat that said it was going to get worse. Now, everyone has joined us and we\u2019re shifting back to the other side.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">A year ago, we were at the maximum underweight on Canadian assets and the dollar. Now we\u2019re only a third or a half of that. We\u2019re accumulating them now.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Q: Why are stocks selling off so much? </p><p itemprop="articleBody">A:People get afraid and that exacerbates things and they can overshoot badly. That\u2019s where we see opportunities.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">We don\u2019t see a global recession. The U.S. is doing fine. Europe is doing better. Not great, but not terrible. U.S. stock price to earnings ratios are higher, but not at a global peak.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">A: I\u2019m not in the business of making forecasts, but of looking at probabilities. But the fact that both (the dollar and oil) have fallen so quickly means it could be sharp, but short.</p><p class="text combinedtext parbase section"> </p><p itemprop="articleBody">Wolf\u2019s last point doesn\u2019t mean that there are no lurches ahead. Even at the bottom prices bounce with a relief rally one day, followed by losses the next.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">News normally seen as positive is ignored while bad news is magnified beyond its real impact. That seems to be where we are now. At some point, that changes, but until then expect a bumpy ride.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Adam Mayers writes about investing and personal finance on Tuesdays and Thursdays. Have a question? Reach him at amayers@thestar.ca </p><p itemprop="articleBody">Canada\u2019s 3 economic energizers in 2016</p><p class="text combinedtext parbase section"> </p><p itemprop="articleBody">Our economy has a lot of promise that\u2019s being overlooked as oil and the dollar fall, says Paul Bosse, a member of the Investment Strategy Group at the giant Vanguard fund company, based in Pennsylvania.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Bosse advises pension funds and large institutions about their mix of stocks and bonds and he\u2019s bullish on Canada.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">\u201cThere\u2019s a lot depth that\u2019s being overlooked,\u201d said Bosse, in Toronto for a briefing with Vanguard clients and Canadian staff.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">He sees three energizers: A low dollar making exports more attractive as the U.S. economy picks up, a central bank that may drop rates further, and Ottawa\u2019s plans to spend on infrastructure, which will encourage demand.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Bosse said our dollar fell 16 per cent against the U.S. dollar in 2015 and Canada created 35,000 manufacturing jobs as a result. He says the federal government\u2019s balance sheet is strong, which means it can support infrastructure spending.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">He doesn\u2019t see a recession and predicts economic growth in the 1 to 1.5 per cent range for the year.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">\u201cWith that as a backdrop, it\u2019s not a bad picture going forward,\u201d Bosse said.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/feeds.articles.business.personal_finance.rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/business/personal_finance/investing/2016/01/18/why-our-outlook-may-be-better-than-we-think-mayers.html', u'author': u'Adam Mayers - Personal Finance Editor', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/business/personal_finance/investing/2016/01/18/why-our-outlook-may-be-better-than-we-think-mayers.html', u'scraped_on': 1453823436, u'published_date': u'Mon, 18 Jan 2016 13:53:29 EST', u'title': u'Why our outlook may be better than we think: Mayers', u'_id': ObjectId('56a795cd1d41c87ba5c9af60'), u'media_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>CALGARY \u2014 Alberta Premier Rachel Notley has said she wants the discussion over pipelines to be "drama free," but this week it was anything but.</p><p>A coalition of Montreal-area mayors came out against the $15.7-billion Energy East Pipeline on Thursday, setting off a bout of cross-Canada sniping between municipal and provincial politicians over a matter that falls within federal jurisdiction.</p><p>Notley said it was "short-sighted" for the Montreal Metropolitan Community to oppose Energy East on the grounds its risks outweigh its economic benefit.</p><p>Other Western politicians had harsher words.</p><p>"I trust Montreal-area mayors will politely return their share of $10B in equalization supported by West," Saskatchewan Premier Brad Wall tweeted.</p><p>Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre retorted with the respective populations of metropolitan Montreal versus Saskatchewan: four million compared to 1.13 million.</p><p>Meanwhile, Brian Jean, head of Alberta\'s Opposition Wildrose party, blasted Coderre for allowing raw sewage to be dumped into the St. Lawrence River, but opposing Energy East on environmental grounds.</p><p>Calgary Mayor Naheed Nenshi made a similar point at the World Economic Forum in Davos, Switzerland: "I could make a joke about how well Montreal protects that particular waterway over the last several months, but I won\'t."</p><p>"I\'ve got a lot of respect for Mayor Coderre. On this, however, he\'s wrong," said Nenshi. "Certainly he needs to hold out for a better deal for himself... that\'s what mayors tend to do. But the deal has to be within reach."</p><p>The tone between Notley and her Ontario counterpart was much more congenial at a news conference Friday.</p><p>Premier Kathleen Wynne praised the climate change initiatives of Alberta\'s NDP government, saying those efforts are making "the national conversation about climate targets and pipelines easier."</p><p>Many of Ontario\'s conditions for supporting the pipeline are starting to be addressed, Wynne added.</p><p>Energy East, proposed by Calgary-based TransCanada Corp. (TSX:TRP), would take up to 1.1 million barrels a day of Alberta crude as far east as an Irving Oil refinery and export terminal in Saint John, N.B.</p><p>In Davos, Prime Minister Justin Trudeau encouraged everyone to work together.</p><p>"I\'m very much in the camp of both premiers Wynne and Notley, who demonstrated that Canada can and should work together on eco issues for all of us. That\'s the focus that I\'ve always taken \u2014 collaborating, respectful, working together to solve the challenges that are facing all Canadians."</p><p>Trevor McLeod, director of the centre for natural resources policy at the Canada West Foundation, said the sub-national pipeline spat is frustrating to watch when there are bigger issues to deal with \u2014 like the U.S. going from Canada\'s biggest customer to its biggest competitor.</p><p>"There\'s big stuff going on right now and we\'re playing this parochial game in Canada about who gets what," he said. "Are we a country or not? If you can\'t get product through the other provinces to global markets, I don\'t think we can sustain this notion that we\'re going to be a trading nation."</p><p>The debate over pipelines has become the "trickiest national unity issue in Canada" over the past five years or so, said Sean Kheraj, a York University historian focused on Canada\'s approach to energy and the environment.</p><p>Similar East-versus-West quarrels have erupted in the past. But the lines of division have flipped.</p><p>Amid the 1970s oil shocks, Ottawa wanted a pipeline to send western crude east to ensure a steady supply of crude and offered subsidies to Interprovincial Pipe Lines, the forerunner of Enbridge Inc. (TSX:ENB), to get it done. Alberta opposed the move because producers could sell oil for a higher price to the Americans than domestically.</p><p>The prime minister at the time \u2014 Pierre Trudeau, the father of the current prime minister \u2014 took a much more hands-on approach to energy issues, said Kheraj.</p><p>"That\'s the irony. In the 1970s, Interprovincial got dragged kicking and screaming into building this pipeline to Montreal and today we\'ve got TransCanada, Kinder Morgan and Enbridge banging down the door trying to get these pipelines built."</p><p>Mount Royal University political scientist Keith Brownsey said Ottawa has the power to declare Energy East a "national project" and approve it over local objections.</p><p>But whether the Liberal government chooses to do so is another matter.</p><p>"I think there will be a reluctance on the part of Mr. Trudeau \u2014 as there was on the part of Mr. Harper \u2014 to make those decisions."</p><p>Follow @LaurenKrugel on Twitter.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/search/?c%5B%5D=news%2Fnational%2Cnews%2Fnational%2F%2A&d=&d1=&d2=&f=rss&l=100&nsa=eedition&q=&s=start_time&sd=desc&t=article', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/news/national/debate-over-pipelines-anything-but-drama-free-as-energy-east/article_e5fbcaa6-bf4f-5edf-80bb-ac896e32492c.html', u'author': u'Lauren Krugel The Canadian Press', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/news/national/debate-over-pipelines-anything-but-drama-free-as-energy-east/article_e5fbcaa6-bf4f-5edf-80bb-ac896e32492c.html', u'scraped_on': 1453833291, u'published_date': u'Fri, 22 Jan 2016 17:36:27 -0500', u'title': u"Debate over pipelines anything but 'drama free' as Energy East debate heats up", u'_id': ObjectId('56a7bc4f1d41c804c420e7d5'), u'media_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/'}

L\u2019heure est venue pour les provinces de commencer \xe0 se demander ce que le Canada peut y gagner et pas seulement ce qu\u2019elles ont \xe0 y gagner individuellement.\xbb</p><p> Touchant, non?</p><p> Eh non, justement, pas tout \xe0 fait...</p><p> Les probl\xe8mes en Alberta sont en effet s\xe9rieux \u2013 baisse du dollar, chute du prix du p\xe9trole, hausse du ch\xf4mage, baisse l\u2019immobilier, etc. Impossible de le nier.  Mais cet appel qui vise \xe0 culpabiliser les opposants \xe0 \xc9nergie Est au Qu\xe9bec en les faisant passer pour des \xe9go\xefstes mesquins de la pire esp\xe8ce incapables de se sacrifier pour le bien de l\u2019Alberta et de l\u2019unit\xe9 canadienne, c\u2019est trop fort de caf\xe9.</p><p> Si seulement la m\xeame \xablogique\xbb s\u2019\xe9tait appliqu\xe9e au Canada anglais \xe0 l\u2019Accord du Lac Meech, cet appel aurait peut-\xeatre un peu plus de cr\xe9dibilit\xe9 aujourd\u2019hui.</p><p> ***</p><p> Tenez, faisons le test ensemble.</p><p> Reprenons les citations de Rick Mercer et adaptons-les \xe0 l\u2019Accord du Lac Meech. Donc, retournons dans le temps comme si nous \xe9tions dans les derniers souffles d'agonie de l'Accord, disons, au printemps 1990, alors que les pires \xe2neries alarmistes et francophobes se disaient sur les pr\xe9sum\xe9s dangers qu'il y aurait eu \xe0 accorder au Qu\xe9bec un simple statut de \xabsoci\xe9t\xe9 distincte\xbb...</p><p> \xabAu risque de sonner comme quelqu\u2019un qui aime profond\xe9ment la f\xe9d\xe9ration nationale, chers compatriotes canadiens-anglais oppos\xe9s \xe0 l\u2019Accord du Lac Meech, tentez de vous rentrer ceci dans la t\xeate : cette question n\u2019a rien \xe0 voir avec Toronto, Calgary ou St-Jean \xe0 Terre-Neuve. Cela n\u2019a rien \xe0 voir avec le Manitoba. C\u2019est \xe0 propos d\u2019une partie francophone du Canada qui tente d\u2019obtenir sa reconnaissance comme soci\xe9t\xe9 distincte dans la constitution canadienne.\xbb  Et pourquoi le faire?</p><p> \xabParce que nous formons un pays. Nous sommes tous dans le m\xeame bain ensemble. C\u2019est extraordinairement d\xe9cent. C\u2019est extraordinairement canadien. Alors, avec tout le respect pour nos compatriotes du Canada anglais, le Qu\xe9bec attend cela depuis tr\xe8s, tr\xe8s longtemps. Aujourd\u2019hui, maintenant, il souffre de votre refus. Nous avons tous besoin de cet Accord. L\u2019heure est venue pour les provinces du ROC (Rest of Canada) de commencer \xe0 se demander ce que le Canada peut y gagner et pas seulement ce qu\u2019elles ont \xe0 y gagner individuellement.\xbb</p><p>  Comme quoi, comme disent les Anglais, it takes two to tango... Que ce soit pour la constitution ou pour vendre du p\xe9trole...</p><p> Et comme quoi, le concept m\xeame d'\xab\xe9go\xefsme\xbb r\xe9gional est d'une extr\xeame subjectivit\xe9...</p><p> ***</p><p> \xa0</p><p> Addendum:</p><p> 1) Juste au moment o\xf9 on croyait avoir tout entendu sur le dossier \xc9nergie Est et ce chantage \xe0 la p\xe9r\xe9quation, voil\xe0 que la CAQ se dit d'accord:</p><p> \xabC\u2019est une v\xe9rit\xe9 qui d\xe9range quand on se fait dire, oui, nous recevons 10,5 milliards $ de p\xe9r\xe9quation ann\xe9e apr\xe8s ann\xe9e\xbb, a reconnu le leader parlementaire Fran\xe7ois Bonnardel lors d\u2019une m\xeal\xe9e de presse en marge du caucus pr\xe9sessionnel de la CAQ, qui se termine aujourd\u2019hui \xe0 Bromont.\xbb</p><p> Une \xabv\xe9rit\xe9 qui d\xe9range\xbb? Mis\xe8re...</p><p> 2) Pour visionner la vid\xe9o de la visite de Rick Mercer au Carnaval de Qu\xe9bec en 2007 alors qu'il \xe9tait accompagn\xe9 de Justin Trudeau et Sophie Gr\xe9goire, c'est ici. Rick Mercer semblait appr\xe9cier le Qu\xe9bec un tantinet plus \xe0 l'\xe9poque...</p><p> \xa0</p><p> \xa0</p><p> \xa0</p><p> \xa0</p><p> \xa0</p>", u'rss_url': u'http://www.journaldemontreal.com/rss.xml', u'language': u'fr', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.journaldemontreal.com/2016/01/26/du-lac-meech-au-pipeline-energie-est', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.journaldemontreal.com/2016/01/26/du-lac-meech-au-pipeline-energie-est', u'scraped_on': 1453833463, u'published_date': u'Tue, 26 Jan 2016 11:26:12 EST', u'title': u'Du Lac Meech au pipeline \xc9nergie Est...', u'_id': ObjectId('56a7bcfe1d41c804c420f5a5'), u'media_url': u'http://www.journaldemontreal.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>CALGARY \u2014 Pipeline proposals just can\'t catch a break these days.</p><p>The latest hurdle came this week when an organization representing 82 Montreal-area municipalities came out against TransCanada Corp.\'s Energy East Pipeline, drawing a sharp rebuke from politicians in Alberta and Saskatchewan.</p><p>Here is an update on the status of other major Canadian pipeline projects:</p><p>Keystone XL:</p><p>TransCanada, the same company behind Energy East, applied for U.S. permission to build its Keystone XL pipeline in September 2008. The idea was to expand an existing cross-border pipeline to give oilsands crude a more direct route to U.S. Gulf Coast refineries.</p><p>At the time, TransCanada thought XL would proceed through the regulatory process just as smoothly as the previous Keystone phases. It was wrong.</p><p>The stretch of pipe cutting a diagonal line from the Saskatchewan-Montana border to southern Nebraska became the focal point of the U.S. environmental movement and a political lightning rod in Washington.</p><p>Debate over Keystone XL centred not only on the environmental effects on the American Heartland in the event of a spill, but on its broader role in climate change. Stop pipelines, the environmental groups argued, and you stop the development of carbon-intensive oilsands crude.</p><p>After a seven-year regulatory saga, U.S. President Barack Obama rejected Keystone XL in November \u2014 weeks before United Nations climate change talks in Paris.</p><p>TransCanada set in motion a US$15-billion challenge under the North American Free Trade Agreement arguing it was treated inequitably. It has also launched a separate federal lawsuit seeking a declaration that Obama overstepped his constitutional power when he rejected Keystone XL.</p><p>TransCanada maintains it has not given up on Keystone XL.</p><p>Northern Gateway:</p><p>Enbridge\'s Northern Gateway pipeline would ship 525,000 barrels a day of oilsands crude from northeast of Edmonton to Kitimat, B.C. Its goal is to sell Alberta crude in lucrative Asian markets. A parallel line would bring 193,000 barrels a day of bitumen-thinning diluent in the opposite direction.</p><p>Northern Gateway has been hugely controversial. The idea of crude-oil laden supertankers navigating the choppy waters of the Douglas Channel on their way out to the Pacific is a non-starter for many British Columbians. The line also passes through huge tracts of unceded First Nations territory in British Columbia. Though Enbridge has signed agreements with many First Nations along the route, many \u2014 especially on the coast \u2014 are staunchly opposed.</p><p>Enbridge has had a federal permit to build Northern Gateway since mid-2014, but recent developments have caused critics to declare Northern Gateway "dead in the water."</p><p>The federal Liberal government has signalled it intends to formalize a tanker ban on B.C.\'s north coast.</p><p>A recent B.C. Supreme Court ruling found the province did not properly consult First Nations over an agreement to cede decision-making power over Northern Gateway to the federal National Energy Board. The ruling essentially forces B.C. to make its own decision on Gateway \u2014 with aboriginal input. Observers say that decision could have implications for another controversial B.C. crude project, Kinder Morgan\'s Trans Mountain expansion.</p><p>Enbridge says it\'s still committed to the project, though it\'s in no hurry to make an official go-ahead decision.</p><p>The company is working to "re-engage" with First Nations and work out an updated cost estimate. The joint review panel\'s report in late 2013 pegged the cost at $7.9 billion.</p><p>Trans Mountain:</p><p>The Canadian arm of U.S. energy giant Kinder Morgan is aiming to nearly triple the capacity of its Trans Mountain pipeline to 890,000 barrels of oil a day. The existing Trans Mountain line currently ships 300,000 barrels a day of various petroleum products from the Edmonton area to the B.C. Lower Mainland and Washington state.</p><p>The project is in the thick of the NEB review process. It has faced stiff opposition from those who do not want to see more crude-filled tankers moving through the Burrard Inlet, including Lower Mainland mayors. The B.C. government has said it doesn\'t support the project because it doesn\'t meet its five conditions for crude pipelines.</p><p>Kinder Morgan filed its regulatory application for the Trans Mountain expansion in late 2013. The National Energy Board hearing process for Trans Mountain has been highly criticized. Opponents have called on Ottawa to halt that review \u2014 and the one into Energy East \u2014 while the NEB process is revamped.</p><p>Though the official cost estimate for the project remains $5.4 billion, Kinder Morgan Canada\'s president said it\'s looking like it will be higher because of currency swings, delays and design changes.</p><p>Follow @LaurenKrugel on Twitter.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/search/?c%5B%5D=news%2Fnational%2Cnews%2Fnational%2F%2A&d=&d1=&d2=&f=rss&l=100&nsa=eedition&q=&s=start_time&sd=desc&t=article', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/news/national/state-of-play-a-look-at-the-status-of-pipeline/article_10e05416-195a-59c1-b1ab-d6f6ba37fe67.html', u'author': u'Lauren Krugel The Canadian Press', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/news/national/state-of-play-a-look-at-the-status-of-pipeline/article_10e05416-195a-59c1-b1ab-d6f6ba37fe67.html', u'scraped_on': 1453833291, u'published_date': u'Fri, 22 Jan 2016 13:26:06 -0500', u'title': u'State of play: A look at the status of pipeline projects in Canada', u'_id': ObjectId('56a7bc4f1d41c804c420e7eb'), u'media_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/'}

Aucun n'a fait de victimes, contrairement \xe0 Lac-M\xe9gantic.</p><p> Les maires des municipalit\xe9s touch\xe9es sont aussi dans leur droit d'exiger des garanties environnementales. {u'full_article': u'<p class="standard-byline">ByStaffThe Canadian Press</p><p>OTTAWA \u2013 The Liberal government is poised to announce new rules today for assessing major resource projects such as oil pipelines.</p><p>A technical briefing is being scheduled for news media before Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr announces the new process this afternoon.</p><p>Liberals say the new rules will cover a transition period that will include existing pipeline applications already in the works.</p><p>Those includes Kinder Morgan\u2019s proposed twinning of the TransMountain pipeline in British Columbia and TransCanada\u2019s massive Energy East project that would take Alberta and Saskatchewan crude to New Brunswick.</p><p>Pipeline politics have dominated the return of Parliament this week amid deep regional acrimony.</p><p>Environmental groups, meanwhile, say any new pipeline approval by the Trudeau government will undermine the Liberals\u2019 commitment to reducing greenhouse gas emissions.</p><p class="story-ad align-left"></p><p class="article-copyright">\xa9\xa0The Canadian Press, 2016</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://globalnews.ca/environment/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://globalnews.ca/news/2480427/federal-government-to-release-new-rules-for-assessing-oil-pipelines/', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://globalnews.ca/news/2480427/federal-government-to-release-new-rules-for-assessing-oil-pipelines/', u'scraped_on': 1453928422, u'published_date': u'Wed, 27 Jan 2016 15:53:09 +0000', u'title': u'Federal government to release new rules for assessing oil pipelines', u'_id': ObjectId('56a92fe71d41c83253897f2e'), u'media_url': u'http://globalnews.ca/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>The Liberal plan to instill confidence in environmental assessments for pipeline megaprojects was panned Thursday by several First Nations groups as well as the mayor of Burnaby, B.C., who accused the federal government of being captured by the oil industry.</p><p>In a joint statement, First Nations leaders from B.C., Manitoba and Quebec slammed as \u201cmodest\u201d the reforms to the resource projects review process announced by the Liberal government, saying Ottawa needs be prepared \u201cto take no for an answer\u201d from indigenous communities with regard to major pipelines that will cross their traditional territory.</p><p>Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr and Environment Minister Catherine McKenna pledged Wednesday to conduct extensive consultations with indigenous communities before deciding whether to approve Kinder Morgan Inc.\u2019s proposed expansion of the Trans Mountain pipeline to Vancouver or TransCanada\u2019s planned Energy East line from Alberta to New Brunswick.</p><p>They also said the government would assess the impact such projects would have on greenhouse gas emissions in the oil sector, though Ms. McKenna said such a review would be one of many factors going into cabinet decisions.</p><p>In a telephone interview, Burnaby Mayor Derek Corrigan said he was \u201cseverely disappointed\u201d in the measures, noting that Prime Minister Justin Trudeau had promised during the election campaign to overhaul the environmental review process and force Kinder Morgan to resubmit its expansion proposal.</p><p>\u201cIt\u2019s clear the oil company lobbyists and the lobbyists for the pipelines have gotten to the Liberals very quickly, and suddenly we\u2019re hearing a very different tune from ministers and from the Prime Minister,\u201d said Mr. Corrigan, whose community lies at the terminus of the Kinder Morgan line. \u201cDuring the election, it was \u2018re-start the process, the public has no faith\u2019 \u2026 and now we\u2019re hearing a desperation to get this oil to the shore.\u201d</p><p>In a release Thursday, the Assembly of First Nations of Quebec and Labrador, the Assembly of Manitoba Chiefs, and the Union of British Columbia Indian Chiefs said the new measures \u2013 which include an assessment of projects\u2019 impacts on greenhouse gas emissions \u2013 fall well short of what is needed to instill confidence among aboriginal people.</p><p>\u201cIt\u2019s unfortunate that we were not consulted about the planned reforms because we could have signalled ahead of time that such reforms did not go nearly far enough,\u201d Regional Chief Ghislain Picard, of the Quebec and Labrador assembly, said in a statement.</p><p>In announcing the additional measures, the Liberal ministers said Ottawa needed to restore public trust in the project review process to win social licence for new pipelines, which the Alberta government and industry insist are critical to the province\u2019s economic well-being. They said the new rules would add four months for a decision on the Kinder Morgan project, now with a December deadline; they lengthened the Energy East project by nine months to the fall of 2018.</p><p>Conservative critics accuse the Liberals of failing the Western oil-producing provinces and resource industry workers by imposing new delays and maintaining a neutral stand on the need for new pipelines. Company spokesmen said they worried about the delays imposed by the new rules, but hoped they would help generate broader public support for their projects.</p><p>First Nations leaders are essentially demanding a veto over resource projects that pose environmental risks to their traditional territory. In their statement Thursday, the aboriginal leaders said the current National Energy Board review process is deeply flawed, with a lack of cross-examination of companies\u2019 evidence, artificial timelines and the exclusion of key evidence.</p><p>\u201cIn regard to the threats that are represented by heavy-oil pipelines proposals, the inherent risks to the environment \u2013 to the rivers, streams and eco-systems \u2013 are just so great that sometimes No is the only answer,\u201d Grand Chief Stewart Phillip, of the Union of B.C. Indian Chiefs, said in an interview.</p><p>Asked what his message was to Albertans and other Canadians who rely on the industry for their livelihood, Chief Phillip said: \u201cThey have to wake up and smell the coffee \u2026</p><p>\u201cThis is at the point in the history of this planet that we need to undertake that paradigm shift to other forms of energy \u2013 solar power, wind power and other means. We can\u2019t just simply continue this obsession with such developments as the tar sands,\u201d he said.</p><p>Alberta Environment Minister Shannon Phillips welcomed the federal measures, saying they would help bolster public confidence. And she said critics should take into account the NDP government\u2019s actions to impose carbon pricing, a cap on oil-sands emissions and measures to ensure the industry operates responsibly.</p><p>\u201cIt is a new day with respect to the environmental responsibilities that Alberta has taken on its energy development,\u201d she said in an interview.</p><p class="article__ontwitter">Follow Shawn McCarthy on Twitter: @smccarthy55</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/?service=rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/news/politics/first-nations-groups-slam-liberal-reforms-to-pipeline-reviews/article28448048/', u'author': u'SHAWN McCARTHY', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/news/politics/first-nations-groups-slam-liberal-reforms-to-pipeline-reviews/article28448048/?cmpid=rss1', u'scraped_on': 1454031560, u'published_date': u'Thu, 28 Jan 2016 20:22:20 -0500', u'title': u'First Nations groups slam Liberal reforms to pipeline reviews', u'_id': ObjectId('56aac2ca1d41c86d1546a826'), u'media_url': u'http://www.theglobeandmail.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>MONTREAL \u2014 Political satirist Rick Mercer is being attacked by comedians, political leaders and others across the country.</p><p>Mercer attacked Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre for opposing TransCanada\u2019s Energy East pipeline project.</p><p>\u201cDenis, this has nothing to do with Montreal, this has nothing to do with Quebec,\u201d he said on the Rick Mercer Report.</p><p class="story-ad"></p><p>Mercer linked the pipeline project to national unity claiming Quebec is a net beneficiary of equalization payments so the province should allow the construction of a pipeline to carry heavy crude from Alberta\u2019s oil sands to New Brunswick.</p><p>However, while Mercer picks out Coderre, yet fails to mention other politicians who oppose the project, including the premier of British Columbia and some of the mayors in the West Coast province.</p><p>Since the video appeared online, many others, including comedian Scott Vrooman attacked Mercer\u2019s online diatribe.</p><p>\u201cCanada doesn\u2019t need a pipeline. What it needs is an economy for the 21st century,\u201d he said in his online rant recorded at the same location as Mercer.</p><p>Some analysts argue Mercer\u2019s attack on Coderre and others against the pipeline could backfire.</p><p>\u201cAnyone who knows the history of this country knows that when the rest of the country tells Quebec to shut up and sit down, they usually lose that argument,\u201d Ethan Cox, editor of Ricochet.media told Global News.</p><p>Robert Sokoro, marketing professor at McGill University, told Global News that the war of words being played out online is a strong example of people using social media to make their case and try to sway public opinion.</p><p>Sokoro was especially impressed with Sol Zanetti, leader of Option Nationale.</p><p></p><p>\u201cI think the Option Nationale video is very compelling argument and that may sway some constituents in what they\u2019re opinions are,\u201d he said.</p><p>The final decision on whether the pipeline will be built will be made by the National Energy Board.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://globalnews.ca/montreal/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://globalnews.ca/news/2486972/rick-mercers-pro-oil-rant-coming-under-fire/', u'author': u'Tim Sargeant', u'original_article_url': u'http://globalnews.ca/news/2486972/rick-mercers-pro-oil-rant-coming-under-fire/', u'scraped_on': 1454117468, u'published_date': u'Sat, 30 Jan 2016 01:17:11 +0000', u'title': u'Rick Mercer\u2019s pro-oil rant coming under fire', u'_id': ObjectId('56ac125d1d41c823ebbac8a2'), u'media_url': u'http://globalnews.ca/'}
{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>Prime Minister Justin Trudeau says it\u2019s not his job to be a \u201ccheerleader\u201d for Energy East or other oil pipelines.</p>&#13;<p>But it is his job to ensure Canadians can get their products to market.</p>&#13;<p>The only way to do that efficiently with Canada\u2019s landlocked oil resources is through pipelines, such as TransCanada Corporation\u2019s $16 billion, 4,600-km project from Alberta to New Brunswick and from there to global markets.</p>&#13;<p>This is a major economic issue for Canada, directly impacting six provinces -- Alberta, Saskatchewan, Manitoba, Ontario, Quebec and New Brunswick.</p>&#13;<p>It is also Trudeau\u2019s first real-world test on a potentially divisive national issue -- our ability to export our oil (and other natural resources) to the world.</p>&#13;<p>Given the provinces\u2019 varying and sometimes opposing views on pipelines, it\u2019s also an issue that demands national leadership from the PM.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau\u2019s job is to do what he charged the previous Conservative government failed to do.</p>&#13;<p>That is, set up a credible approval process for pipelines that recognizes their importance to our economy, protects the environment, ensures public safety, respects aboriginal land rights and provides for meaningful public consultation.</p>&#13;<p>But it also has to be one designed to get responsibly built and managed pipelines approved, not lost in a never-ending deluge of red tape that kills off every proposal by imposing unrealistic and unattainable demands.</p>&#13;<p>No pipeline project, for example, can guarantee there will never be an oil spill, just as no railway project -- another way of transporting oil -- can guarantee there will never be a train derailment.</p>&#13;<p>Humans are fallible. So is the equipment and machinery they create.</p>&#13;<p>But what a proper regulatory process does is ensure risks are kept to a minimum and that when a crisis occurs, there is a practical emergency response plan in place to deal with it.</p>&#13;<p>As prime minister, Justin Trudeau\u2019s father, Pierre, understood his job was to be more than just the \u201cheadwaiter to the provinces\u201d and that on key issues, it is the responsibility of the national government to lead.</p>&#13;<p>Ensuring the ability of all of Canada\u2019s industries to get their products to market is one of the most important things we elect a prime minister to do.</p>&#13;<p>That is a must. Taking selfies is optional.</p>&#13;<p>\xa0</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/home/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/2016/01/31/trudeau-must-lead-on-energy-east', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/2016/01/31/trudeau-must-lead-on-energy-east', u'scraped_on': 1454286993, u'published_date': u'Sun, 31 Jan 2016 18:28:38 CST', u'title': u'Trudeau must lead on Energy East', u'_id': ObjectId('56aea8911d41c86b9aedb4e9'), u'media_url': u'http://www.winnipegsun.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>Prime Minister Justin Trudeau says it\u2019s not his job to be a \u201ccheerleader\u201d for Energy East or other oil pipelines.</p>&#13;<p>But it is his job to ensure Canadians can get their products to market.</p>&#13;<p>The only way to do that efficiently with Canada\u2019s landlocked oil resources is through pipelines, such as TransCanada Corporation\u2019s $16 billion, 4,600-km project from Alberta to New Brunswick and from there to global markets.</p>&#13;<p>This is a major economic issue for Canada, directly impacting six provinces -- Alberta, Saskatchewan, Manitoba, Ontario, Quebec and New Brunswick.</p>&#13;<p>It is also Trudeau\u2019s first real-world test on a potentially divisive national issue -- our ability to export our oil (and other natural resources) to the world.</p>&#13;<p>Given the provinces\u2019 varying and sometimes opposing views on pipelines, it\u2019s also an issue that demands national leadership from the PM.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau\u2019s job is to do what he charged the previous Conservative government failed to do.</p>&#13;<p>That is, set up a credible approval process for pipelines that recognizes their importance to our economy, protects the environment, ensures public safety, respects aboriginal land rights and provides for meaningful public consultation.</p>&#13;<p>But it also has to be one designed to get responsibly built and managed pipelines approved, not lost in a never-ending deluge of red tape that kills off every proposal by imposing unrealistic and unattainable demands.</p>&#13;<p>No pipeline project, for example, can guarantee there will never be an oil spill, just as no railway project -- another way of transporting oil -- can guarantee there will never be a train derailment.</p>&#13;<p>Humans are fallible. So is the equipment and machinery they create.</p>&#13;<p>But what a proper regulatory process does is ensure risks are kept to a minimum and that when a crisis occurs, there is a practical emergency response plan in place to deal with it.</p>&#13;<p>As prime minister, Justin Trudeau\u2019s father, Pierre, understood his job was to be more than just the \u201cheadwaiter to the provinces\u201d and that on key issues, it is the responsibility of the national government to lead.</p>&#13;<p>Ensuring the ability of all of Canada\u2019s industries to get their products to market is one of the most important things we elect a prime minister to do.</p>&#13;<p>That is a must. Taking selfies is optional.</p>&#13;<p>\xa0</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.ottawasun.com/opinion/editorial/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.ottawasun.com/2016/01/31/trudeau-must-lead-on-energy-east', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.ottawasun.com/2016/01/31/trudeau-must-lead-on-energy-east', u'scraped_on': 1454287764, u'published_date': u'Sun, 31 Jan 2016 19:28:38 EST', u'title': u'Trudeau must lead on Energy East', u'_id': ObjectId('56aeab941d41c86bcf74e4c8'), u'media_url': u'http://www.ottawasun.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>Prime Minister Justin Trudeau says it\u2019s not his job to be a \u201ccheerleader\u201d for Energy East or other oil pipelines.</p>&#13;<p>But it is his job to ensure Canadians can get their products to market.</p>&#13;<p>The only way to do that efficiently with Canada\u2019s landlocked oil resources is through pipelines, such as TransCanada Corporation\u2019s $16 billion, 4,600-km project from Alberta to New Brunswick and from there to global markets.</p>&#13;<p>This is a major economic issue for Canada, directly impacting six provinces -- Alberta, Saskatchewan, Manitoba, Ontario, Quebec and New Brunswick.</p>&#13;<p>It is also Trudeau\u2019s first real-world test on a potentially divisive national issue -- our ability to export our oil (and other natural resources) to the world.</p>&#13;<p>Given the provinces\u2019 varying and sometimes opposing views on pipelines, it\u2019s also an issue that demands national leadership from the PM.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau\u2019s job is to do what he charged the previous Conservative government failed to do.</p>&#13;<p>That is, set up a credible approval process for pipelines that recognizes their importance to our economy, protects the environment, ensures public safety, respects aboriginal land rights and provides for meaningful public consultation.</p>&#13;<p>But it also has to be one designed to get responsibly built and managed pipelines approved, not lost in a never-ending deluge of red tape that kills off every proposal by imposing unrealistic and unattainable demands.</p>&#13;<p>No pipeline project, for example, can guarantee there will never be an oil spill, just as no railway project -- another way of transporting oil -- can guarantee there will never be a train derailment.</p>&#13;<p>Humans are fallible. So is the equipment and machinery they create.</p>&#13;<p>But what a proper regulatory process does is ensure risks are kept to a minimum and that when a crisis occurs, there is a practical emergency response plan in place to deal with it.</p>&#13;<p>As prime minister, Justin Trudeau\u2019s father, Pierre, understood his job was to be more than just the \u201cheadwaiter to the provinces\u201d and that on key issues, it is the responsibility of the national government to lead.</p>&#13;<p>Ensuring the ability of all of Canada\u2019s industries to get their products to market is one of the most important things we elect a prime minister to do.</p>&#13;<p>That is a must. Taking selfies is optional.</p>&#13;<p>\xa0</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/home/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/2016/01/31/trudeau-must-lead-on-energy-east', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/2016/01/31/trudeau-must-lead-on-energy-east', u'scraped_on': 1454287747, u'published_date': u'Sun, 31 Jan 2016 17:28:38 MST', u'title': u'Trudeau must lead on Energy East', u'_id': ObjectId('56aeab831d41c86bcf74e28d'), u'media_url': u'http://www.edmontonsun.com/'}

THOMAS, Ont. -- Another fire in Ontario has killed a large number of livestock.</p><p>Ontario Provincial Police said approximately 90 dairy cows died Monday morning in a barn fire in Elgin County, in southwestern Ontario.</p><p>Const. Troy Carlson said 10 or 15 cows escaped the blaze, which caused damage estimated at $1.5 million.</p><p>Carlson said it took firefighters several hours to bring the fire under control due to the large amount of stored hay, which is more difficult to extinguish.</p><p>Last week, firefighters had to dodge spooked cows as they battled a blaze following a crash on the TransCanada Highway in central Ontario, that left a cattle transport driver and seven or eight cattle dead.</p><p>More than 2,000 pigs died in mid-January in a barn fire north of London, Ont., while fires earlier in the month at two barns in the Guelph, Ont. area killed more than 50 horses, including 43 racehorses.</p><p>About 500 goats and 30 cows were also killed in a barn fire in mid-January. {u'full_article': u'<p>Infrastructure projects and the Energy East pipeline were on the agenda as Prime Minister Justin Trudeau and Mayor Denis Coderre met Tuesday morning in Montreal.</p><p>It was a meeting planned before a spat erupted between the provinces over the pipeline project, the two say.</p><p>Coderre has been roundly criticized since coming out in opposition to Energy East last week. He is the president of the Montreal Metropolitan Community, which represents 82 Montreal-area municipalities and cities. The group rejected the project, saying the risks of an environmental disaster outweigh any potential benefits the project may bring.</p><p>Coderre said the project is worth about $2 million a year in economic benefits to the Montreal area, while the cleanup of a major oil spill could cost between $1 billion and $10 billion.</p><p>When asked whether he would try to convince the group to change the group\'s opinion, Trudeau dodged the question saying the federal government\u2019s role in the matter is to put in place a process whereby TransCanada, in this case, can demonstrate a project is in the public interest.</p><p>\u201cWhat we\u2019re going to do \u2026 is establish a clear process that will look at greenhouse gas emissions, build on work that\u2019s already been done and give Canadians \u2013 the different levels of government, scientists, Aboriginal people \u2013 the chance to make their opinions heard and participate in the decision-making process, he said.</p><p>"You can\'t build a strong economy without protecting environment at same time. It\'s not one or the other," he said.</p><p>Trudeau went on to crticize the role the Harper government played in the process, saying they acted like "cheerleaders" instead of "responsible referees" for the process.</p><p>Coderre spoke about striking a balanced approach between economic and sustainable development.</p><p>"We said yes to Enbridge [the Line 9B pipeline reversal project] because they\'ve done their homework, so we need to have the environmental impact [reports], we need to understand because there\'s no plan B for the planet," he said.</p><p>The criticisms of Coderre and the group of municipal leaders came from politicians in Saskatchewan and Alberta. Alberta Premier Rachel Notley called the Montreal-area leaders\u2019 decision \u201cshort-sighted,\u201d while Saskatchewan Premier Brad Wall tweeted Quebec should return the equalization payments it receives.</p><p>The meeting occurred hours before the federal environment commissioner released the results of an audit which shows the National Energy Board is failing to enforce pipeline approval conditions or follow up on compliance problems.</p><p>Coderre and Trudeau also spoke about infrastructure projects, including light rain and prolonging the metro\'s blue line, as well as the effort in welcoming refugees.</p><p>-- with files from The Canadian Press</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://montreal.ctvnews.ca/rss/ctv-news-montreal-1.822366', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://montreal.ctvnews.ca/energy-east-pipeline-trudeau-says-ottawa-s-role-is-to-facilitate-talks-1.2752067', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://montreal.ctvnews.ca/energy-east-pipeline-trudeau-says-ottawa-s-role-is-to-facilitate-talks-1.2752067', u'scraped_on': 1453823114, u'published_date': u'Tue, 26 Jan 2016 07:41:00 -0500', u'title': u"Energy East pipeline: Trudeau says Ottawa's role is to facilitate talks", u'_id': ObjectId('56a7948a1d41c87ba5c99703'), u'media_url': u'http://montreal.ctvnews.ca/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>Stock markets began\xa0the week with a thud, as major equity indexes in Toronto and New York registered hefty losses Monday alongside\xa0another sharp drop\xa0in oil prices.</p><p>By the close, Toronto\u2019s lead index was off 246 points or nearly two per cent to close at 12,143. Energy stocks led to\xa0the downside, falling over five per cent, as U.S. light crude prices shed almost $2 US a barrel to close at $30.34 in New York.\xa0</p><p>Oil\xa0continued to slide in after-hours trading, slipping below $30 US. On the flip side, gold stocks rose\xa0as bullion prices registered another uptick.</p><p>In New York, all three major indexes wound up deep in\xa0the red, with the Dow Jones Industrial Average shedding 208 points, the S&amp;P 500 Index down almost 30 points and the tech-rich Nasdaq Composite Index slipping almost 73 points.</p><p>Monday\u2019s pullback\xa0followed a sharp\xa0shortcovering\xa0rally Friday,\xa0as stocks and oil prices rebounded from\xa0Wednesday\u2019s lows, when the S&amp;P 500 bottomed out at\xa0just\xa01,812, 15 per cent below\xa0its record\xa0high.</p><p>But\xa0the lack of follow-through Monday confirms that the bears remain in charge, pointing to\xa0more downside ahead. With billions of dollars exiting U.S. stock funds in recent weeks,\xa0investors are trimming their exposure to riskier assets following\xa0the worst\xa0start\xa0to a new year in stock market history.</p><p>Many now expect the major U.S. indexes to fall into a full-fledged bear market \u2014 defined as\xa0a\xa0decline of at least 20 per cent from their 2015 peak levels \u2014 before the washout is complete.\xa0That could take a couple more\xa0months.</p><p>As of Monday\u2019s close, the Dow has dropped\xa013.4\xa0per cent from its\xa0record\xa0high of 18,351 reached last May, while the S&amp;P 500 is 12\xa0per cent below its May peak\xa0of 2,134. The Nasdaq Composite Index has given up\xa013.6\xa0per cent since it reached a peak of\xa05,231 last July.</p><p>To plunge into\xa0bear market territory, the Dow would have to fall to 14,650 \u2014 1,235\xa0points or 7.7 per cent below Monday\u2019s close \u2014 and the S&amp;P 500 would have to shed a further\xa0172\xa0points or 9.1 per cent to 1,705.</p><p>Toronto\u2019s benchmark S&amp;P/TSX Composite Index is already in bear market territory. Canada\u2019s resource-heavy market\xa0has shed about 22.4\xa0per cent since hitting its record high of\xa015,657 in September 2014. But it\u2019s hardly alone.</p><p>Many other markets, including\xa0Russia, Japan, Germany, France, Hong Kong and Brazil, have also tumbled into bear market territory. If the U.S. follows suits, it\xa0would be the last big domino to fall.</p><p>Despite the fireworks in the stock market, the U.S. economy continues to chug along, and most economists see the odds of a U.S. recession at less than 25 per cent. The decoupling of the market and the economy has surprised many investors, but it shouldn\u2019t. Bear markets can and do sometimes occur in periods of economic growth.</p><p>As for the never-ending decline in oil prices, the wall of worry only seems to grow higher with each passing week. Last week, the threat of new supplies from Iran was the big focus. On Monday, the focus turned to war-torn Iraq, which reported record oil output\xa0of more than 4.1 million barrels per day in December.\xa0</p><p>While\xa0Alberta\u2019s landlocked crude still can\u2019t reach world markets, due to vocal opposition to pipelines in provinces\xa0such as\xa0Quebec and British Columbia, Iraq, Saudi Arabia, Russia and other oil-producing nations are happily filling the gap.</p><p>\u201cLook at the amount of Iraqi oil now coming to the U.S. Gulf Coast,\u201d says Robert Johnston, CEO of New York-based Eurasia Group, a consulting firm that\xa0tracks global oil markets and political risks on behalf of major investors.</p><p>\u201cThose are heavy oil barrels, much like Canada\u2019s diluted bitumen. That shouldn\u2019t really happen. Iraqi oil should go to China and Canadian oil\xa0should go to the U.S.\xa0It\u2019s sort of\xa0a\xa0strange world.\u201d</p><p>Indeed it is. Canada was once considered a politically stable country that was attractive to foreign investors. But that is changing, says Johnston.</p><p>The virulent opposition in Quebec to\xa0TransCanada\u2019s proposed Energy East pipeline and equally vocal opposition in B.C. to the\xa0proposed\xa0expansion of the\xa0TransMountain pipeline to\xa0Burnaby, B.C., has global investors reassessing the appeal of\xa0investing in Canada, notwithstanding Prime Minister Justin Trudeau\u2019s sales pitch at the World Economic Forum in Davos, Switzerland.</p><p>\u201cThere is a view that Canada\xa0does have some political risk now,\u201d Johnston told the Alberta Industrial Heartland Association\u2019s annual gathering\xa0last week. \u201cIt\u2019s not the kind of risk we see in Nigeria or Iraq or Russia. It\u2019s more on the policy, regulatory, or environmental side. {u'full_article': u'<p>Saskatchewan\'s premier is firing back at Montreal area mayors after they spoke out against the Energy East pipeline on Thursday.</p><p>The Montreal Metropolitan Community represents 82 municipalities. On Thursday it said it opposes TransCanada\'s project, and will defend that position at Quebec\'s environmental impact and National Energy Board hearings.</p><p>Denis Coderre, Montreal\'s mayor and head of the group, said the decision was unanimous and that the environment risks of a spill far outweigh any economic benefits.</p><p>Brad Wall had a sharp response, saying in a statement it was a "sad day for our country", that the mayors were being "parochial" about the project, and that it would benefit all of Canada including the Montreal municipalities.</p><p>He said the people in those municipalities will benefit from about $10 billion in equalization payments, which the Canadian energy sector and western Canada have supported for almost a decade.</p><p>"Is it too much to expect that these Quebec municipal leaders would respond to this reality with generous support for a pipeline that supports the very sector that has supported them," Wall said in a statement.</p><p dir="ltr" lang="en" xml:lang="en">I trust Montreal area mayors will politely return their share of $10B in equalization supported by west #EnergyEast https://t.co/DUtlu4VjPx</p><p>\u2014 Brad Wall (@PremierBradWall) January 21, 2016</p><p></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://cjme.com/rss-feed/6181', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://cjme.com/article/435889/sask-premier-fires-back-after-montreal-area-mayors-reject-energy-east', u'author': u'Lisa Schick, Canadian Press', u'original_article_url': u'http://cjme.com/article/435889/sask-premier-fires-back-after-montreal-area-mayors-reject-energy-east', u'scraped_on': 1453823035, u'published_date': u'Thu, 21 Jan 16 17:24:14 -0600', u'title': u'Sask. premier fires back after Montreal-area mayors reject Energy East', u'_id': ObjectId('56a7943b1d41c87ba5c98f24'), u'media_url': u'http://cjme.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>With the spectre of an East-West battle erupting over the proposed Energy East pipeline, Alberta Environment Minister Shannon Phillips said Monday the pipeline must be evaluated on its merits.</p>&#13;<p>\xa0</p>&#13;<p>Controversy over the $15.7-billion project, which would move Alberta oilsands crude as far east as Saint John, N.B., erupted last week after Montreal\'s Mayor Denis Coderre came out against the project, triggering a war of words between himself and several western politicians.</p>&#13;<p>"I have to say that this retreat to old tropes is not helpful on either side," Phillips said in an interview. "Certainly the Montreal area mayors were being, in our view, ungenerous and short-sighted. This is not your father\'s federalism ...</p>&#13;<p>"This kind of chest-beating rhetoric serves no one on either side."</p>&#13;<p>Likewise, Alberta Energy Minister Marg McCuaig-Boyd said progress was made Friday after Premier Rachel Notley met with her Ontario counterpart, Kathleen Wynne, winning some verbal support for the project\'s merits.</p>&#13;<p>The energy minister said Montreal\'s mayor should sit down with the pipeline\'s proponent, TransCanada, to discuss his concerns.</p>&#13;<p>"At the end of the day, it\'s the National Energy Board that will look at the merits of any of the pipelines," McCuaig-Boyd said Monday. {u'full_article': u'<p>Guess who\'s going to dinner?</p>&#13;<p>In an unusual twist on Canada-U.S. relations, which for years have failed to live up to the stereotype of the world\'s two supposedly closest allies, a departing president with nothing to gain is having a rookie Canadian prime minister over for an honour that\'s become rare for us, a White House state dinner.</p>&#13;<p>U.S. President Barack Obama will roll out the red carpet for Prime Minister Justin Trudeau on March 10, evidently a hastily-arranged timeline for a function that the White House, on its website, says normally takes six months to prepare.</p>&#13;<p>South of the border, tongues have been wagging about which Canadian celebrities -- the U.S. does recognize some -- might be included on the guest list along with Trudeau, no stranger to celebrity himself despite his short months in office.</p>&#13;<p>North of the border, however, the attention paid to the upcoming gala has been more sober, noting, for example, that it\'s been 19 years since a Canadian prime minister was last feted at the White House.</p>&#13;<p>Indeed, it\'s been so long since a glass was hoisted to a PM at 1600\xa0Pennsylvania Ave., that one guest bound to be there -- Hillary Clinton, the presumptive Democratic nominee for the fall presidential race -- was the last First Lady to do so, when she and then-president Bill Clinton welcomed Jean Chretien in 1997.</p>&#13;<p>Cut through the polite dinner talk, and that should be worriesome to Canadians.</p>&#13;<p>State dinners are more than just a meal; they\'re an instrument of diplomacy and power. They have been since 1874, when Ulysses S. Grant held the first foreign state dinner for the king of Hawaii, a sovereign oceanic state the U.S. was already engulfing. Only those who matter to the Americans get invited.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau, at this stage, is more selfie than statesman, so it\'s not his diplomatic skills Obama wants. More likely, it\'s one liberal leader wanting to introduce another to those he hopes will succeed him.</p>&#13;<p>Still, there\'s no shortage of issues that need attention between Ottawa and Washington: a fragile economy, global trade, the energy sector, the war against ISIS, among them.</p>&#13;<p>On many files, our interests don\'t intersect, a point underlined this week as Canadian energy giant TransCanada filed a lawsuit against the Obama administration for rejecting its proposed Keystone XL oilsands pipeline to the Gulf Coast.</p>&#13;<p>Make no mistake, this should be a long overdue working meal.</p>&#13;<p>Bon appetit.</p>&#13;<p>-- Postmedia Network</p><p class="clearfix ads-container"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.thepeterboroughexaminer.com/opinion/editorial/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.thepeterboroughexaminer.com/2016/01/07/state-dinner-chatter-should-be-interesting', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.thepeterboroughexaminer.com/2016/01/07/state-dinner-chatter-should-be-interesting', u'scraped_on': 1453833287, u'published_date': u'Thu, 07 Jan 2016 19:08:35 EST', u'title': u'State dinner chatter should be interesting', u'_id': ObjectId('56a7bc4e1d41c804c420e65f'), u'media_url': u'http://www.thepeterboroughexaminer.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>Melissa Lachance of Montreal looks at a map of the proposed Energy East pipeline, at a rally organized by Greenpeace and other groups at Jack Layton Park in Hudson on July 4, 2015.Frederic Hore / Montreal Gazette</p><p>From Quebec\u2019s enviro-nationalists to Alberta\u2019s old energy apologists, few are tackling the issue of TransCanada\u2019s contentious Energy East pipeline with any semblance of balance. Some are even exacerbating what has now become a minor national unity crisis.</p><p>When the autopsy of this messy debate is conducted it will find at least three men responsible: Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre, Saskatchewan Premier Brad Wall and the CBC\u2019s Rick Mercer. So consumed with their narrow interests, the politicians seem to have forgotten that Canada remains a federation, not a collection of fiefdoms, while the ranting comedian reduces Canada\u2019s complex equalization system to a series of transactions between east and west.</p><p>\u201cWe all need this thing,\u201d Mercer said, clever as always, but failing to demonstrate that we truly need this one particular oil pipeline as a magic bullet to rescue Canada\u2019s sluggish economy. Mercer\u2019s Qu\xe9b\xe9cois counterpart, Jean-Ren\xe9 \u201cInfoman\u201d Dufort, joined a chorus of parody videos pointing out that receiving equalization payments doesn\u2019t mean Quebec should \u201cferme sa gueule\u201d and embrace new pipelines as British Columbia and the United States reject them.</p><p>While asymmetrical federalism can have its drawbacks, Wall expressed on social media last week that he would seemingly prefer a form of random-federalism, where an oil-empowered western premier acts as if he has the authority to hang federal equalization dollars over the heads of Quebecers.</p><p>\u201cI trust Montreal-area mayors will politely return their share of $10-billion in equalization supported by west(ern provinces),\u201d Wall quipped, to which Parti Qu\xe9b\xe9cois MNA Jean-Fran\xe7ois Lis\xe9e replied, \u201cI trust the West will refund the $10-billion of Quebec taxes spent on big oil tax breaks and grants over the years.\u201d</p><p>It\u2019s a childish debate for Wall to ignite; one that fails to consider the purpose of federal governments. The Saskatchewan premier should know that pitting regions against each other fuels sovereignist hysteria and is counterproductive for the 100,000 workers who lost their jobs last year as a result of the downturn in the energy sector.</p><p>\u201cSay thank you,\u201d Coderre then tweeted, waving those same federal dollars over the heads of Saskatchewanians while Conservative interim leader Rona Ambrose warned that Coderre\u2019s rhetoric \u201cisn\u2019t in the spirit of national unity\u201d (neither was Wall\u2019s).</p><p>The Rest of Canada\u2019s position is seen as paternalistic by some commentators in Quebec. \u201cColonialist schemes\u201d are forcing Quebecers into a position of \u201cconstant submission,\u201d wrote sovereignist penseur Mathieu Bock-C\xf4t\xe9. Apparently eager to capitalize on Western missteps, Quebecor colleague Jos\xe9e Legault even compared the Energy East spat to the failure of the Meech Lake constitutional reforms (wishful thinking)!</p><p>I trust Prime Minister Justin Trudeau has reminded his provincial and municipal counterparts that, on a handful of federal files like Energy East, it simply isn\u2019t the prerogative of regions to set the tone of the debate. There is no need for such firmly entrenched stances on either side when the National Energy Board and Quebec\u2019s Bureau d\u2019audiences publiques sur l\u2019environnement have yet to complete their work.</p><p>Communication on the issue has been so muddled that a source close to the industry mused to me that \u201cthe biggest question most journalists have is whether the pipelines will be underground or above ground.\u201d (Energy East would be underground, even under the St. Lawrence River, which presents fewer environmental risks than transport over an overloaded rail system.) If journalists are so confused about the Energy East plan, the public at large is surely disconnected from the debate (making polls on the issue largely irrelevant).</p><p>The pipeline is a private endeavour that is TransCanada\u2019s, not Wall\u2019s, to sell to Canadians. It may be seen as necessary, but it is not free of risk and is certainly not an example of sustainable development.</p><p>While regional considerations should be just that, considerations, a final Energy East decision should be based on sound science resulting from rigorous consultation and evaluation; not what\u2019s best for Coderre\u2019s fiefdom or Wall\u2019s. Conservative panic over a struggling oil industry, however, has backfired and placed TransCanada in a more disadvantageous position as the prime minister, among his considerations, now must consider growing discontent among Quebec nationalists.</p><p>Dan Delmar is an independent public relations consultant at Provocateur Communications and the host of The Exchange, Mondays and Wednesdays at 8-10 p.m. on CJAD 800 Montreal.</p><p>twitter.com/DanDelmar</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/opinion/columnists/dan-delmar-denis-coderre-brad-wall-and-rick-mercers-pipeline-polemics-ill-serve-canada', u'author': u'Edie Austin, Montreal Gazette', u'original_article_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/opinion/columnists/dan-delmar-denis-coderre-brad-wall-and-rick-mercers-pipeline-polemics-ill-serve-canada', u'scraped_on': 1454095062, u'published_date': u'Fri, 29 Jan 2016 19:11:10 +0000', u'title': u"Dan Delmar: Denis Coderre, Brad Wall and Rick Mercer's pipeline polemics ill serve Canada", u'_id': ObjectId('56abbad81d41c81c13aa9b4b'), u'media_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>Scottish philosopher David Hume said it best: reason is, and ought only to be, a slave to the passions.</p><p>If you\u2019ve ever tried to reason yourself into a feeling (\u201cI really ought to love Mr. X, or Wagner operas, or pickled herring\u201d), you\u2019ll know that for the vast majority of humanity, it is a vain enterprise. That\u2019s because feelings don\u2019t come from reason and the mind. They come from emotions and the heart.</p><p>Hume\u2019s point was that reason doesn\u2019t tell us what to want, only how to get it. You may be surprised to learn that this matters where oil pipelines are concerned.</p><p>Ottawa has announced it intends to expand the factors regulators consider when weighing whether to approve new pipelines, such as Kinder Morgan, Energy East and others. Henceforth, the National Energy Board, for example, when considering pipelines, will be called upon to assess the contribution of upstream operations to greenhouse gas emissions, in addition to things such as design, safety, environmental impact and conformity with legal requirements such as consulting and accommodating affected aboriginal communities.</p><p>That\u2019s perfectly reasonable. The issue of climate change caused by human activities is a huge one and has spawned significant public opposition within Canada to our petroleum industry and to the pipeline infrastructure needed to gets its products to market.</p><p>The problem, however, is that our whole regulatory process for pipeline approval is based on reason, on a thoughtful attempt to design a process that examines objective evidence dispassionately. When that weighing of the evidence shows that pipelines meet the tests established by legislators and regulators, those pipelines are approved.</p><p>Opposition to pipelines, however, is notoriously resistant to reason and evidence.</p><p>The U.S. State Department conducted numerous environmental reviews of the proposed Keystone XL pipeline designed to bring Alberta and U.S. crude to the U.S. Gulf Coast. Those reviews included looking at the impact on greenhouse gas emissions. Every one of these reasoned evidence-based reviews said that KXL\u2019s impact would be negligible and that this was not a ground to refuse the presidential permit that TransCanada needed to build the project.</p><p>But the pipeline opponents didn\u2019t care about the evidence. They believed that they were eco-warriors fighting for \u201cclimate justice.\u201d President Barack Obama bowed to their unreasoning opposition to pipelines when he turned down KXL.</p><p>Here in Canada, in an attempt to mute the opposition of environmental groups to pipelines to the east and west coasts, Alberta\u2019s NDP government negotiated a deal with some of the oilpatch and several environmental groups that would cap the greenhouse gas emissions of the oilsands and raise already high environmental standards even higher.</p><p>This strategy of making reasoned process changes to win over opponents has already failed. On the West Coast, the opposition to the Kinder Morgan pipeline to Burnaby, B.C., is only getting noisier, while the federal Liberals themselves are trying to kill the Northern Gateway project indirectly by legislating an oil tanker ban in northern B.C. waters.</p><p>In Montreal, all the local mayors have come out in opposition to Energy East. On the day Ottawa announced its new regulatory measures, more than 70 non-governmental organizations wrote the prime minister and premiers to oppose any new pipelines.</p><p>Toughened process requirements cannot satisfy pipeline opponents, or even cause them to be less vociferous in their attacks, because they believe that any process that approves pipelines is itself vicious and flawed and they are willing to toss democracy, the rule of law and balanced treatment of conflicting interests on the trash heap to achieve their aims.</p><p>At some point, our politicians are going to confront the hard truth that there are no process fixes that will satisfy pipeline opponents\xa0\u2014\xa0they believe pipelines are bad and the approval process a weapon to be commandeered in the struggle against them. There is no compromise that will win their assent. Then our leaders will have to make choices about whose side they\u2019re on. It won\u2019t be pretty.</p><p>Brian Lee Crowley\xa0is the managing director of the Macdonald-Laurier Institute, an independent non-partisan public policy think-tank in Ottawa.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/opinion/columnists/crowley-pipeline-protests-are-fuelled-by-emotions-not-the-facts', u'author': u'David Marsden, Calgary Herald', u'original_article_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/opinion/columnists/crowley-pipeline-protests-are-fuelled-by-emotions-not-the-facts', u'scraped_on': 1454148201, u'published_date': u'Sat, 30 Jan 2016 10:00:00 +0000', u'title': u'Crowley: Pipeline protests are fuelled by emotions, not the facts', u'_id': ObjectId('56ac8a6f1d41c82adc50c8ed'), u'media_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/'}

He\u2019s worried that adding more consultations with indigenous people and the public separate from the NEB review could add months if not years to the process.</p><p>However, as the Conservative Harper government learned after accusing critics of the Northern Gateway Pipeline of being radicals and enemies of the state, and changing environmental and fisheries laws to facilitate construction across more than a thousand rivers and streams and a couple of mountain ranges, an official stamp of approval doesn\u2019t exactly translate into a project going ahead.</p><p>Even though Prime Minister Justin Trudeau\u2019s Liberals may not have got everything right with the interim review process for Kinder Morgan\u2019s Trans Mountain and TransCanada\u2019s Energy East pipeline projects, the revamped process has the potential to get more buy-in from pragmatic ordinary Canadians even if the environmental activists insist that all oil must remain in the ground.</p><p>The requirements for meaningful consultations with First Nations, assessing the climate impact of upstream greenhouse gas emissions, consideration of the views of Canadians in affected communities, and basing decisions on science, traditional knowledge of first people and other evidence all lend credibility to the review process and shore up public confidence that the process isn\u2019t rigged to accommodate the energy companies.</p><p>This doesn\u2019t mean Montreal or Vancouver area residents, for instance, get a veto, but that their concerns will be considered and addressed as much as possible.
