{u'full_article': u"<p>Prime Minister Justin Trudeau, left, and Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre address reporters at Montreal city hall onJan. 26.They discussed, among other things, the mayor's opposition to the proposed Energy East pipeline.Phil Carpenter / Montreal Gazette</p><p>The NDP, Green Party and the environmental left claim the federal government\u2019s new pipeline approval process falls well short of both protecting the environment and what the Liberals promised in last year\u2019s election.</p><p>The Conservatives, resource-based provinces and energy sector claim it\u2019s too onerous on the industry and further damages the national economy. Or might this be a golden opportunity for First Nations to stonewall pipeline projects for other political reasons?</p><p>And what exactly is the Liberal government talking about when it says: \u201cDirect and upstream greenhouse gas emissions linked to the projects under review will be assessed\u201d?</p><p>One gets why the government\xa0needed to move the pipeline approval process away from the exclusive jurisdiction of the National Energy Board (NEB). Clearly, this week\u2019s report from Canada\u2019s\xa0environmental watchdog that suggested the NEB didn\u2019t track pipeline companies to ensure they were complying\xa0with terms set out for their projects showed flaws in the system.\xa0</p><p>One even gets\xa0Environment Minister Catherine McKenna\u2019s view that there might be a need to\xa0\u201crebuild Canadians\u2019 trust in our environmental assessment processes\u201d and a \u201cneed to take into account the views and concerns of Canadians\u201d\xa0in the wake of the Stephen Harper Conservatives\u2019 approach.</p><p>But there\u2019s something really disturbing about the political immaturity in Trudeau\u2019s suggestion that he will not be a \u201ccheerleader\u201d for the pipeline industry industry like the last government was.</p><p>There are tens of thousands of people who have lost their jobs in the western oil sector \u2014\xa0many of them without unionized severance benefits and pensions. Maybe we do need a cheerleader for Energy East right now.</p><p>Similarly, this government appears to be unable to distinguish between pipelines proposed through remote pristine wilderness and TransCanada\u2019s Energy East (again, running through urban Quebec where the mayors\u2019 concerns seem to be less about the environment than \u201cwhat\u2019s in it for us?\u201d\xa0</p><p>When even groups like\xa0Environmental Defence suggest that it \u201cdoesn\u2019t make sense\u201d for Energy East to fall under these same new rules, it would seem obvious that the Liberals missed the mark.</p><p>Sadly, though, Trudeau and his party\xa0don\u2019t get any of this.</p><p>Mandryk is the political columnist for the Regina Leader-Post.</p>", u'rss_url': u'http://leaderpost.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://leaderpost.com/opinion/columnists/energy-east-pipeline-project-needs-support-from-prime-minister-justin-trudeau', u'author': u'Murray Mandryk', u'original_article_url': u'http://leaderpost.com/opinion/columnists/energy-east-pipeline-project-needs-support-from-prime-minister-justin-trudeau', u'scraped_on': 1454158066, u'published_date': u'Sat, 30 Jan 2016 12:30:49 +0000', u'title': u'Mandryk: Trudeau needs to be a cheerleader for Energy East', u'_id': ObjectId('56acb0f61d41c830fda1f511'), u'media_url': u'http://leaderpost.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p class="articlerelatedphotogallery section"></p><p class="articlerelatedvideogallery section"></p><p itemprop="articleBody">By creating new conditions for pipeline development, Prime Minister Justin Trudeau\u2019s Liberal government has opened an interesting can of worms.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Wednesday\u2019s joint announcement by Environment Minister Catherine McKenna and Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr does not deliver a definitive yes or no to controversial projects like TransCanada\u2019s proposed Energy East heavy-oil pipeline from Alberta to New Brunswick.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Rather, it amends the criteria by which such projects are judged.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">But the announcement also sends conflicting signals about the Liberal government\u2019s ultimate intentions.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Is Ottawa serious about taking into account the climate-change effects of pipeline development? If it were, arguably no new pipelines would be built to service the Alberta oilsands, already the fastest-growing source of Canadian greenhouse gases.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Or is the government merely forcing pipeline proponents to jump through hoops in an effort to make their projects more acceptable to the general public?</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Some of the new interim measures announced Wednesday aren\u2019t new. The ministers said the government wants to hear from affected local communities when deciding on projects such as oil pipelines. But that already happens.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">They said indigenous groups affected by any project must be consulted. But that, as the Supreme Court has ruled, is the law.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Other new measures are unclear. The ministers said, for instance, that the government will base its decisions not just on science but on the traditional knowledge of indigenous peoples \u201cand other relevant evidence,\u201d whatever that means.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">But one new measure is potentially radical. The government says it will explicitly look at something the current National Energy Board does not consider \u2014 whether new pipelines would lead to more greenhouse gas emissions from the Alberta oilsands.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The ministerial statement speaks to more than just pipelines. A spokesperson for McKenna says the new interim measures will apply to any project currently undergoing a federal environmental assessment, of which there are 89.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">But pipelines remain the political focus.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">In Ontario, Quebec and British Columbia, pipeline critics fret about leaks. Even the Ontario Energy Board has weighed in, reporting last summer that TransCanada\u2019s plan to move heavy oil though its existing cross-Canada, natural-gas pipeline would endanger provincial waterways, including Lake Nipigon, the Ottawa River and the St. Lawrence.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Montreal-area mayors such as Denis Coderre object for similar reasons. In B.C., much of the opposition to Kinder Morgan is centred in the Vancouver region, where critics worry that the pipeline will lead to more tanker traffic and dangerous oil spills.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Yet, in Ontario at least, criticism on this level can easily be soothed. The Ontario Energy Board report makes common-sense suggestions such as relocating portions of the pipeline away from waterways.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">It also suggests that TransCanada replace damaged portions of the pipeline that have been patched with plastic tape.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">And it calls for the pipeline plan to be tweaked to ensure that residents of Eastern Ontario still have enough natural gas to heat their homes in the winter.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">All of these remedies are easily doable. If TransCanada agreed, Premier Kathleen Wynne\u2019s Ontario government would find it politically easier get off the fence and back a project that, at base, it does not oppose.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Greenhouse gases pose a thornier problem. The Ontario Energy Board commissioned research that calculates Energy East will increase carbon emissions in Canada by less than 2 per cent.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">That may not be much mathematically. But at a time when Canada is unable to meet its self-imposed carbon-reduction targets, it does represent movement in the wrong direction.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Trudeau has long said that the aim of new pipeline rules, such as those announced this week, is to help oilsands producers market their bitumen.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">I\u2019m not sure that they will be enough to save Kinder Morgan. Even the B.C. government is washing its hands of that pipeline.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">But Energy East is an easier sell. As long as its critics remain focused on spills rather than climate change, the prime minister\u2019s gambit may well succeed.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Thomas Walkom\u2019s column appears Wednesday, Thursday and Sunday.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/feeds.articles.news.canada.rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/news/canada/2016/01/31/justin-trudeaus-pipeline-gambit-could-salvage-energy-east-walkom.html', u'author': u'Thomas Walkom - National Affairs', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/news/canada/2016/01/31/justin-trudeaus-pipeline-gambit-could-salvage-energy-east-walkom.html', u'scraped_on': 1454267264, u'published_date': u'Sun, 31 Jan 2016 05:00:00 EST', u'title': u'Justin Trudeau\u2019s pipeline gambit could salvage Energy East: Walkom', u'_id': ObjectId('56ae5b811d41c865d6c1504f'), u'media_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/'}
{u'full_article': u"<p>FILE -- A dump truck works near the Syncrude oil sands extraction facility near the town of Fort McMurray, Alberta on Sunday June 1, 2014. The furore over a New Democrat candidate's remarks about leaving a lot of Alberta's oilsands in the ground is a reflection of how poorly the issue is understood, say energy experts.JASON FRANSON / THE CANADIAN PRESS</p><p>The verbal battles between Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre and western premiers and mayors are reruns of a bad movie. Let\u2019s change the channel and look at TransCanada\u2019s proposed Energy East pipeline in light of climate change. That was not on anyone\u2019s mind 35 years ago during tussles around the National Energy Program.</p><p>TransCanada\u2019s proposed Energy East pipeline is so capacious that it could single-handedly allow expansion of Alberta\u2019s oilsands by over 40 per cent. That\u2019s the same percentage rise as the sands emission cap that Alberta\u2019s new climate action plan permits.</p><p>The rise of oilsands emissions from 70 to 100 million tonnes a year will prevent Canada from meeting its laudable and ambitious targets at the Paris climate talks in December and Canada\u2019s 2009 commitment at the G8 meetings in L\u2019Aquila, Italy to lower carbon emissions by 80 per cent below Canada\u2019s 1990 level of 600 million tonnes by 2050. That means a drop to 120 million tonnes of greenhouse gases.</p><p>To hit Canada\u2019s promises, oilsands output and emissions must be capped at 70 million tonnes and then phased out. We can\u2019t get to a low carbon future unless we do so.</p><p>U.S. President Barack Obama killed the Keystone XL oil pipeline after a 1,000 protesters were arrested outside the White House; Prime Minister Justin Trudeau effectively killed the Northern Gateway line by banning oil tankers in the treacherous waters off B.C.\u2019s northern coast after many British Columbians declared it will not proceed; and B.C. Premier Christy Clark\u2019s government opposed the expansion of Kinder Morgan\u2019s existing oil pipeline to the Vancouver area after opposition grew strong.</p><p>Opposition to Energy East is growing as strong.</p><p>Energy East is the final sands oil exporting line left standing. That\u2019s why oil companies and western politicians are so desperate to get it approved. But it doesn\u2019t mean it\u2019s the right thing to do.</p><p>Enbridge\u2019s Line 9 started bringing western Canadian oil and North Dakota shale oil to Quebec\u2019s two refineries in December. Those refineries are not likely to take on additional oil from the Energy East line. All or most would flow onto Irving Oil\u2019s refinery in Saint John, N.B.</p><p>However Mark Sherman, plant manager at the Irving refinery has stated that Energy East would send \u201cway more than we would ever use at this refinery, so the bulk of it would all be exported.\u201d</p><p>Alberta\u2019s oilsands are the fastest growing source of Canada\u2019s greenhouse gas pollution.</p><p>Oilsands output will grow substantially only if it has an outlet to a coast.</p><p>The U.S. Environmental Protection Agency pegs oilsands emissions much higher than conventional oil. Alberta\u2019s oilsands can\u2019t be greened.</p><p>It\u2019s the production of oil and natural gas, not in their use in transportation in Canada (cars and trucks), that is this country\u2019s biggest source of greenhouse gases.</p><p>Canada has just enough conventional, non-fracked, non-sands oil to supply all Canadians as we steadily lower consumption. It would also give every Canadian energy security. But there\u2019s not enough such oil to continue exports.</p><p>Our country\u2019s emissions will plummet when Canada phases them out.</p><p>Why run a pipeline 4,600 kilometres to New Brunswick anyway when Newfoundland has enough conventional oil to supply all east coasters? Most live on or near a coast. Why pipe it when it can be shipped?</p><p>That would avoid incursions on First Nations lands. Oil tankers could be phased out as East Coasters\u2019 oil use falls, whereas a pipeline would need three decades of shipping oilsands oil at full volume to amortize its building costs.</p><p>Trudeau and the premiers are scheduled to meet by March 11 to fashion a plan to transition Canada to a low-carbon future. They should nix Energy East.</p><p>Gordon Laxer is author of After the Sands. Energy and Ecological Security for Canadians and is founding director of Parkland Institute at the University of Alberta. He lives in Gravenhurst, Ont.</p>", u'rss_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/opinion/columnists/opinion-energy-east-would-facilitate-oilsands-expansion-and-thats-bad-for-climate-change', u'author': u'Edie Austin, Montreal Gazette', u'original_article_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/opinion/columnists/opinion-energy-east-would-facilitate-oilsands-expansion-and-thats-bad-for-climate-change', u'scraped_on': 1454266958, u'published_date': u'Sun, 31 Jan 2016 18:00:51 +0000', u'title': u"Opinion: Energy East would facilitate oilsands expansion, and that's bad for climate change", u'_id': ObjectId('56ae5a5a1d41c865d6c133a4'), u'media_url': u'http://montrealgazette.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>"There\'s not a country in the world," a shaggy-haired Justin Trudeau told a curious Calgary audience in early October 2012, "that would find 170 billion barrels of oil under the ground and leave them there."</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau was launching the campaign that would make him Liberal leader. To be taken seriously, the young Quebecer needed to cast himself as an economic pragmatist. This required the careful creation of some ideological distance between himself and his dad, whose gifts, it is fair to say, lay outside economics. The symbol of choice would be Alberta oil.</p>&#13;<p>But here\'s the thing about pulling those 170 billion barrels from the ground: It follows that the resulting carbon emissions don\'t vary whether the oil goes to market through a pipeline, in a rail car or behind a tractor trailer. The oil gets burned. From a climate-change standpoint, it\'s a zero-sum game.</p>&#13;<p>So, what does it mean that Trudeau\'s government is now adding new layers of environmental and political scrutiny to the pipeline approvals process? And what does the rhetoric signify? In a nutshell, it means the Grits no longer consider Thomas Mulcair and his New Democrats to be a threat. Nor are they much concerned about Ontario conservatives, or Conservatives.</p>&#13;<p>In late 2012, Trudeau and his team faced two strategic problems, and one big opportunity.</p>&#13;<p>Problem 1 was a hard-charging Stephen Harper, pre-Mike Duffy Senate scandal. Problem 2 was the new NDP leader, Mulcair, who had won the leadership of his party on the promise of moving it to the centre.</p>&#13;<p>Mulcair also yielded the opportunity: In an apparent bid to court sentiment in Ontario\'s manufacturing heartland, he began talking up "Dutch disease," the ostensible hardship caused by high oil prices (we should wish for such hardship, now) and attacking TransCanada\'s Keystone XL plan to build a pipeline linking Alberta with the Texas Gulf Coast.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau backed Keystone unequivocally. In doing so he ticked off several important strategic boxes at a stroke. He made himself appear economically more serious than Mulcair. He got attention in Alberta. He reassured Ontario swing voters, always seeking a leader who can mind the store. And he cast himself as a pro-business Liberal, someone to whom a weary Harper voter might switch in a pinch.</p>&#13;<p>It worked, from a Liberal standpoint. Capping the strategy was that Harper himself, despite having crafted an omnibus budget in 2012 that seemed wholly designed to get Canadian resources to market, had failed to get results. Contrary to Trudeau\'s recent assertion, the former PM was never a cheerleader for these projects -- and that was part of the problem. In December 2013, when the National Energy Board joint review panel effectively kicked Enbridge\'s Northern Gateway plan to the curb, Harper and his ministers were mum.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau, through all this and into last year\'s election season, presented himself as the one leader who would help Alberta\'s economy, and by extension the rest of the country\'s, by furthering pipelines within the context of a new environmental regime that would be fair and transparent.</p>&#13;<p>Liberals will argue this is what they\'ve done with their new process. And that might hold some water, but for the accompanying rhetorical transmutation, which has Liberal ministers opposing the evils of unrestrained Big Oil.</p>&#13;<p>No one familiar with the Keystone debate will fail to interpret this as other than a sop to pipeline opponents, whose endgame is not sustainable development but the effective shutdown of northern Alberta. And few familiar with Trudeau\'s early leadership campaign will fail to perceive it as anything but a shift away from pragmatism, made possible by his overwhelming victory last Oct. 19.</p>&#13;<p>Twitter.com/mdentandt</p>&#13;<p>\xa0</p><p class="clearfix ads-container"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.kingstonthisweek.com/opinion/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.kingstonthisweek.com/2016/01/31/new-pipeline-process-reveals-trudeaus-shift', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.kingstonthisweek.com/2016/01/31/new-pipeline-process-reveals-trudeaus-shift', u'scraped_on': 1454288748, u'published_date': u'Sun, 31 Jan 2016 19:49:56 EST', u'title': u"New pipeline process reveals Trudeau's shift", u'_id': ObjectId('56aeaf6c1d41c86c4ecbe301'), u'media_url': u'http://www.kingstonthisweek.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>"There\'s not a country in the world," a shaggy-haired Justin Trudeau told a curious Calgary audience in early October 2012, "that would find 170 billion barrels of oil under the ground and leave them there."</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau was launching the campaign that would make him Liberal leader. Contrary to Trudeau\'s recent assertion, the former PM was never a cheerleader for these projects -- and that was part of the problem. And he cast himself as a pro-business Liberal, someone to whom a weary Harper voter might switch in a pinch.</p>&#13;<p>It worked, from a Liberal standpoint. Contrary to Trudeau\'s recent assertion, the former PM was never a cheerleader for these projects -- and that was part of the problem. In December 2013, when the National Energy Board joint review panel effectively kicked Enbridge\'s Northern Gateway plan to the curb, Harper and his ministers were mum.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau, through all this and into last year\'s election season, presented himself as the one leader who would help Alberta\'s economy, and by extension the rest of the country\'s, by furthering pipelines within the context of a new environmental regime that would be fair and transparent.</p>&#13;<p>Liberals will argue this is what they\'ve done with their new process. And that might hold some water, but for the accompanying rhetorical transmutation, which has Liberal ministers opposing the evils of unrestrained Big Oil.</p>&#13;<p>No one familiar with the Keystone debate will fail to interpret this as other than a sop to pipeline opponents, whose endgame is not sustainable development but the effective shutdown of northern Alberta. {u'full_article': u'<p>"There\'s not a country in the world," a shaggy-haired Justin Trudeau told a curious Calgary audience in early October 2012, "that would find 170 billion barrels of oil under the ground and leave them there."</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau was launching the campaign that would make him Liberal leader. To be taken seriously, the young Quebecer needed to cast himself as an economic pragmatist. This required the careful creation of some ideological distance between himself and his dad, whose gifts, it is fair to say, lay outside economics. The symbol of choice would be Alberta oil.</p>&#13;<p>But here\'s the thing about pulling those 170 billion barrels from the ground: It follows that the resulting carbon emissions don\'t vary whether the oil goes to market through a pipeline, in a rail car or behind a tractor trailer. The oil gets burned. From a climate-change standpoint, it\'s a zero-sum game.</p>&#13;<p>So, what does it mean that Trudeau\'s government is now adding new layers of environmental and political scrutiny to the pipeline approvals process? And what does the rhetoric signify? In a nutshell, it means the Grits no longer consider Thomas Mulcair and his New Democrats to be a threat. Nor are they much concerned about Ontario conservatives, or Conservatives.</p>&#13;<p>In late 2012, Trudeau and his team faced two strategic problems, and one big opportunity.</p>&#13;<p>Problem 1 was a hard-charging Stephen Harper, pre-Mike Duffy Senate scandal. Problem 2 was the new NDP leader, Mulcair, who had won the leadership of his party on the promise of moving it to the centre.</p>&#13;<p>Mulcair also yielded the opportunity: In an apparent bid to court sentiment in Ontario\'s manufacturing heartland, he began talking up "Dutch disease," the ostensible hardship caused by high oil prices (we should wish for such hardship, now) and attacking TransCanada\'s Keystone XL plan to build a pipeline linking Alberta with the Texas Gulf Coast.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau backed Keystone unequivocally. In doing so he ticked off several important strategic boxes at a stroke. He made himself appear economically more serious than Mulcair. He got attention in Alberta. He reassured Ontario swing voters, always seeking a leader who can mind the store. And he cast himself as a pro-business Liberal, someone to whom a weary Harper voter might switch in a pinch.</p>&#13;<p>It worked, from a Liberal standpoint. Capping the strategy was that Harper himself, despite having crafted an omnibus budget in 2012 that seemed wholly designed to get Canadian resources to market, had failed to get results. Contrary to Trudeau\'s recent assertion, the former PM was never a cheerleader for these projects -- and that was part of the problem. In December 2013, when the National Energy Board joint review panel effectively kicked Enbridge\'s Northern Gateway plan to the curb, Harper and his ministers were mum.</p>&#13;<p>Trudeau, through all this and into last year\'s election season, presented himself as the one leader who would help Alberta\'s economy, and by extension the rest of the country\'s, by furthering pipelines within the context of a new environmental regime that would be fair and transparent.</p>&#13;<p>Liberals will argue this is what they\'ve done with their new process. And that might hold some water, but for the accompanying rhetorical transmutation, which has Liberal ministers opposing the evils of unrestrained Big Oil.</p>&#13;<p>No one familiar with the Keystone debate will fail to interpret this as other than a sop to pipeline opponents, whose endgame is not sustainable development but the effective shutdown of northern Alberta. And few familiar with Trudeau\'s early leadership campaign will fail to perceive it as anything but a shift away from pragmatism, made possible by his overwhelming victory last Oct. 19.</p>&#13;<p>Twitter.com/mdentandt</p>&#13;<p>\xa0</p><p class="clearfix ads-container"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.thesudburystar.com/opinion/column/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.thesudburystar.com/2016/01/31/new-pipeline-process-reveals-trudeaus-shift', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.thesudburystar.com/2016/01/31/new-pipeline-process-reveals-trudeaus-shift', u'scraped_on': 1454289517, u'published_date': u'Sun, 31 Jan 2016 19:49:56 EST', u'title': u"New pipeline process reveals Trudeau's shift", u'_id': ObjectId('56aeb26d1d41c86ce92da803'), u'media_url': u'http://www.thesudburystar.com/'}
{u'full_article': u"<p>An aerial view of the 400 ton trucks and 100 ton electric rope shovels used at Suncor's Millennium Mine oilsands operation north of Fort McMurray.Ryan Jackson / Edmonton Journal</p><p>Verbal battles between Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre and western premiers and mayors are reruns of a bad old movie.</p><p>Let\u2019s change the channel and look at TransCanada\u2019s proposed Energy East pipeline in light of climate change. That was not on anyone\u2019s mind 35 years ago during tussles around the national energy program.</p><p>TransCanada\u2019s proposed Energy East pipeline is so capacious that it could single-handedly allow expansion of Alberta\u2019s sands by\xa0more than\xa040 per cent. That\u2019s the same percentage rise as the sands emission cap that Alberta\u2019s new climate action plan permits.</p><p>The rise of sands emissions from 70 million to 100 million tonnes a year will prevent Canada from meeting its laudable and ambitious targets at the Paris climate talks in December and Canada\u2019s 2009 commitment at the G8 meetings in L\u2019Aquila, Italy, to lower carbon emissions by 80\xa0per cent\xa0below Canada\u2019s 1990 level of 600 million tonnes by 2050. That means a drop to 120 million tonnes of greenhouse gases.</p><p>To hit Canada\u2019s promises, sands output and emissions must be capped at 70 million tonnes,\xa0then phased out. We can\u2019t get to a low carbon future unless we do so.</p><p>Some in the oilpatch wonder why pipelines are approved easily within the U.S, but not within Canada. The answer is simple. The four major oil pipelines under review in Canada are mainly sands oil exporting lines.</p><p>U.S. President Barack Obama killed the Keystone XL oil pipeline after protests. Prime Minister Justin Trudeau effectively killed the Northern Gateway line by banning oil tankers from B.C.\u2019s northern coast after public concerns.\xa0B.C. Premier Christy Clark\u2019s government opposed the expansion of Kinder Morgan\u2019s existing oil pipeline to the Vancouver area after resistance grew.</p><p>Opposition to Energy East is growing as strong.</p><p>Energy East is the final sands oil exporting line left standing. That\u2019s why Big Oil and western politicians are so desperate to get it approved. Desperation leads to flared tempers. But it doesn\u2019t mean it\u2019s the right thing to do.</p><p>Enbridge\u2019s Line 9 started bringing western Canadian oil and North Dakota shale oil to Quebec\u2019s two refineries in December. Those refineries will not take on additional oil from the Energy East line. All or most would flow to Irving Oil\u2019s refinery in Saint John, N.B.</p><p>However, Mark Sherman, plant manager at the Irving refinery has stated Energy East would send \u201cway more than we would ever use at this refinery, so the bulk of it would all be exported\u201d.</p><p>Alberta\u2019s sands output, the fastest growing source of Canada\u2019s greenhouse gas pollution,\xa0will grow substantially only if it has an outlet to a coast.</p><p>The U.S. Environmental Protection Agency pegs sands emissions much higher than conventional oil. Alberta\u2019s sands can\u2019t be greened.</p><p>It\u2019s the production of oil and natural gas, not in their use in transportation in Canada (cars and trucks), that is this country\u2019s biggest source of greenhouse gases.</p><p>Canada has just enough conventional, non-fracked, non-sands oil to supply all Canadians as we steadily lower consumption. It would also give every Canadian energy security. But there\u2019s not enough such oil to continue exports.</p><p>Why run a pipeline 4,600 kilometres to New Brunswick anyway when Newfoundland has enough conventional oil to supply the East? Most live on or near a coast. Why pipe it when it can be shipped?</p><p>That would avoid incursions on First Nations lands. Oil tankers could be phased out as East Coast use falls, whereas a pipeline would need three decades of shipping sands oil at full volume to amortize its building costs.\xa0</p><p>Prime Minister Trudeau and the premiers are scheduled to meet by March 11 to fashion a plan to transition Canada to a low-carbon future. They should nix Energy East.</p><p>Gordon Laxer is author of\xa0After the Sands. Energy and Ecological Security for Canadians\xa0and is founding director of Parkland Institute at the University of Alberta.</p><p>\xa0</p>", u'rss_url': u'http://edmontonjournal.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://edmontonjournal.com/opinion/columnists/opinion-energy-east-should-be-scrapped', u'author': u'Kathy Kerr', u'original_article_url': u'http://edmontonjournal.com/opinion/columnists/opinion-energy-east-should-be-scrapped', u'scraped_on': 1454290363, u'published_date': u'Mon, 01 Feb 2016 01:16:33 +0000', u'title': u'Opinion: Energy East should be scrapped', u'_id': ObjectId('56aeb5bb1d41c86d14e71f6c'), u'media_url': u'http://edmontonjournal.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p class="articlerelatedphotogallery section"></p><p class="articlerelatedvideogallery section"></p><p itemprop="articleBody">Moving away from a fossil-fuel-based economy will not be simple, but it is, ultimately, necessary</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Last week, the Liberal government added a speed bump and a potential detour in the path of transcontinental oil pipelines.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Environment and Climate Change Minister Catherine McKenna joined Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr in announcing that all resource projects, including pipelines, will now take into account the greenhouse gas emissions involved in their development, including extraction, delivery and processing.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The government will also appoint a ministerial adviser to consult with indigenous communities whose lands would be affected by such projects.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Although these are merely interim changes while a permanent review process is hammered out, the decision will directly affect the controversial Trans Mountain oil pipeline in B.C. and TransCanada\u2019s Energy East project, whose review periods are now extended four and six months respectively.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The stated intention is to restore \u201ctrust\u201d in a review process which was seen as compromised and high-handed under the previous government.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">While many environmental groups perceive the move as a positive step, they also note that it is hard, if not impossible, to square further infrastructure investment in tarsands oil with the December 2015 Paris commitment to significantly reduce greenhouse gas emissions.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Moving away from a fossil-fuel-based, \u2018pipeline economy\u2019 will not be simple, but it is, ultimately, necessary.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">U.S. President Barack Obama, in rejecting the Keystone XL pipeline last November, noted that the U.S. will continue to rely on oil and gas as \u201cwe transition, as we must transition\u201d to renewable energy.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Like Prime Minister Justin Trudeau, Obama followed an administration that embraced fossil fuels as the primary, and virtually unassailable, engine of economic prosperity. In torpedoing the Keystone pipeline, Obama stated that America is now a \u201cglobal leader\u201d in combating climate change, and approving such a project would \u201cundercut such leadership.\u201d</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Rejecting Keystone was thus a matter of \u201ckeeping face\u201d in a world increasingly recognizing the need to transition to a sustainable world economy, one which invests in a diversified field of energy production, including solar, wind and geothermal sources.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">At the recent World Economic Forum in Davos, Switzerland, Prime Minister Trudeau also strove to recast Canada\u2019s international eco-image, calling Canada\u2019s diversity the \u201cengine of invention.\u201d</p><p itemprop="articleBody">\u201cMy predecessor wanted you to know Canada for its resources,\u201d he said. \u201cI want you to know Canadians for our resourcefulness.\u201d</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Both Trudeau and Obama know the stakes, both politically and ecologically, are high.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">On January 20, while Trudeau was addressing world leaders in Davos, scientists from NASA and the U.S. National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) released their independent analyses of the planet\u2019s surface temperatures for the past year.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">2015 was clearly, according to both studies, the hottest year on record.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The NASA team reported that 2015 was more than a full degree Celsius warmer than temperatures in 1880, when regular record-keeping commenced.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">The findings are especially disconcerting in light of the stated aspiration of the Paris agreement not to exceed a 1.5-C temperature increase over pre-industrial temperatures.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">A pipeline economy is linear, extractive, and, at the end of the day, and the end of the pipeline, ecologically and economically deleterious.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">First, such infrastructure is based on high oil prices, which don\u2019t always remain high.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">When Brent crude recently dropped below US$28 a barrel, commentators noted that oil was now cheaper than the barrels it was stored in.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Second, pipelines have a tendency to leak.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Since 2011, Alberta alone has experienced five major pipeline spills. This past July, for example, one of the largest leaks in Alberta\u2019s history witnessed 5 million litres of a mixture of bitumen, water and sand ooze from a Nexen Energy pipeline south of Fort McMurray, coating an area of approximately 16,000 square metres. In addition, as reported by Global News, from 1976-2013, Alberta experienced an average of two crude oil spills a day. That amounts to 28,666 crude oil spills overall \u2014 that\u2019s a lot of leakage.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">To continue to build \u201ctrust,\u201d Canada\u2019s government is being invited to continue to shift responsibly away from a pipeline economy to an economy of diversity, one that invests in renewable energy and listens and responds to the concerns of those most affected by its energy decisions.</p><p data-type="endnote"><p itemprop="articleBody">Stephen Bede Scharper is associate professor of environment at the University of Toronto. Stephen.scharper@utoronto.ca </p></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/feeds.articles.opinion.rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/opinion/commentary/2016/02/01/moving-away-from-a-pipeline-economy-scharper.html', u'author': u'Stephen Bede Scharper - Christianity', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/opinion/commentary/2016/02/01/moving-away-from-a-pipeline-economy-scharper.html', u'scraped_on': 1454320399, u'published_date': u'Mon, 1 Feb 2016 04:30:00 EST', u'title': u'Moving away from a pipeline economy: Scharper', u'_id': ObjectId('56af2b0f1d41c87c71068c3b'), u'media_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/'}

THOMAS, Ont. -- Another fire in Ontario has killed a large number of livestock.</p>&#13;<p>Ontario Provincial Police said approximately 90 dairy cows died Monday morning in a barn fire in Elgin County, in southwestern Ontario.</p>&#13;<p>Const. Troy Carlson said 10 or 15 cows escaped the blaze, which caused damage estimated at $1.5 million.</p>&#13;<p>Carlson said it took firefighters several hours to bring the fire under control due to the large amount of stored hay, which is more difficult to extinguish.</p>&#13;<p>Last week, firefighters had to dodge spooked cows as they battled a blaze following a crash on the TransCanada Highway in central Ontario, that left a cattle transport driver and seven or eight cattle dead.</p>&#13;<p>More than 2,000 pigs died in mid-January in a barn fire north of London, Ont., while fires earlier in the month at two barns in the Guelph, Ont. area killed more than 50 horses, including 43 racehorses.</p>&#13;<p>About 500 goats and 30 cows were also killed in a barn fire in mid-January. {u'full_article': u'<p>No other major oil exporting nation would wilfully impose so many obstacles on the development of the struggling energy industry, a vital source of wealth in this country for the past several decades.DARRYL DYCK / THE CANADIAN PRESS</p><p>Does Canada have a death wish? That\u2019s how\xa0a veteran investment adviser I know characterizes this country\u2019s apparent\xa0desire to cripple\xa0its own energy industry in\xa0some weirdly misguided ideological campaign\xa0to save the planet.</p><p>The Trudeau government\u2019s latest move \u2014 to\xa0add some ill-defined climate test to the\xa0lengthy,\xa0years-long\xa0regulatory review process that already exists for new oil pipelines \u2014 makes him apoplectic. No wonder.</p><p>No other major oil exporting nation would\xa0wilfully impose so many obstacles\xa0on the development of the struggling\xa0energy industry, a vital source of wealth in this country for the past several decades.</p><p>Certainly not the U.S., despite President Barack Obama\u2019s green rhetoric. Definitely not Vladimir Putin\u2019s Russia, which is shipping more oil than ever to China.\xa0Not Saudi Arabia, Iraq, or any of the other members of the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries (OPEC).\xa0Not Alberta competitors\xa0such\xa0Mexico or Venezuela.\xa0Not Brazil, Australia or Indonesia. No one.\xa0</p><p>Canada alone, the global boy scout, makes the regulatory hurdles for new oil export pipelines all but impossible to satisfy.\xa0And for what? To\xa0pacify\xa0Canada\u2019s\xa0noisy environmental lobby, which will never be appeased? To keep aboriginal activists happy, despite the sky-high unemployment levels on most reserves?</p><p>And what is the pipeline opponents\u2019 overall objective,\xa0exactly? To shut in the oilsands and save the world from frying? Please, spare me the nonsensical rhetoric. The numbers don\u2019t add up.</p><p>The oilsands\xa0contribute less than\xa0two-tenth of one per cent of global carbon emissions. That\u2019s\xa00.002 per cent cent.\xa0How\xa0small\xa0is that? On\xa0a $75 tank of gasoline, 0.002 per cent equates to 15 cents. It\u2019s a\xa0rounding error.</p><p>Yet the Liberal government\xa0and much of the left-leaning Toronto-based media\xa0have convinced gullible Canadians that the very future of sacred\xa0Gaia\xa0hinges on keeping Alberta\u2019s bitumen in the ground. Alberta\u2019s climate change plan hasn\u2019t altered their radical views one iota.</p><p>The\xa0eco warriors\u2019\xa0fallback argument \u2014 that bitumen has a higher carbon footprint than conventional oil \u2014 is equally\xa0hyperbolic. It\u2019s like counting how many angels can do a jig on the head of a pin.\xa0In global terms, it\u2019s the square root of zilch.</p><p>On a well-to-wheels basis (i.e., between the wellhead and the gas tank), the difference is under 10\xa0per cent. It\xa0continues to shrink\xa0as oilsands producers become\xa0more efficient. What\u2019s more, much of the oil the U.S. imports from producers in Mexico, Venezuela or elsewhere carries\xa0virtually the same carbon footprint.</p><p>Yet while Obama slammed the door shut on TransCanada\u2019s Keystone XL pipeline, he hasn\u2019t said a word about imposing a\xa0carbon tax on Mexican or Iraqi\xa0imports. Nor does he seem\xa0averse to all the pipeline building going on under his watch in the U.S.</p><p>\u201cWe\u2019ve\xa0been building tons of pipelines in the U.S. It\xa0seems to have been a pretty friendly environment in terms of building oil pipelines in our country,\u201d says Jeff Mower, New York-based\xa0director of Americas oil news for Platts, an\xa0energy industry news and research organization.</p><p>\u201cMaybe\xa0not everywhere, but definitely going south\xa0from Cushing (a major oil storage hub in Oklahoma) there\u2019s been a lot of pipeline build-out. And now, with the lifting of the crude oil export restrictions, I imagine there is going to be more built,\u201d he adds. That includes the new Bakken oil pipeline to connect\xa0North Dakota\u2019s oilfields with refineries in Illinois.</p><p>If the CBC, Toronto Star and other left-leaning\xa0national media spent half as much time educating\xa0people\xa0on facts like these,\xa0rather than endlessly rehashing\xa0the latest anti-oil propaganda\xa0from Greenpeace, Neil Young, Bill McKibben or Leonardo (what\u2019s a chinook?) DiCaprio, perhaps Canadians\xa0could have an adult conversation about emissions or\xa0the need for new energy infrastructure. Right now, it\u2019s impossible.</p><p>Imagine if Ottawa imposed its\xa0new climate test on\xa0British Columbia\u2019s $5-billion coal export industry which conveniently bypasses\xa0B.C.\u2019s carbon tax? What if it applied its climate test on any new auto plants, on Quebec\u2019s\xa0aerospace industry\xa0(hello, Bombardier), or all\xa0new airport infrastructure? Cue the outrage. Yet Alberta is regarded as fair game.</p><p>Oh well, not to worry. I\u2019m sure\xa0Prime Minister Justin Trudeau will gladly explain all when\xa0he visits Alberta Premier Rachel Notley on her home turf this week.\xa0</p><p>Meanwhile, another contact of mine, a former\xa0senior bureaucrat\xa0in the provincial\xa0government, has calculated\xa0how much money\xa0Alberta taxpayers and firms\xa0have shipped\xa0to Ottawa\xa0over the past 15 years, over and above what the feds have reinvested here.</p><p>The\xa0figures\xa0he came up with are truly staggering \u2014 totalling many times the current value of the $17.4 billion Alberta Heritage Savings Trust Fund. Want to know why Alberta isn\u2019t as rich as Norway? You can start right there.</p><p>While he\u2019s here, perhaps Trudeau\xa0could set aside some time to\xa0address that topic. I\u2019m sure Albertans would\xa0appreciate his insights.</p><p>glamphier@postmedia.com</p><p>\xa0</p><p>\xa0</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://edmontonjournal.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://edmontonjournal.com/opinion/columnists/gary-lamphier-liberals-new-climate-test-latest-hurdle-for-pipelines', u'author': u'Gary Lamphier, Edmonton Journal', u'original_article_url': u'http://edmontonjournal.com/opinion/columnists/gary-lamphier-liberals-new-climate-test-latest-hurdle-for-pipelines', u'scraped_on': 1454376767, u'published_date': u'Tue, 02 Feb 2016 01:18:52 +0000', u'title': u"Gary Lamphier: Liberals' new 'climate test' latest hurdle for pipelines", u'_id': ObjectId('56b0073f1d41c810b3135afb'), u'media_url': u'http://edmontonjournal.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>Premier Rachel Notley releases the findings of the Alberta Royalty Review Advisory Panel in Calgary on Friday, Jan. 29, 2016. (Ted Rhodes/Postmedia)</p><p>As the policy drops continue, it becomes ever more obvious the NDP is imposing\xa0an all-embracing eco-industrial system\xa0unlike anything Alberta has ever seen.</p><p>This is nothing less than a revolution planned and directed from the legislature. For\xa0sheer scope and ambition, it far outstrips anything done by the surprising NDP demigod, the late PC premier Peter Lougheed.</p><p>The revolution spreads across climate change, carbon tax, pipelines, green incentives, energy royalties, personal taxes, debt accumulation and, as of Monday, direct incentives to petrochemical companies.</p><p>Premier Rachel Notley is determined to fashion a complex new \u201cprogressive\u201d economy that cannot be readily dismantled by some stray conservative government of the future.</p><p>\u201cIt\u2019s a genuine industrial strategy,\u201d Mount Royal University political scientist Keith Brownsey said admiringly after the royalty review was released Friday, \u201cand it\u2019s brilliant.\u201d</p><p>Maybe most\xa0of Alberta will say that some day. If it works.</p><p>That is, if it rescues the province from failing competitiveness, from the domestic war on Canadian oil, from hyper-dependence on one\xa0resource, from\xa0smarter opponents in both foreign boardrooms and around eco-campfires at home; if it creates jobs and green pop-up companies, and revenues that pull the province out of fiscal quicksand.</p><p>It is fascinating to see how the NDP, which is supposed to be inexperienced and incompetent, has pushed this agenda along so quickly that even the opposition parties are forced to keep catching up. They were surprisingly muted Monday.</p><p>Some NDP moves are just opportunistic\xa0rebranding of things the PCs were doing as\xa0they neared the end of days. The petrochemical incentives announced Monday, for instance, have been discussed for three years.</p><p>The New Democrats simply grab these smaller bricks,\xa0cement them into their much larger policy wall, and take credit for originality.</p><p>They\u2019re highly skilled at massaging issues and shaping messages. Last Friday, for instance, they turned most talk about the royalty review away from royalty rates and toward capital costs.</p><p>Almost lost in all this \u2014 by design, perhaps \u2014 was the plain fact that rates will be adjusted for wells drilled in 2017 and beyond. There was no explanation of what those increases\xa0would be.</p><p>Ex-premier Ed Stelmach wouldn\u2019t have got away with that. But the NDP mostly\xa0did, perhaps because they\u2019ve induced a numb exhaustion with change.</p><p>Inevitably, elements of the policy web don\u2019t make sense. Is it logical, for instance, to impose a carbon tax that will raise the price of natural gas, and then effectively lower that price for a select few, using $500 million in royalty credits for producers who supply petrochemical plants?</p><p>The policy is already picking winners and losers, both by sector and by region.</p><p>They were dancing in the aisles\xa0of Edmonton\u2019s \u201cIndustrial Heartland\u201d Monday when the petrochemical incentives were announced.</p><p>New plants that use heavily subsidized methane and propane will create plastics, fertilizer and other materials for the world will likely locate there (although it\u2019s unclear why they couldn\u2019t be elsewhere too.)</p><p>It\u2019s hard to resist the notion\xa0that this bonanza northeast of Edmonton is partial payback for the looming phase-out of the coal industry, which is located largely west of Edmonton.</p><p>The NDP doesn\u2019t seem worried by the increasingly hostile mood among\xa0some labour leaders, like AFL president Gil McGowan, who charges that the royalty report wasn\u2019t \u201cprogressive\u201d and ignored union input.</p><p>This government is\xa0after the great mass of suddenly job-hungry Albertans.\xa0That means stalling some progressive projects and pushing others ahead. But the whole structure remains\xa0the most radical the province has ever seen.</p><p>In his first two years, Lougheed reviewed and raised oil and gas royalties, bought Pacific Western Airlines, and withheld shipping permits from TransCanada (imagine that!) on grounds that prices were too low.\xa0</p><p>Lougheed was widely condemned as a socialist. People wondered how they could have elected such an inept amateur. But he became a hero after the policies gelled and\xa0Alberta began to prosper.</p><p>Notley surely longs for that future. She might just get it \u2014 although, compared to her, Lougheed was an amateur in the change game.</p><p>Don Braid\u2019s column appears regularly in the Herald</p><p>dbraid@calgayherald.com\xa0</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/opinion/columnists/braid-notleys-changes-add-up-to-an-alberta-revolution', u'author': u'Don Braid, Calgary Herald', u'original_article_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/opinion/columnists/braid-notleys-changes-add-up-to-an-alberta-revolution', u'scraped_on': 1454378646, u'published_date': u'Tue, 02 Feb 2016 01:58:05 +0000', u'title': u"Braid: Notley's changes add up to an Alberta revolution", u'_id': ObjectId('56b00e961d41c8110eaa7f10'), u'media_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>When the federal government revealed it was going to change the way the National Energy Board undertakes environmental assessments of pipelines, it was a sure sign the fate of Energy East would be delayed.</p>&#13;<p>Now we have an idea how much longer the wait will be.</p>&#13;<p>It\u2019ll be mid-2018 before we hear yea or nay from the NEB about TransCanada\u2019s pipeline proposal, part retrofit and part new construction, to carry oilsands and other types of crude to New Brunswick.</p>&#13;<p>Of course, there\u2019s no guarantee there won\u2019t be further delays, as was demonstrated by the process surrounding Energy East\u2019s trans-border cousin, Keystone XL.</p>&#13;<p>That project was tied up in protracted environment assessments and reassessments by the U.S. State Department until it was finally put out of its misery last year by President Barack Obama.</p>&#13;<p>But let\u2019s say Energy East\u2019s new 2018 deadline holds up and the future of the pipeline will indeed be known as scheduled.</p>&#13;<p>The timing will be interesting in many ways.</p>&#13;<p>According to a crude oil price forecast issued by Deloitte at the end of 2015, West Texas Intermediate is expected to recover to a yearly average of US$55 by 2018, with inflation accounted for.</p>&#13;<p>And before you scoff at forecasts, you might be interested to learn the yearly average price for crude in 2015 falls within the extreme low end of a U.S. Energy Information Agency outlook published in 2010. (Again, prices adjusted for inflation.)</p>&#13;<p>Looking at the economy as a whole, forecasts by Canada\u2019s Parliamentary Budget Office and international agencies predict Canada\u2019s GDP will continue to grow slowly and steadily over the next two years, despite the hit from low oil.</p>&#13;<p>Even Alberta, the epicentre of the oil slump\u2019s fallout, was pegged by RBC in a December forecast to grow 0.9% in 2016 and a more robust 2.7% in 2017.</p>&#13;<p>Should these forecasts come to pass, conditions will be such that the future of Canada\u2019s energy sector, energy-producing provinces and the wider economy won\u2019t be so dependent on the completion of any lone pipeline project \u2014 which is just the kind of thing politicians want to hear.</p>&#13;<p>Speaking of whom: There will also be interesting political fallout from delaying the outcome of Energy East until mid-2018.</p>&#13;<p>Provincially, Alberta will be into the twilight months of the NDP government\u2019s term, with an election expected in May 2019.</p>&#13;<p>Energy East has the potential to become the poster child of energy development under an NDP government.</p>&#13;<p>By then, it will become clear how effective it will have been for Team Notley to attempt to barter higher environmental standards for easing the way for oilsands crude to reach markets domestically and worldwide.</p>&#13;<p>Something similar will be seen on the national scene.</p>&#13;<p>With a federal election expected in fall 2019, the Energy East decision will precede the year-long pseudo-campaign as federal parties jockey for position ahead of the real thing.</p>&#13;<p>Just a few months ago, Justin Trudeau\u2019s Liberals campaigned (and won) on a pro-energy platform with more local input on big projects \u2014 including Energy East and Trans Mountain, a proposed B.C. pipeline expansion.</p>&#13;<p>Come election day next time around, we will know whether this was a bona fide attempt to give communities a stronger voice in developing energy projects, or whether it was a means of allowing the federal government to side-step important decisions by giving someone else the last word.</p>&#13;<p>Who could have known pipeline procrastination could turn out to be so productive.\xa0</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': {u'full_article': u"<p>Premier Brad Wall's argument on Energy East and equalization isn't helpful.TROY FLEECE / Regina Leader-Post</p><p>From Quebec\u2019s enviro-nationalists to Alberta\u2019s old energy apologists, few are tackling the issue of TransCanada\u2019s contentious Energy East pipeline with any semblance of balance.\xa0Some are even exacerbating what has now become a minor national unity crisis.</p><p>When the autopsy of this messy debate is conducted, at least three men will be found responsible: Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre, Saskatchewan Premier Brad Wall, and the CBC\u2019s Rick Mercer. Consumed with their narrow interests, the politicians seem to have forgotten that Canada remains a federation, not a collection of fiefdoms, while the ranting comedian reduces Canada\u2019s complex equalization system to a series of transactions between East and West.</p><p>\u201cWe all need this thing,\u201d Mercer said, clever as always, but failing to demonstrate that we truly need this one particular oil pipeline as a magic bullet to rescue Canada\u2019s sluggish economy. Mercer\u2019s Quebecois counterpart, Jean-Rene \u201cInfoman\u201d Dufort, joined a chorus of parody videos pointing out that receiving equalization payments doesn\u2019t mean Quebec should embrace new pipelines while British Columbia and the United States reject them.</p><p>While asymmetrical federalism can have its drawbacks, Wall expressed on social media that he would seemingly prefer a form of ransom federalism, where an oil empowered western premier acts as if he has the authority to hang federal equalization dollars over the heads of Quebecers.</p><p>\u201cI trust Montreal-area mayors will politely return their share of $10-billion in equalization supported by west(ern provinces),\u201d Wall quipped, to which Parti Quebecois MNA Jean-Francois Lisee replied, \u201cI trust the West will refund the $10 billion of Quebec taxes spent on big oil tax breaks and grants over the years.\u201d</p><p>It\u2019s a childish debate for Wall to ignite, one that fails to consider the purpose of federal governments. He should know that pitting regions against each other fuels sovereigntist hysteria and is counterproductive for the 100,000 workers who lost their jobs last year as a result of the downturn in the energy sector.</p><p>\u201cSay thank you,\u201d Coderre then tweeted, waving those same federal dollars over the heads of Saskatchewanians while Conservative interim leader Rona Ambrose warned that Coderre\u2019s rhetoric \u201cisn\u2019t in the spirit of national unity.\u201d (Neither was Wall\u2019s.)</p><p>The Rest of Canada\u2019s position is seen as paternalistic by some Quebec commentators. \u201cColonialist schemes\u201d are forcing Quebecers into a position of \u201cconstant submission,\u201d wrote sovereigntist thinker Mathieu Bock-Cote. Apparently eager to capitalize on western missteps, Quebecor colleague Josee LeGault even compared the Energy East spat to the failure of the Meech Lake constitutional reforms (wishful thinking)!</p><p>I trust Prime Minister Justin Trudeau has reminded his provincial and municipal counterparts that, on a handful of federal files like Energy East, it simply isn\u2019t the prerogative of regions to set the tone of the debate. There is no need for such firmly entrenched stances on either side when the National Energy Board and Quebec\u2019s Office of Environmental Public Hearings have yet to complete their work.</p><p>Communication on the issue has been so muddled that a source close to the industry mused that \u201cthe biggest question most journalists have is whether the\xa0pipelines will be underground or above ground.\u201d\xa0 Energy East would be underground, even under the St. Lawrence River, which presents fewer environmental risks than transport via an overloaded rail system. If journalists are confused about the Energy East plan, the public at large is surely disconnected from the debate, making polls on the issue largely irrelevant.</p><p>The pipeline is a private endeavour that is for TransCanada\u2019s, not Wall, to sell to Canadians. It may be seen as necessary, but it is not free of risk and certainly isn\u2019t an example of sustainable development.</p><p>While regional considerations should be just that, considerations, a final Energy East decision should be based on sound science resulting from rigorous consultation and evaluation \u2014 not what\u2019s best for Coderre\u2019s fiefdom or Wall\u2019s. Conservative panic over a struggling oil industry, however, has backfired and placed TransCanada in a more disadvantageous position as the prime minister, among his considerations, now must consider growing discontent among Quebec nationalists.</p><p> Dan Delmar is a Montreal public relations consultant, writer and broadcaster.</p>", u'rss_url': u'http://thestarphoenix.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://thestarphoenix.com/opinion/letters/0203-edit-delmar-view', u'author': u'Sarath Peiris, Saskatoon StarPhoenix', u'original_article_url': u'http://thestarphoenix.com/opinion/letters/0203-edit-delmar-view', u'scraped_on': 1454505526, u'published_date': u'Wed, 03 Feb 2016 12:52:24 +0000', u'title': u'Viewpoint: Wall ignites childish debate over Energy East', u'_id': ObjectId('56b1fe361d41c87573c7defb'), u'media_url': u'http://thestarphoenix.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>MONTREAL -- The rejection by Montreal-area municipal leaders of TransCanada Corp.\'s proposed Energy East pipeline prompted a sharp political rebuke from some parts of Western Canada on Thursday.</p><p>The Montreal Metropolitan Community, which represents 82 municipalities and 3.9 million residents, said it opposes the project and will defend that position at Quebec environmental impact and National Energy Board hearings.</p><p>Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre, the current president of the organization, said the decision was unanimous and that the environmental risks of a spill far outweigh any economic benefits for the region.</p><p>That position sparked anger in Alberta and Saskatchewan.</p><p>Deron Bilous, Alberta\'s minister of economic development and trade, said his province is now doing its share to combat climate change while facing tremendous challenges with the collapse of oil prices.</p><p>"We all look to the same resource revenues to create jobs and fund the social programs we all depend on," Bilous said. "The mayor of Montreal\'s statement today is therefore both ungenerous and short-sighted. Everyone loses if we destroy our resource economy."</p><p>Saskatchewan Premier Brad Wall called it sad that leaders from Quebec would be so "parochial" about a project that would benefit all of Canada.</p><p>Wall said Quebec will receive about $10 billion in equalization payments this year.</p><p>"For the better part of the past decade the western Canadian energy sector and western Canadian taxpayers have supported a great portion of these transfer payments as well as the Canadian economy," he said in a statement.</p><p>"Is it too much to expect that these Quebec municipal leaders would respond to this reality with generous support for a pipeline that supports the very sector that has supported them?"</p><p>Coderre said the project is worth about $2 million a year in economic benefits to the Montreal area, while the cleanup of a major oil spill could cost between $1 billion and $10 billion.</p><p>Environmental consultations were held across the Montreal territory last September and October and Coderre said the majority of the 140 groups that submitted briefs were opposed to the project.</p><p>TransCanada maintains that pipelines are safer and more environmentally friendly than rail for transporting oil.</p><p>A TransCanada spokesman was hopeful the company could keep channels open with elected Montreal-area officials.</p><p>"We will continue to listen to other elected leaders in Quebec and stakeholders across the province as we take their concerns and input seriously," Jonathan Abecassis said in a statement.</p><p>The proposed pipeline would take Alberta crude as far east as an Irving Oil refinery in Saint John, N.B., and would be capable of carrying up to 1.1 million barrels a day from the West to the East.</p><p>The project would include existing TransCanada (TSX:TRP) pipeline as far east as Montreal, plus new pipeline to be constructed through Quebec.</p><p>Wildrose Leader Brian Jean said the Alberta NDP government\'s strategy around pipelines is failing and that it\'s time Premier Rachel Notley started standing up for the province against "unfair attacks."</p><p>"While Mr. Coderre dumps a billion litres of raw sewage directly into his waterways and benefits from billions in equalization payments, his opposition to the Energy East pipeline is nothing short of hypocritical," Jean said.</p><p>"Montreal buys millions of {u'full_article': u'<p>MONTREAL \u2014 The company behind the proposed\xa0Energy East Pipeline ramped up its Quebec charm offensive on Wednesday, announcing a deal to create more than 120 jobs if the project proceeds.</p><p>TransCanada (TSX:TRP) is hoping it can\xa0woo Quebecers to get on side with the multibillion-dollar\xa0pipeline after it signed an agreement with power equipment manufacturer ABB Canada to build 22 electrical houses in the Montreal region.</p><p>But Energy East will be a tough sell in Quebec. Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre along with more than 80 other\xa0municipal leaders recently rejected the pipeline over environmental concerns, accusing TransCanada of arrogance.</p><p>John Soini, the new president of Energy East, made sure to deliver the majority of his announcement in French, despite difficulty speaking the language.</p><p>His presence Wednesday was part of what the company calls its "new approach" towards convincing Quebecers that the\xa0$15.7-billion pipeline project is in the province\'s best economic interests and that\xa0TransCanada cares about citizens\' concerns.</p><p>"For me, it\'s all about listening,"\xa0Soini said, adding that Quebecers should expect to see him more often. "It\'s all about being in the community. I\'m happy to be here and to spend time here. I\'m not a big-city guy. I\'m not a stuffed suit."</p><p>TransCanada plans to build a pipeline from Alberta\'s oilsands that would wind its way through Montreal and ending in Saint John, N.B.</p><p>The project would ship 1.1 million barrels of oil a day and allow Alberta\'s crude \u2014 which is currently landlocked\xa0\u2014 to be exported to international markets other than the United States.</p><p>While Coderre has voiced his concerns with Energy East, he has also signalled he is open to changing his mind if TransCanada "does its homework."</p><p>Soini\xa0said the agreement announced Wednesday, which would create 120 temporary jobs, is contingent on Energy East getting sanctioned. The deal with ABB Canada \u2014 a division of\xa0ABB Group in Switzerland \u2014 is also expected to create another 90 spinoff jobs outside of the Montreal area, TransCanada said.</p><p>The electrical houses that would be built under the deal are prefabricated structures that\xa0contain electrical and automation equipment\xa0for powering pump stations along the pipeline.</p><p>Getting the green light for Energy East will likely take more than promises of temporary jobs, however.</p><p>Environmental activists continue to push governments to stop further development of the oilsands. They argue that any new pipeline will increase global oil supply and therefore increase greenhouse gas emissions.</p><p>Jeff Gaulin of the Canadian Association of Petroleum Producers, who attended Wednesday\'s announcement, said world oil demand will increase for some time and pipelines have been proven to be the safest way of transporting it.</p><p>"You\'re never going to win an argument with someone who says, \'No more oil,\' and\xa0politicians who take that view are unlikely to ever support a pipeline," Gaulin said.</p><p>Louis Bergeron, Energy East\'s vice-president for New Brunswick and Quebec, said he appreciates that in order to have this project accepted TransCanada needs to show Quebecers there is more in it for them.</p><p>"Coderre mentioned several times that we have to do our homework," Bergeron said. "So I think today what we\'re saying is that we are doing it. We have a lot more coming up and it\'s my job to make sure we satisfy this principle that the Quebec government requested for us to maximize the economic benefits of this project."</p><p>Giuseppe Valiante, The Canadian Press</p><p>\xa92016 The Canadian Press</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://cjme.com/rss-feed/6194', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://cjme.com/article/460323/transcanada-tries-win-over-quebec-energy-east-promise-jobs', u'author': u'The Canadian Press', u'original_article_url': u'http://cjme.com/article/460323/transcanada-tries-win-over-quebec-energy-east-promise-jobs', u'scraped_on': 1454527879, u'published_date': u'Wed, 03 Feb 16 12:56:37 -0600', u'title': u'TransCanada tries to win over Quebec on Energy East with promise of jobs', u'_id': ObjectId('56b255871d41c80208e1de2e'), u'media_url': u'http://cjme.com/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>OTTAWA \u2014 The controversial Energy East pipeline\xa0has now been endorsed by a provincial premier from each of Canada\'s three main political parties,\xa0interim Conservative leader Rona Ambrose\xa0said Wednesday in the House of Commons.</p><p>She was, of course, trying to score political points by depicting Justin Trudeau\'s federal Liberal government as dragging its heels on TransCanada\'s proposed $15.7-billion west-to-east energy project.</p><p>But the comment illustrates a much broader point, one echoed earlier this week at an energy conference in Ottawa: it\'s time Canada\'s environmental and energy proponents stopped talking past one another.</p><p>That theme was driven home by Michael Cleland, a former senior bureaucrat and energy industry leader who gave the keynote address Tuesday at an annual forum studying\xa0Canada\'s energy landscape.</p><p>"Almost 30 years after the emergence of the climate debate, the\xa0public discussions on energy and climate continue\xa0as if they were\xa0being conducted on different planets," Cleland told a room full of energy industry executives and diplomats.</p><p>His comments came as Trudeau was travelling west for two days of talks in Edmonton and Calgary with NDP Premier Rachel Notley and others to discuss Alberta\'s foundering oilpatch.</p><p>And while much of the immediate political fracas is centred on pipelines, their approval process and market access for landlocked oil and gas in a sector devastated by $30-per-barrel oil, there are much bigger, longer-term forces at play.</p><p>Cleland, who parlayed senior positions with Finance and Natural Resources Canada into leading roles with the Canadian Electricity Association and the\xa0Canadian Gas Association, is now a senior fellow at the University of Ottawa. He says\xa0"real transformative change in energy is rather rare" and hasn\'t happened in living memory \u2014 dating back to the\xa0widespread electrification of\xa0society and the development of the internal combustion engine.</p><p>Nonetheless, Cleland says the world appears to be on the cusp of another transformation.</p><p>While Canada\'s carbon-intensive energy commodities are sure to be needed for the foreseeable future,\xa0he said, the long-term trend is in\xa0"one direction."</p><p>As Cleland noted, the International Energy Agency\'s base case forecast sees emissions of greenhouse gases climbing 16 per cent from 2013 to 2040. Yet in December in Paris, the same governments who are members of the IEA\xa0"cheerfully adopted" a goal\xa0to hold global warming to 1.5 degrees Celsius by 2050, requiring a drastic reduction in global emissions.</p><p>Cleland called it "refreshing" to see provincial, territorial and federal environment ministers "connect some of the dots" last week after meeting in Ottawa, when they frankly acknowledged that Canada\xa0is not on track to meet its 2020 or 2030 emissions targets.</p><p>"What the environment ministers did not say was that to meet any of the 2030 national targets being bruited about implies a change in trajectory which is probably impossible under any circumstances," he said.</p><p>"Are we willing to tell consumers that their energy costs need to go up by 50 to 100 per cent or more \u2014 pretty much starting tomorrow \u2014 in order to meet our 2030 greenhouse gas commitments?"</p><p>Those are the hard realities that governments of all stripes, industry, environmentalists and citizens at large need to digest and discuss.</p><p>"Reconciling our energy and climate aspirations will involve the biggest change in our energy systems in over 100 years."</p><p>But as Cleland put it,\xa0restoring public confidence in institutions,\xa0maintaining investor confidence in Canadian resources,\xa0reducing GHGs and moving on leading technologies are\xa0mutually compatible goals, "if we\'re realistic about it."</p><p>\u2014 Follow @BCheadle on Twitter</p><p>Bruce Cheadle, The Canadian Press</p><p>\xa92016 The Canadian Press</p>', u'rss_url': {u'full_article': u'<p>OTTAWA \u2014 The controversial Energy East pipeline has now been endorsed by a provincial premier from each of Canada\'s three main political parties, interim Conservative leader Rona Ambrose said Wednesday in the House of Commons.</p><p>She was, of course, trying to score political points by depicting Justin Trudeau\'s federal Liberal government as dragging its heels on TransCanada\'s proposed $15.7-billion west-to-east energy project.</p><p>But the comment illustrates a much broader point, one echoed earlier this week at an energy conference in Ottawa: it\'s time Canada\'s environmental and energy proponents stopped talking past one another.</p><p>That theme was driven home by Michael Cleland, a former senior bureaucrat and energy industry leader who gave the keynote address Tuesday at an annual forum studying Canada\'s energy landscape.</p><p>"Almost 30 years after the emergence of the climate debate, the public discussions on energy and climate continue as if they were being conducted on different planets," Cleland told a room full of energy industry executives and diplomats.</p><p>His comments came as Trudeau was travelling west for two days of talks in Edmonton and Calgary with NDP Premier Rachel Notley and others to discuss Alberta\'s foundering oilpatch.</p><p>And while much of the immediate political fracas is centred on pipelines, their approval process and market access for landlocked oil and gas in a sector devastated by $30-per-barrel oil, there are much bigger, longer-term forces at play.</p><p>Cleland, who parlayed senior positions with Finance and Natural Resources Canada into leading roles with the Canadian Electricity Association and the Canadian Gas Association, is now a senior fellow at the University of Ottawa. He says "real transformative change in energy is rather rare" and hasn\'t happened in living memory \u2014 dating back to the widespread electrification of society and the development of the internal combustion engine.</p><p>Nonetheless, Cleland says the world appears to be on the cusp of another transformation.</p><p>While Canada\'s carbon-intensive energy commodities are sure to be needed for the foreseeable future, he said, the long-term trend is in "one direction."</p><p>As Cleland noted, the International Energy Agency\'s base case forecast sees emissions of greenhouse gases climbing 16 per cent from 2013 to 2040. Yet in December in Paris, the same governments who are members of the IEA "cheerfully adopted" a goal to hold global warming to 1.5 degrees Celsius by 2050, requiring a drastic reduction in global emissions.</p><p>Cleland called it "refreshing" to see provincial, territorial and federal environment ministers "connect some of the dots" last week after meeting in Ottawa, when they frankly acknowledged that Canada is not on track to meet its 2020 or 2030 emissions targets.</p><p>"What the environment ministers did not say was that to meet any of the 2030 national targets being bruited about implies a change in trajectory which is probably impossible under any circumstances," he said.</p><p>"Are we willing to tell consumers that their energy costs need to go up by 50 to 100 per cent or more \u2014 pretty much starting tomorrow \u2014 in order to meet our 2030 greenhouse gas commitments?"</p><p>Those are the hard realities that governments of all stripes, industry, environmentalists and citizens at large need to digest and discuss.</p><p>"Reconciling our energy and climate aspirations will involve the biggest change in our energy systems in over 100 years."</p><p>But as Cleland put it, restoring public confidence in institutions, maintaining investor confidence in Canadian resources, reducing GHGs and moving on leading technologies are mutually compatible goals, "if we\'re realistic about it."</p><p>\u2014 Follow @BCheadle on Twitter</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/search/?c%5B%5D=news%2Fnational%2A&d=&d1=&d2=&f=rss&l=25&q=&s=start_time&sd=desc&t=article', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/news/national/energy-conference-hears-it-s-time-for-frank-talk-on/article_99ffd105-6ae8-52f7-933f-c1f6d3653c5b.html', u'author': u'Bruce Cheadle The Canadian Press', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/news/national/energy-conference-hears-it-s-time-for-frank-talk-on/article_99ffd105-6ae8-52f7-933f-c1f6d3653c5b.html', u'scraped_on':
