{u'full_article': u'&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13; &#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;The TransCanada Pipelines Ltd. building in downtown Calgary at 450 1 St. SW. On Aug. 1, 2013, the company announced it was proceeding with the Energy East Pipeline project. Sun File&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="premiumStoryIcon"/>&#13;&#13;Article&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="zero">Report an error</p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p>The National Energy Board is directing TransCanada to rework its application to build the contentious Energy East pipeline because the document is too hard to understand \u2014 even for experts. &#13;The federal energy watchdog, in a letter to the Calgary-based pipeline builder (TSX:TRP), said it\'s not requesting a whole new application.&#13;Rather, it wants the reams of information already filed to be repackaged in a way that is easier to navigate.&#13;The company first submitted its application to build the Alberta-to-New-Brunswick pipeline in October 2014.&#13;The document was 30,000 pages at the time \u2014 filling 68 binders in 11 boxes \u2014 and the NEB says it has since become even more unwieldy.&#13;One of the more noteworthy changes was a decision to scrap a planned export terminal in Cacouna, Que., due to concerns over the impact on the beluga whale habitat. The project now includes only one marine export terminal in Saint John, N.B.&#13;"When considering the numerous supplemental reports, project updates, errata and amendments coupled with the sheer volume of information presented in the application, the board is of the view that the application, in its present form, is difficult even for experts to navigate," the board wrote to TransCanada in a letter Wednesday.&#13;"The board is concerned that it will be even more difficult for the general public to comprehend and navigate. The board is also concerned about the impact of this on the fairness and efficiency of the hearing process and the potential burdens on all parties.&#13;TransCanada (TSX:TRP) has until Feb. 17 to provide a proposed table of contents for the revised version and let the board know when it plans to file the complete document.&#13;"Public participation is a fundamental element of our hearing process," the board wrote.&#13;"It is critical that, during the hearing process, participants are able to understand the application and easily locate sections of the application most relevant to their interests."&#13;The $15.7-billion pipeline would ship a million barrels of western crude a day as far east as Saint John. The goal is to connect Canadian crude to domestic refineries as well as international customers.&#13;TransCanada spokesman Mark Cooper said despite the additional work, the company is focused on a 2020 startup date for Energy East.&#13;"This is an opportunity for us to restate our desire to have informed conversations with Canadians about Energy East," he said.&#13;"Energy East is supportive of taking constructive steps to make the review process as accessible as possible. Therefore, we will take the NEB\'s direction and work to prepare consolidated applications in both English and French, to assist all Canadians in better understanding this important infrastructure project."</p>&#13;<p>Related content: Opposition to Energy East pipeline might have backfired: Expert </p>&#13;&#13;&#13;&#13;<p class="ga-bottom" id="ga-bottom-main"></p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.calgarysun.com/news/rss.xml', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.calgarysun.com/2016/02/03/national-energy-board-says-energy-east-application-too-hard-to-understand-orders-changes', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.calgarysun.com/2016/02/03/national-energy-board-says-energy-east-application-too-hard-to-understand-orders-changes', u'scraped_on': 1454540758, u'published_date': u'Wed, 03 Feb 2016 14:11:47 MST', u'title': u'National Energy Board says Energy East application too hard to understand, orders changes', u'_id': ObjectId('56b287da1d41c80f21188d71'), u'media_url': u'http://www.calgarysun.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>CALGARY -- The National Energy Board is directing TransCanada to rework its application for the contentious Energy East pipeline because the document is too hard to understand -- even for experts.</p><p>The federal energy watchdog, in a letter to the Calgary-based pipeline builder (TSX:TRP) on Wednesday, said it\'s not requesting a whole new application.</p><p>Rather, it wants the reams of information already filed to be repackaged in a way that is easier to navigate.</p><p>The company first submitted its application to build the 4,600-kilometre pipeline in October 2014.</p><p>The document was 30,000 pages at the time -- filling 68 binders in 11 boxes -- and the NEB says it has since become even more unwieldy.</p><p>One of the more noteworthy changes was a decision to scrap a planned export terminal in Cacouna, Que., due to concerns over the impact on the beluga whale habitat. The project now includes only one marine export terminal in Saint John, N.B.</p><p>Its price tag has also risen from $12 billion to $15.7 billion.</p><p>"When considering the numerous supplemental reports, project updates, errata and amendments coupled with the sheer volume of information presented in the application, the board is of the view that the application, in its present form, is difficult even for experts to navigate," the board told TransCanada.</p><p>"The board is concerned that it will be even more difficult for the general public to comprehend and navigate. The board is also concerned about the impact of this on the fairness and efficiency of the hearing process and the potential burdens on all parties."</p><p>TransCanada (TSX:TRP) has until Feb. 17 to provide a proposed table of contents for the revised version and let the board know when it plans to file the complete document. The English and French versions must mirror each other.</p><p>Adam Scott, with Environmental Defence, said groups wanting to participate in the review have had a tough time making sense of the application.</p><p>"From the perspective of anybody trying to learn about it, for over a year now it\'s been nearly impossible to get all of the right information," he said.</p><p>Scott said an easier-to-follow application is welcome, but he\'s skeptical all the information gaps can be filled this way -- especially given the sheer scale of the project.</p><p>"I don\'t think it\'s going to resolve the issue," said Scott.</p><p>"(But) they can certainly make a more readable, cleaned-up version."</p><p>The pipeline would ship a million barrels of western crude a day as far east as Saint John, N.B. The goal is to connect Canadian crude to domestic refineries as well as international customers.</p><p>TransCanada spokesman Mark Cooper said despite the additional work, the company is focused on a 2020 startup date for Energy East.</p><p>"This is an opportunity for us to restate our desire to have informed conversations with Canadians about Energy East," he said.</p><p>"Energy East is supportive of taking constructive steps to make the review process as accessible as possible. Therefore, we will take the NEB\'s direction and work to prepare consolidated applications in both English and French, to assist all Canadians in better understanding this important infrastructure project."</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://edmonton.ctvnews.ca/rss/ctv-news-edmonton-1.822347', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.ctvnews.ca/business/energy-east-application-too-hard-to-understand-national-energy-board-1.2763683', u'author': u'Lauren Krugel', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.ctvnews.ca/business/energy-east-application-too-hard-to-understand-national-energy-board-1.2763683', u'scraped_on': 1454541442, u'published_date': u'Wed, 3 Feb 2016 14:38:00 -0700', u'title': u'Energy East application too hard to understand: National Energy Board', u'_id': ObjectId('56b28a821d41c80faf161f49'), u'media_url': u'http://edmonton.ctvnews.ca/'}

{u'full_article': u'<p>Prime Minister Justin Trudeau speaks to media after meeting with Alberta Premier Rachel Notley in Edmonton on Wednesday, Feb. 3, 2016.Jason Franson / THE CANADIAN PRESS</p><p>With\xa0Prime Minister Justin Trudeau coming\xa0to the heart of the oilpatch\xa0on Thursday\xa0\u2014 and facing harsh criticism over his\xa0government\u2019s pipeline stance \u2014\xa0Calgary\u2019s\xa0top Liberal says there should be no\xa0question about Ottawa\u2019s support of the energy industry.</p><p>Trudeau, along with Premier Rachel Notley and federal Natural Resources Minister Jim Carr, will take part in two roundtables \u2014 one with oil and gas producers, and another\xa0with energy service companies \u2014 as well as tour the downtown YWCA.\xa0</p><p>Veterans Affairs Minister Kent Hehr, whose Calgary Centre riding encompasses downtown and most of the head offices of Canada\u2019s energy\xa0giants, said Trudeau is coming to\xa0show that \u201cAlberta has a true partner\u201d in his government.\xa0</p><p>\u201cOur national government is acutely aware that Alberta is struggling,\u201d he said.</p><p>\u201cWe want to be there to talk with stakeholders, meet with people who are currently struggling and show that we understand this\xa0\u2014\xa0that we as a government are listening, learning and we\u2019re going to be reacting to what\u2019s happening on the ground.\u201d</p><p>Oil prices that have plunged over the past year \u2014\xa0closing Wednesday at\xa0US$32.28 a barrel \u2014\xa0have wreaked havoc on the energy industry, leading to more than 40,000 oilpatch layoffs and sending the provincial economy into recession.</p><p>But the Trudeau government is facing fierce criticism from the Opposition Conservatives for its handling of energy issues.</p><p>The Tories have blasted the government for introducing new interim measures\xa0that will extend the ongoing review process for the Energy East and Trans Mountain oil pipeline proposals.</p><p>The Liberals recently voted down a Conservative motion calling on the government to back Energy East, which would ship Alberta oilsands crude to New Brunswick for refining.</p><p>Opening new markets for Alberta energy is seen by the province as vital for restoring economic growth, enabling petroleum producers to access higher prices for their crude\xa0\u2014 and generate more royalties for government coffers.\xa0</p><p>In Edmonton, Trudeau met with Notley on Wednesday and the two leaders issued a joint statement saying they agreed \u201cwe must get these resources to market in responsible, sustainable ways.\u201d</p><p>The prime minister would not answer a question on whether the Liberal cabinet would green light TransCanada\u2019s $15.7-billion Energy East project if it meets with National Energy Board approval, saying he would not \u201cpredict or\xa0shortcut any of the processes going through.\u201d</p><p>Trudeau said Canadians want a rigorous evaluation process, and he dismissed the approach of the previous Conservative government toward pipelines.\xa0</p><p>\u201cMarginalizing community concerns, ignoring environmental science, refusing to engage with indigenous communities, was not a way to fast-track pipelines,\u201d he said.</p><p>Earlier in the day in Ottawa, Conservative MP Michelle Rempel said Trudeau is creating\xa0new uncertainty with the government\u2019s changes to a\xa0pipeline regulatory process that was the \u201cmost open, most fair, most well respected, scientific environmental review processes in the world.\u201d</p><p class="p1">\u201cThe government needs to stop blaming this solely on low commodity prices,\u201d said the Calgary Nose Hill MP.</p><p class="p1">\u201cFrankly, every single thing that we have heard from the government so far is like a checklist on how to, you know, ruin the western Canadian economy.\u201d</p><p>With files from {u'full_article': u'<p class="articlerelatedphotogallery section"></p><p class="articlerelatedvideogallery section"></p><p itemprop="articleBody">EDMONTON\u2014Kudos to Justin Trudeau and Alberta\u2019s Rachel Notley for taking the time to talk face-to-face about the province\u2019s economic challenges on Wednesday. The Liberal prime minister has a compelling interest in keeping the channels open with the NDP premier.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">If Trudeau can\u2019t get along with Notley, he is unlikely to get along with any Alberta government. Her two top advisers \u2014 Richard Dicerni on the public service side and Brian Topp in the more political role of chief-of-staff \u2014 know the federal capital inside out and have worked with the federal Liberals in the past.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Dicerni served in a senior role in the unity backrooms of Pierre Trudeau\u2019s government at the time of the 1980 Quebec referendum. Topp was Jack Layton\u2019s lead negotiator in the talks that led to the 2008 Liberal-NDP coalition accord.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">But while the prime minister can offer Notley some financial relief in the upcoming federal budget, the real test of their relationship will be the resolution of the pipeline file and the fate of Alberta\u2019s efforts to connect the oilsands to tidewater.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">It is by far the most contentious issue on the federal-provincial radar. Yet, this is one national discussion that is unlikely to be resolved between first ministers. To date, the lead players in the debate have mostly been municipal politicians.</p>Joanne Monaghan was the mayor of Kitimat, B.C., on whose watch a plebiscite was held on the Northern Gateway pipeline in 2014. As the end point for the pipeline bringing oil from Alberta, Kitimat would be home to a marine terminal where tankers would load up. Almost 60 per cent of her constituents voted no, driving another nail in the coffin of the moribund $6.5-billion Enbridge project.Derek Corrigan is the mayor of Burnaby, B.C. The Trans Mountain pipeline runs through the city and its corporate owner, Kinder Morgan, wants to triple its capacity. Corrigan is hardly the only Vancouver-area mayor to oppose the pipeline but he is one of the most vocal. Last May, he told a community meeting he was prepared to be arrested and see his political career come to an end to stop the project. He called last week\u2019s federal announcement of an expanded consultation process and an extended approval timeline a disappointment.<p class="text combinedtext parbase section">Marc Demers is the mayor of Laval, Quebec\u2019s third largest city, and one of 82 Montreal-area municipal leaders who came out against the Energy East pipeline. While most Canadians see Denis Coderre as the face of the region\u2019s opposition to TransCanada\u2019s current plans, Demers has been campaigning hard against the pipeline since early last fall.</p><p class="text combinedtext parbase section">Gilles Lehouillier is the major of L\xe9vis, across from Quebec City. He turned down TransCanada\u2019s plan to locate a terminal for the Energy East pipeline in his city and remains ambivalent about the project as whole.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">There are mayors in the pro-pipeline camp who are not from Saskatchewan and Alberta \u2014 the two provinces whose oil industry needs access to a coast \u2014 but their support tends to be more tentative than the opposition of some of their mayoral colleagues.</p><p class="text combinedtext parbase section">Quebec City\u2019s R\xe9gis Labeaume gave his support in principle to the Energy East pipeline last week. In the same breath though, he said TransCanada had proven inept at addressing the environmental concerns arising from the project.</p><p class="text combinedtext parbase section">Mel Norton, the mayor of Saint John, N.B., where some of the oil delivered by the pipeline would be refined, also supports Energy East but his municipal council wants more definitive answers from TransCanada as to its benefits for the region.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">Canada has hit upon defining issues that have pitted regions against region in the past. Think of the National Energy Program in the early \u201980s, the 1988 free trade agreement with the United States \u2014 opposed by Ontario but supported by Quebec and Alberta \u2014 and three divisive constitutional rounds. But none of those discussions engaged Canada\u2019s municipal politicians in the way that this one does.</p><p itemprop="articleBody">No mayor holds a veto over a pipeline but it will be hard to secure a social licence for any project absent more support from the political leaders who are closest to the day-to-day life of so many voters.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/feeds.articles.news.canada.rss', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/news/canada/2016/02/04/support-from-mayors-essential-for-any-pipeline-project-hbert.html', u'author': u'Chantal H\xe9bert - National Affairs', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/news/canada/2016/02/04/support-from-mayors-essential-for-any-pipeline-project-hbert.html', u'scraped_on': 1454581363, u'published_date': u'Thu, 4 Feb 2016 05:00:00 EST', u'title': u'Support from mayors essential for any pipeline project: H\xe9bert', u'_id': ObjectId('56b326751d41c820b569ef33'), u'media_url': u'http://www.thestar.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>IF the struggle by TransCanada Corp. to try to finesse its Energy East pipeline proposal across most of Canada seems difficult, it pales in comparison with a much larger question: Are we serious about climate change? If we are, we cannot extract any more than 10 per cent of the oil underground in Alberta. If we aren\u2019t serious about working to combat increasingly weird and dangerous weather caused by greenhouse gases, we\u2019ll allow TransCanada and other pipeline builders to carry on. Those are some pretty stark questions considering the importance of the energy sector to Canada\u2019s economy.</p><p>TransCanada was content to work the back channels in its otherwise public bid to ship oil from Alberta through an old natural gas pipeline that crosses sensitive areas of Northwestern Ontario, to refineries and for export in Atlantic Canada.</p><p>Ontario and Quebec, where most of the plan resides, are crucial to Trans-Canada\u2019s bid. Ontario Premier Kathleen Wynne has sided with Alberta\u2019s Rachel Notley on the company\u2019s side. Their view: Let\u2019s worry about climate change later; for now, under certain safety and environmental conditions, let\u2019s get this product to those who want to buy it. The fact that, as letter writer Peter Lang reminds us today, the Ontario Energy Board has said conclusively there are more risks than benefits to the project seems to have evaded Wynne.</p><p>Quebec Premier Philippe Couillard has been noncommittal, but 82 mayors, led by Montreal\u2019s Denis Coderre, may have forced his hand. The mayors want no part of a plan that continues to raise questions and concerns the more it is examined.</p><p>So, TransCanada has tossed a hail Mary pass. It announced Wednesday a deal with a Swiss manufacturer to build 22 electrical pumping stations in the Montreal region. This transparent ruse to win over 82 mayors with the promise of 120 mostly temporary jobs is offensive. And why not have a Canadian company build the stations? (TransCanada will be in Thunder Bay next week to make \u201ca major funding announcement\u201d in support of the 211 telephone assistance line.)</p><p>But whether Energy East, and its new president\u2019s I\u2019m-just-a-regular guy approach in announcing the Quebec initiative, succeed, the larger questions still loom. Canada has taken a lead in global climate change efforts in proposing targets lower than those officially adopted. Can we hope to reach any target if we carry on extracting oil and burning it? And how do we get there?</p><p>This is hardly something that can be changed overnight. Our economy, our world, is too dependent on oil to turn off the taps tomorrow. But by committing to new pipelines today, Canada will be ensuring that the will to embrace new options and technologies is delayed. Most climate scientists warn that this is a recipe for more and more widespread weather disaster.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/search/?c%5B%5D=opinion%2Feditorials%2A&d=&d1=&d2=&f=rss&l=25&q=&s=start_time&sd=desc&t=article', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/opinion/editorials/pipelines-and-beyond/article_742b9744-cac3-11e5-a3ac-9f5a17bf5e35.html', u'author': u'None', u'original_article_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/opinion/editorials/pipelines-and-beyond/article_742b9744-cac3-11e5-a3ac-9f5a17bf5e35.html', u'scraped_on': 1454583885, u'published_date': u'Thu, 04 Feb 2016 06:00:00 -0500', u'title': u'Pipelines and beyond', u'_id': ObjectId('56b3304e1d41c8224e2fcc24'), u'media_url': u'http://www.chroniclejournal.com/'}
{u'full_article': u'<p>Mayor Naheed Nenshi.Ted Rhodes / Calgary Herald</p><p>OTTAWA \u2014 Mayor Naheed Nenshi is in the nation\u2019s capital, urging mayors across the country to support pipelines.</p><p>\u201cRather than an inflammatory conversation pitting energy advocates against environmental advocates, we need to work together as communities, as a nation, to get this right,\u201d Nenshi told a coalition of mayors on Thursday morning.</p><p>He spoke at Ottawa City Hall just before two days of talks between the federal government and 21 mayors.</p><p>His remarks come a month after a group of 82 Montreal-area mayors who last month\xa0rejected the Energy East pipeline\xa0proposal, which would convert an existing natural gas pipeline to transport Alberta crude, and add a section from Montreal to Saint John, N.B.</p><p>The Quebec mayors group said the project\u2019s environmental risks outweighed economic benefits for the province.</p><p>Among those who opposed the pipeline was Laval Mayor Marc Demers.</p><p>\u201cBefore taking a position, we did our homework,\u201d he said Thursday before Nenshi\u2019s speech. Demers stressed the city had its engineers analyse risks, and considered\xa0Canada\u2019s pledge at this year\u2019s Paris conference climate to reduce carbon emissions.</p><p>\u201cWe met with the people from TransCanada \u2026 and the answers they gave us didn\u2019t assure us.\u201d</p><p>Demers also bristled at suggestions Quebec should be less critical because it receives the lion\u2019s share of federal transfer payments. In the next financial year, Quebec is to receive $2,571 per person in tax transfers, versus $1,366 for each Albertan.</p><p>\u201cI don\u2019t think that people in Canada lose their right of speech because they get money from the federal government. I mean, Quebec has been contributing quite a lot to the Canadian economy in the past 10, 15, 100 years,\u201d Demers said.</p><p>But Quebec City Mayor R\xe9gis Labeaume said he feared such divisions could jeopardize the beleaguered Quebec plane-maker Bombardier\u2019s request for a federal bailout.</p><p>\u201cI read the papers in the west, and I feel just waiting to see what the federal government will decide about Bombardier. I\u2019m kind of worried.\u201d</p><p>After a 2013 oil-train derailment killed 47 Quebecers, Labeaume said he supports \u201cmuch safer\u201d pipelines, but not the Energy East project because of the lack of consultation.</p><p>\u201cIf we didn\u2019t learn from Lac-M\xe9gantic, I don\u2019t know when we\u2019re going to learn,\u201d he told reporters in French, before blasting TransCanada. \u201cI don\u2019t know if they\u2019re arrogant or incompetent, but they don\u2019t know how to work. It\u2019s kind of stupid.\u201d</p><p>Montreal Mayor Denis Coderre, who\u2019s been among the most outspoken against pipeline projects, was not present for Nenshi\u2019s speech.</p><p>During his speech, Nenshi noted the United States ambassador to Canada was in the room, and said believed the Keystone XL rejection was about competition, not environmental concerns.</p><p>\u201cOur biggest customer, our only customer, is now our biggest competitor. We must find access to other markets,\u201d said Nenshi, who touted the need for safe electricity in developing countries.</p><p>\u201cIt is our opportunity to make change in the world, and we\u2019re allowing petty discussions between ourselves, preventing us from making that kind of difference.\u201d</p><p>The\xa0Big City Mayors\u2019 Caucus\xa0will meet Thursday with the ministers for aboriginal affairs, infrastructure and public safety.</p><p>Nenshi and other mayors are anticipating a funding announcement when federal Infrastructure Minister Amarjeet Sohi makes a public statement Thursday afternoon.</p><p>On a visit to Edmonton, Sohi said Wednesday the government\xa0will open the taps\xa0on hundreds of millions of dollars in capital spending this year in Alberta as it aims to bolster the province\u2019s ailing economy.</p><p>Nenshi said he was happy Prime Minister Justin Trudeau mentioned the National Energy Board process, but wanted \u201ca bit more certainty\u201d he would follow the NEB\u2019s decision.</p><p>Trudeau is in Calgary on\xa0Thursday\xa0to meet with oilpatch leaders.</p><p>\u201cIt\u2019s not for municipalities, it\u2019s not for provinces to impose conditions,\u201d Nenshi said. \u201cThis is a sideshow, it\u2019s a distraction. So I think we should actually focus on letting the regulator do its work.\u201d</p><p>On Friday, the mayors\u2019 caucus meets with Trudeau, as well as the federal finance, immigration and environment ministers.</p><p>More to come.</p>', u'rss_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/feed/', u'language': u'en', u'in_drupal': False, u'redirected_article_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/news/local-news/nenshi-urges-big-city-mayors-to-work-together-to-get-pipelines-built', u'author': u'Tony Seskus, Calgary Herald', u'original_article_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/news/local-news/nenshi-urges-big-city-mayors-to-work-together-to-get-pipelines-built', u'scraped_on': 1454602724, u'published_date': u'Thu, 04 Feb 2016 15:48:25 +0000', u'title': u'Nenshi urges big city mayors to work together to get pipelines built', u'_id': ObjectId('56b379eb1d41c82b6874e6f3'), u'media_url': u'http://calgaryherald.com/'}
